sales jumped 13.8% in March from February, durable-goods orders rose 6.1% and economic activity

Gold Asserts Role As Investor Haven From Global Woes
Commodities Report
Gold Asserts Role As Investor Haven From Global Woes
By Alison Guerriere Ciaccio
323 words
27 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The New York precious-metals complex rallied, with gold asserting its safe-haven status as tensions mount
in the Middle East before tomorrow's U.N. Security Council deadline for Iran to freeze its enrichment of
uranium.
Gold continued to benefit from pockets of safe-haven hedging as well as speculative commodity-fund buying
interest on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, traders said.
Most-active June gold gained $7.80 an ounce to $642. Spot April gold also gained $7.80 to $638.80.
Analysts at MKS Finance in Geneva said a weak U.S. dollar and oil-driven inflation continue to act in favor of
gold as well as a physical demand pickup as the Indian wedding season moves into full swing through June.
"We believe that the scenario of fund profit-taking and [buying on price dips] will continue in these choppy
markets with the retesting of the last week's 25-year high of $645.75 not being out of reach," the MKS
analysts said.
The silver market followed its sister metal as it moved through $13 an ounce, with the most-active July
settling up 25.6 cents at $12.939. Spot May gained 25.5 cents to $12.815.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures fell for the third straight session after weekly U.S. data damped supply concerns before
the summer driving season. U.S. refinery utilization jumped by two percentage points last week to 88.2%, the
highest rate since January. The June contract settled 95 cents lower at $71.93 a barrel on the New York
Mercantile Exchange.
EXCHANGES: Simultaneous electronic and open-outcry grain trading at the Chicago Board of Trade will
begin Aug. 1, the first time the exchange will allow the electronic-trading platform to operate at the same time
as pit-traded contracts.

Stocks Retreat Slightly as the Dollar Continues Pullback on Yen and Euro
Monday's Markets
Stocks Retreat Slightly as the Dollar Continues Pullback on Yen and Euro
By E.S. Browning
481 words
25 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER HITTING a six-year high on Friday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average took a breather yesterday, but
the day's stock declines were minor.
The blue-chip average fell 11.13 points, or 0.1%, to 11336.32, still up 5.8% this year. Other stock indexes
also fell, as did the prices of oil and gold.
To many on Wall Street, it appeared to be simply a normal break in the action, as people cashed in on a
variety of investments that have been strong this year.
"Things are up so high at this point that people are just very indecisive," said Bob Basel, senior trader at
Citigroup's New York brokerage arm.
But Mr. Basel pointed out that, despite the sharp pullback in oil futures, the price of crude remains high, and
the weekend's run-up in gasoline prices made some investors nervous. Oil declined $1.84 to $73.33 a barrel
on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Oil futures had set another exchange high Friday in New York.
Other traders said the dollar's continuing retreat also was worrying some people. The dollar hit a
seven-month low against the euro and a three-month low against the yen. A falling dollar boosts profits for
U.S. multinationals, but it increases the cost of imported goods, which can stoke inflation. Dollar declines
also can make foreigners nervous about investing in U.S. securities.
Gold futures fell $11.60 to $620.60 a troy ounce.
Even companies that reported strong quarterly profits, such as Caterpillar, finished the day with declines.
Caterpillar shares, which have been at record levels for much of this year, pulled back after rising to within a
few cents of Friday's record intraday price of $80 a share. American Express also beat profit forecasts, but its
shares declined as revenue missed expectations.
Investors did get some good news, as bond prices rose, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note down
to 4.987%. A pullback of that benchmark yield below 5% marks a welcome respite, since the 10-year yield
can affect other interest rates.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.24%, or 3.17 points, to 1308.11, up 4.8% this year. The Nasdaq
Composite Index declined 0.4%, or 9.48 points, to 2333.38, up 5.8% in 2006.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated; bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000
invested. The 30-year bond was up 13/32 to yield 5.067%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 114.46 yen, down from 116.65, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2403 from $1.2349.

The Design Economy
The Design Economy
By Andy Kessler
715 words
24 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
You'd think it's time to stock canned goods and sweep out the bomb shelter. Oil is over $70 a barrel, Iran's
got nukes, and soon they'll price gas per ounce. The Fed has jacked short rates up 15 times. Guys, you're
killing us. The yield curve is as flat as a subsidized Iowa corn field. There's $1 trillion of teaser rate Adjustable
Rate Mortgages about to burst all over Southern California and 'burbs everywhere. Gold is over $600,
commodities are roaring, the dollar is dropping again, there are trade deficits as far as the eye can see and
GM is on life support. Drunken sailors in D.C. are running a sea of red ink and every time it's sunny, some
antigrowth global warming nutjob wags his finger at your $100-to-fill SUV. It feels like the '70s all over again,
a blaze of malaise. Except . . .
The stock market is like the little engine that could, and seems to go up everyday. The Dow is just shy of its
all-time high, the S&P 500 is over 1300 and Nasdaq has doubled in three years. Yee-ha, bull markets are fun,
but something doesn't add up. They say the stock market climbs the Wall of Worry, but this is ridiculous -- it's
got the Wile E. Coyote Acme Jetpack on.
So what is it? The stock market knows something and is predicting some future the rest of us can only guess
at. I think it knows how the economy is now structured and is not letting on. It may have figured out that we're
about halfway through a transition from an industrial economy to a design economy. And all bets are off.
Banks don't fund growth anymore, the stock market does. A flat yield curve is less important. Why borrow at
6% when you can sell shares to fund expansion. Those that have to borrow, like GM, are rightly starved.
Those that can grow unabated (Google, Goldman and yes, financial firm GE) use the stock market for funds.
Perhaps here's how the world works these days. No need to borrow billions and build big ethylene plants
anymore. You invent something here (chip, movie, iPod, medicine, financial instrument), email the design
overseas for manufacture in $1-an-hour factories (OK, not financial stuff), and then ship it back for
consumption. Sure, this runs up trade deficits, and our precious dollars leave the country, but that's only half
the story. Those dollars come back and invest in the U.S. Most go into long bonds, 10-years and 30-years.
That's why Alan Greenspan left with a puzzled look on his face. Foreign buying is keeping long rates low; the
yield curve is flat.
But maybe the stock market has figured out that we're running out of long bonds. Maybe, just maybe, the
surprise is fiscal discipline being voted into office in November and shrinking red ink in D.C. Marginal rate tax
cuts and lower rates on dividends and cap gains might actually work and increase revenue. If we run smaller
deficits, then there'll be fewer bonds for foreigners to buy, so they have to buy something else with those
dollars and the next big pot of liquidity is -- hmm, let me think for a second, oh yeah, on Wall and Broad, the
$15 trillion stock market. When bonds are scarce, foreigners are going to have to buy our stocks, or so the
stock market might be screaming.
It may also be saying that gold is dead, our economy is structured to generate a return on investment on
even $70 oil, trade deficits are actually a good thing, innovation will resolve long-term health-care liabilities,
and that lower prices for beach-front homes won't derail the economy. But make up your own mind. The time
to load up was three years ago. The stock market is notoriously schizophrenic. Remember, it thought you
could sell pet food over the Internet. But it's more often right than wrong, especially when proving pessimists
wrong and optimists delusional.
---
Mr. Kessler's next book, "The End of Medicine," will be published by Collins in July.

Interest-Rate Volatility Nears Lows --- Mortage Market Is Steady, Some Hedge Plays Slow Amid Less Uncertainty
Credit Markets
Interest-Rate Volatility Nears Lows --- Mortage Market Is Steady, Some Hedge Plays Slow Amid Less
Uncertainty
By Danielle Reed
890 words
22 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Oil's over $75 a barrel. Gold is over $600 an ounce. The 10-year Treasury yield is above 5%.
But volatility -- a measure of expectations for fluctuations in interest rates -- nonetheless keeps dropping.
Volatility has been heading toward historic lows, helping the mortgage market -- one of the markets most
sensitive to interest-rate swings -- to hold steady even though interest rates and commodities prices
continue to rise.
The sheer size of the $5.5 trillion mortgage-bond market means that sharp moves there often ripple into other
areas of the fixed-income universe including the benchmark U.S. Treasurys market, which in turn set the bar
for financing costs for homeowners and companies.
Because mortgage bonds are embedded with a call option since homeowners often pay off their mortgages
before they mature, interest-rate volatility plays a critical role in how these securities are valued and how
attractive they appear to investors.
Despite commodity prices that were last seen in more inflationary periods in the 1970s and early 1980s, the
options market -- frequently tapped by mortgage-bond investors to hedge interest-rate risk -- doesn't seem to
think there is much to worry about in terms of swings in interest rates.
Volatility as measured by the price of options to enter into interest-rate/swap contracts called swaptions has
steadily fallen all year. Interest-rate swaps are contracts that allow investors to exchange fixed for
floating-rate interest payments, which adapt to changes in the rate environment.
The price for a three-year option to buy a 10-year interest-rate-swap contract is currently not too far off its
12-year low, seen in 1998, said John Sim, a mortgage analyst with J.P. Morgan. At the current price, the cost
of $1 million worth of three-year options on 10-year-swap contracts would be $38,800.
One reason volatility is so low, Mr. Sim said, is that mortgage bonds now are owned by investors who for
various reasons are less active hedgers than owners of mortgage bonds in years past were.
Housing finance agencies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, who were once the dominant buyers of mortgages,
have pulled back in recent years. They have also shifted their holdings somewhat away from fixed-rate
mortgages, and toward floating-rate paper, which doesn't have to be hedged as much for interest-rate risk,
noted Amin Majidi, director of mortgage-backed-securities research for Deutsche Bank in New York.
Hedge funds are also less active buyers of swaptions to hedge their mortgage positions. That is in part a
function of the current relatively flat Treasury yield curve environment, Mr. Majidi said. A flat curve -- that is, a
small gap between the yield on two-year and 10-year Treasury notes -- implies less uncertainty about future
rates, and less volatilty, Mr. Majidi said.
Aside from who owns mortgage bonds, the other force affecting volatility -- excluding shocks from world
events, like the Russian debt crisis in 1998 -- is the Federal Reserve. It has been hiking short-term rates
since mid-2004, and yet volatility has declined as the policy makers have more clearly broadcast their
intentions to the market, said Kevin Jackson, senior mortgage strategist with RBC Capital Markets in
Chicago.
When the market isn't surprised, rates tend not to move suddenly. The same goes for economic data. Even
though oil and other commodity prices are high, Mr. Jackson said, inflation seems to be fairly tame and
economic reports seem to be coming in not too far off expectations in general.

--- Page 15 ---
Page 15 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With volatility low -- and heading lower -- mortgage bonds should continue to do well, said Mr. Sim of J.P.
Morgan. The reason is that when the cost of hedging mortgage bonds goes lower, the bonds are more
valuable to investors.
Treasurys
Longer dated Treasury prices held firm Friday, as investors placed bets on the shape of the yield curve in the
absence of any data releases.
The yield curve measures the difference between the yields on the two- and 10-year Treasury notes. That
gap has in recent sessions been widening as investors sold longer-term bonds due to inflation worries.
Friday, investors began to bet on the gap shrinking -- or the curve flattening, in bond-market parlance -- again
in the short term. The shorter maturities face pressure ahead of the sale of new two- and five-year notes next
week by the government.
"Curve flatteners have been going on," said Rick Klingman, head of government trading at ABN Amro in New
York. "There has been some good buying of the 15-year sector against selling the front end."
At 4 p.m., the benchmark 10-year note was up 9/32 point, or $2.8125 per $1,000 face value, at 96 3/32. Its
yield fell to 5.010% from 5.045% Thursday, as yields move inversely to prices. The 30-year bond was up
21/32 point at 90 30/32 to yield 5.095%, while the two-year note fell 1/32 to 99 16/32 to yield 4.902%.
The yield curve flattened to 0.11 percentage point from Thursday's 0.15 percentage point.

Insufficient Fund: Booming Economy Leaves the IMF Groping for Mission --- With Fewer Global Deadbeats, The Agency Loses Clout, And a Source...
Insufficient Fund: Booming Economy Leaves the IMF Groping for Mission --- With Fewer Global
Deadbeats, The Agency Loses Clout, And a Source of Income --- Skeptical Reception in Bolivia
By Matt Moffett and Bob Davis
2,204 words
21 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- When world finance ministers gather here tomorrow for the International Monetary Fund's
spring meeting, the agency that has confidently dispensed advice to countless governments in distress will be
searching hard for answers to its own identity crisis.
For much of its 61 years, the 184-member-nations IMF has acted as the fire brigade for the global financial
system. When economic crises flared up in countries ranging from Mexico to Turkey, the IMF would try to
snuff them with emergency loans. In return for cash, economically troubled countries were compelled to
undertake harsh new policies -- often cuts in government spending or interest-rate increases -- to try to
fireproof their economies.
Now the IMF faces a novel predicament: A robust global economy, growing at a 4% clip since 2003, has left
the IMF with a dearth of financial firestorms to manage, and fewer countries willing to borrow from it and heed
its dire lending conditions. Flush with cash and eager to regain control over their economic policies, 10
countries, from Russia to Brazil to Argentina, have repaid loans to the IMF ahead of schedule in recent years.
The IMF's current loan portfolio of $35 billion is its smallest since the 1980s.
The effect is twofold: A shrinking loan portfolio greatly diminishes the IMF's influence over global economic
policy. IMF loan disbursements are conditioned on the enactment, within defined time frames, of measures
including privatization of state-owned companies, budget cuts, interest-rate increases and stiffer financial
regulation. Once IMF loans are ended, the momentum for economic reform in one-time borrowers may fizzle.
That's a worry in Latin America, especially where populist politicians are winning power across the continent.
Fewer loans also means less interest income, and thus fewer dollars in the IMF coffers. In an irony that has
provoked tittering among many emerging-market finance ministers, the agency that has long preached
belt-tightening now must practice it itself. Over the next three years, the IMF figures it may sustain operating
losses of nearly $600 million, and have to dip into its nearly $9 billion in cash reserves to cover the shortfall.
To reduce the red ink, the Fund has already capped personnel levels at 2,800 and is planning budgets that
would lag behind the rate of inflation. It may start charging nations for technical advice that the IMF now
provides free. If that doesn't work, it may have to tap its vast gold hoard of 103 million ounces, valued at
$63.5 billion at today's prices and held in the vaults of IMF member nations.
To be sure, the global good times have been powered by low interest-rates, U.S. consumer spending and
Asian demand for commodities -- a combination that could come to a halt, and plunge developing countries
into crises and knocking on the IMF's door again.
But for now, thanks to the vast sums of capital sloshing around the world's economy, developing nations are
enjoying more freedom than ever before to chart more individualistic courses. From 2001 to 2005, the key
emerging economies in Asia, led by China, have seen their reserves grow about 2.5 times to $1.7 trillion. In
Latin America, reserves of the largest economies nearly doubled over the same time period to $223 billion,
according to ABN-Amro.
The IMF is trying gamely to change the way it does business, from lender to "confidential adviser," as Rodrigo
de Rato, the IMF's managing director, puts it. That entails a mixture of sound economic advice, outreach to
one-time opponents -- and a splash of public relations. Arm-twisting is out; persuasion is in. When Mr. de
Rato visited Tegucigalpa, Honduras, in February, he not only went to see the president and finance minister,
but also trekked to the rough-and-tumble Campo Cielo barrio. He toured a project where social workers were
removing gang-insignia tattoos from youths and the IMF even sent some money later.
"We were amazed that the big banker would visit the barrio," says Pedro Chico, a Roman Catholic educator
who works with disadvantaged Tegucigalpa youths. A little bit of apple polishing certainly didn't hurt Mr. de

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Rato while he was trying to nudge the newly elected government of Manuel Zelaya to control government
salaries to meet deficit targets.
At tomorrow's meeting, finance ministers from around the world will start mapping out directions for the IMF's
future at the Fund's plush headquarters, with its polished granite floors and 14-story atrium complete with
waterfall.
The Fund is being pulled in several different ways by key members. The U.S. wants the IMF to police
foreign-exchange rates with an eye to prodding China into strengthening its currency to ease the gaping U.S.
trade deficit. The central bankers of the United Kingdom and Canada are pressing the Fund to stiffen its
monitoring of the economic policies and fiscal health of developing and industrialized nations.
Meanwhile, the IMF's chief economist, Raghuram Rajan, is pushing to have the Fund offer a kind of
"insurance" to developing countries. So long as their policies meet IMF standards they would be assured
easier access to IMF funds in emergencies. In a frank speech last month, Mr. Rajan complained of the
"ebbing spirit of internationalism," and said no reform plan will work unless the major economies recapture
the shared sense of purpose that inspired the Fund's creation. "Unfortunately, paying attention to the global
community is seen as weakness today rather than responsible global citizenship."
Mr. de Rato is seeking a middle ground and is looking for ways to monitor Chinese foreign-exchange rates,
American fiscal and trade deficits and European economic lethargy. He hopes that regional sessions on
these so-called imbalances -- an IMF form of group therapy -- would push countries to make adjustments.
The IMF was conceived in July 1944 by founders determined not to repeat the mistakes that led to the Great
Depression. Besides stepping in as emergency lender to wealthier countries facing balance-of-payment
problems, the Fund also set up a system of "surveillance," or monitoring, to advise countries on their
economic policies.
Many important decisions by the Fund require 85% approval by shareholder nations, with votes apportioned
to each country based on its general position in the world economy. The largest single shareholder, the U.S.,
accounts for about 17% of the total vote, giving it effective veto power on issues such as expanding the
Fund's financial base or changing voting shares.
Over the decades, the Fund's focus shifted to developing nations. When oil prices fell and Mexico ran out of
money to pay its lenders in 1982, the IMF had to step in and lead a bailout of Mexico and several emerging
countries to avert a global-banking meltdown. The IMF led rescue packages to Asia in the late 1990s and
supported the former Soviet Union and its Eastern Europe client states in making the transition from
communism.
But the IMF's harsh austerity policies, and perception that it was insensitive to their impact on working people,
have caused deeply ingrained ill-will. In South Korea, which went through a financial crisis in 1997-1998,
critics bitterly remarked that the Fund's initials stood for "I'M Fired."
Indonesia's 1997 IMF program was perhaps the most intrusive and listed 140 conditions the country needed
to meet, including boosting reforestation and disbanding its clove monopoly, to dismantle what economists
called "crony capitalism." An IMF requirement that the Indonesia government cut fuel subsidies, which the
government slashed faster than the IMF called for, led to the rioting that prompted the resignation of
President Suharto in 1998.
The memory of that harsh medicine is hindering the IMF today as it tries to nudge Indonesia to clean up its
state-owned banks, which account for about 40% of all lending, and privatize them. The idea is facing
resistance both inside and outside the government. Indonesia's Minister for State Enterprises Sugiharto, who
oversees privatization plans, is an economic nationalist who has declared publicly that Jakarta can manage
its own affairs and need not heed IMF advice.
The IMF has been careful not to push too hard. "One of the lessons learned from the Asian crisis is that if a
government is not itself committed to policies, the likelihood of its following through with implementing them is
very limited," says Stephen Schwartz, the Fund's senior representative in Jakarta. The Fund also can't afford
to lose many more clients: Indonesia is the IMF's No. 2 borrower after Turkey and accounts for about 22% of
the IMF's loans outstanding -- and some Indonesians have talked about paying off the IMF early to get it off
the country's back.
Many developing countries are inspired to distance themselves from the IMF by the example of Argentina,
which announced the payback of its $9.8 billion debt to the Fund in December, closing the books on a long
tortured chapter in its history. Beginning in the early 1990s, the IMF had provided financing supporting
Argentina's risky strategy to keep its peso pegged one-to-one to the dollar. The IMF overlooked warning
signals that the government deficit and debt levels were expanding too much to support the currency, a 2004
IMF audit concluded.

--- Page 18 ---
Page 18 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
After the economy collapsed in 2001, Argentina flouted many of the IMF's recommendations to get the
country on its feet -- and has grown rapidly nonetheless. When Argentina finally attained the wherewithal to
pay off the Fund last December, jubilant Argentine demonstrators released balloons saying "Ciao IMF."
Contempt for the Fund runs so high in Argentina that it inspired a popular Monopoly-like board game, "Eternal
Debt," in which the object is "to defeat the IMF."
Yet, Argentina's outlook is increasingly precarious. Inflation has been surging and Argentine President
Nestor Kirchner has been trying to suppress it with increasingly erratic measures. He recently imposed a ban
on beef exports to try to spur domestic supply and lower the price. Argentina has kept natural gas and
electricity prices artificially low; the result is that there's been a dearth of investment, raising fears of
potentially disastrous energy shortages.
Without a lending program, there is little the IMF can do to convince Argentina to change course. Argentina
still receives a couple of visits a year from IMF delegations and is subject to critiques in an annual review. But
Claudio Loser, a former IMF chief for Latin America, thinks the Fund's advice will fall on deaf ears. "With this
government, hearing the word IMF is like hearing a curse," he says.
Bolivia is another example of the IMF's fading influence in the region. One of Latin America's poorest nations,
Bolivia has borrowed repeatedly from the Fund, which has pressed the government to fight inflation through
budget cuts and to encourage foreign investment through modest taxation.
In mid-February, Anoop Singh, the Latin America chief, and his top lieutenants flew to Bolivia to meet with the
new regime of President Evo Morales, a leftist indigenous leader who has been critical of the IMF and has
threatened to re-nationalize the energy industry. The IMF team held a town meeting with labor and
indigenous leaders decked out in traditional Bolivian bowler hats. Mr. Singh, a 55-year-old Indian native,
joked with the crowd that he was sorry he didn't know how speak Spanish despite "being `Indian' himself." He
parried concerns about IMF influence by informing the crowd that the Fund had written off nearly all the
country's loans.
But not all of the Bolivians who attended were convinced of the IMF's good intentions. Donato Duran, a union
leader, says he still believes that "the IMF only cares about rich nations and rich Bolivians." Bolivia's IMF
program expired at the end of March, and Mr. Morales's critics worry that the IMF will now have little influence
over the government.
IMF officials say their methods have enjoyed better success in Brazil. In August of 2002, a couple of months
before presidential elections, Brazil faced an imminent financial meltdown sparked by investor fears of the
economic agenda of the front-running leftist candidate, Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva. The IMF stepped in with a
$30 billion aid package, structured to offer Mr. da Silva, an ex-union leader who had long criticized the IMF, a
carrot to pursue sound policies. Brazil got part of the money right away, but the bulk would be disbursed only
if the incoming government continued policies to control spending.
Since taking office, the da Silva government has won over investors with a disciplined budget policy. Brazil
recently paid off its $15.5 billion debt to the IMF without strain. After announcing the payback, Mr. da Silva
employed colloquial Portuguese, bragging that Brazil once again was "owner of its nose."
---
James Hookway in Bangkok, Thailand, and Raphael Pura in Kuala Lumpur, Malaysia, contributed to this
article.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Ann Davis
408 words
21 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Today's Market Forecast]
Pause to Refresh?
The commodities markets lived up to their volatile reputation yesterday, with precious-metals prices moving
sharply lower. But most analysts see the downdraft as merely momentary before prices begin moving higher
again.
Yesterday's wee hours showed signs of typical revelry. In overnight trading, gold and silver futures jumped.
Both metals looked poised to add to Wednesday's multi-decade highs.
But then the festive mood turned quickly grim. Silver started to plunge in overseas markets, and gold quickly
followed. Energy and other metals also sold off. By day's end, the current-month gold contract had dropped
$12.80 an ounce, or 2%, to $619.80, and silver had its biggest one-day percentage loss in 19 years: down
$1.9890, or 14%, to $12.51 an ounce. The slump across commodities spurred talk of a correction in the
red-hot sector.
But there are plenty of reasons to think that the bull market in metals, fossil fuels and other raw goods isn't
over yet. Far from a run for the exits, "this was just profit-taking," says Michael Cuggino, president and
portfolio manager for the Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds. "The prices have come so far, so fast, that
there's a little bit of sobering up going on."
World economic growth could make the market keep rising. Demand for fuel and other raw materials is
growing in every corner of the world. "Name a country that is in recession. There aren't any," Bridgewater
Associates, a money-management firm in Westport, Conn., pointed out Tuesday in a report to clients. That
means increasing consumption, on a global scale, of raw materials. "A global slowdown is unlikely until
interest rates rise and equities fall," Bridgewater says.
One reason for commodities' retreat yesterday may be temporary. The dollar strengthened, making
dollar-denominated commodities less attractive to foreign investors. Many traders believe the longer-term
trend for the dollar is still downward.
Another reason commodities may lurch higher again: Adjusted for inflation, some commodities' peaks look
less lofty. Oil's close at $71.95 yesterday is below the April 1980 high, which would be $97.55 in current
dollars. And for gold and silver to match their highs in 1980, they would have to hit $2,170 and nearly $125,
respectively, in March 2006 dollars.

Stocks Edge Higher, Building on Rally Despite Rising Prices in Oil and Gold
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Edge Higher, Building on Rally Despite Rising Prices in Oil and Gold
By Shefali Anand
470 words
20 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCK PRICES inched forward, in a modest echo of Tuesday's big gains.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, in its fourth gain in the past five sessions, rose exactly 10 points, or
0.09%, to 11278.77. The Dow surged 194.99 points Tuesday.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 2.28 points, or 0.17% to 1309.93. The Nasdaq Composite
Index climbed 0.63%, or 14.74 points, to 2370.88. Indexes of small stocks closed at another record.
Markets rallied Tuesday on indications from the Federal Reserve that it might be close to stopping its
interest-rate increases. Rising rates can pinch corporate earnings, among other things, which typically hurts
stocks.
Stock investors yesterday shrugged off gains in oil and gold prices, which traditionally move inversely to
stocks. Crude-oil futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose for a third consecutive day, touching
$72.17 a barrel, up 1.2% or 82 cents. Gold for April delivery rose for the third straight day, by $12.60, or 2%,
to $632.60 an ounce, and is up 22% for the year to date.
Ted Oberhaus, director of stock trading at Lord Abbett, said yesterday's strength in stocks came as a "major
surprise" for investors who might have been expecting stocks to retreat after the previous day's gains. He
added that it shows stock prices "will react to other stimulus than just commodity prices."
Another factor giving strength to the market was strong earning numbers from several companies, said Tim
Heekin, director of trading at San Francisco brokerage firm Thomas Weisel Partners. Shares of Dow
component United Technologies jumped 6.62% to $62.80 after it reported an 18% increase in first-quarter
income. Yahoo's stock rose 7% to $33.54, based on its earnings announcement after Tuesday's market close
in which it declared a 34% increase in revenue, even as its profit for the first quarter declined 22%.
Bond markets were down, following news of a higher-than-expected "core" consumer price index number,
which excludes food and energy prices, for March. The CPI, which provides a measurement of inflation,
gained 0.3%, which was higher than the expected 0.2%.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose; bonds fell. The price on the 10-year Treasury note broke a two-day winning streak by declining
12/32 points, or $3.75 per $1,000 invested, to push up the yield to 5.03%. The 30-year bond fell 26/32 to yield
5.132%.
The dollar weakened. It traded at 117.22 yen, down from 117.33 yen, while the euro rose slightly against the
dollar to $1.2390 from $1.2319.

U.K. Stocks Touch 5-Year High --- Commodity-Price Lift, Sign U.S. Rate Rises May End Add Fuel
World Stock Markets
U.K. Stocks Touch 5-Year High --- Commodity-Price Lift, Sign U.S. Rate Rises May End Add Fuel
By Alistair MacDonald
997 words
20 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
London -- THE UNITED KINGDOM'S benchmark share index touched five-year highs, helped by a rise in
commodity prices and a suggestion that U.S. interest-rate increases are nearing an end, which fueled gains
across the world's stock markets.
With Britain's economy expected to continue growing this year, and mergers-and-acquisitions activity still
strong after a months-long boom, strategists say London's market could have more room to run in the longer
term.
But some contend that London's stock market may first see a pullback as investors realize companies will be
hard-pressed to maintain profit-margin increases they have posted for the past year and thus that earnings
expectations may be exaggerated.
Teun Draaisma, head of European equity strategy at Morgan Stanley, says the outlook for U.K. stocks, as
with other European stock markets, is positive over the next two years. But he cautions that in the next three
to six months he expects a pullback in the U.K. and across Europe as earnings estimates are lowered.
Still, the FTSE 100 at one point yesterday hit 6100, a level not seen since February 2001, at the tail end of
the technology-stock boom that later led to several years of poor performance in the U.K. and other European
stock markets. The index closed at 6089.80, up 45.70 points, or 0.8%.
The gains followed Tuesday's rally for U.S. stocks, the best gains of the year there, after minutes from the
latest Federal Reserve meeting suggested the Fed's two-year run of steady rate increases may be ending.
Many investors had worried that further increases in U.S. interest rates would risk hurting global
consumption and investment.
So the suggestion that rates didn't have further, if any more, to rise, also lifted other European stock markets.
Many Asian markets also rose; South Korea's main index set a new peak during intraday trading, and Hong
Kong and Taiwan reached five-year highs as well. Japan's 225-share Nikkei Stock Average rose 117.26
points, or 0.7%, to 17350.12.
The prospect of a standstill in U.S. interest-rate increases hurt the dollar, which at one point fell to $1.2385
against the euro, lowest since September. In late New York trading, the euro stood at $1.2390.
In the U.K., the stock market has rebounded in the past three years, as a range of conditions has favored
investors. "U.K. corporate profitability is very high; the cost of their financing isn't; share-price valuations are
still low; and the interest-rate environment, where the Bank of England has recently cut rates, is supportive,"
said Robert Parkes, a U.K. strategist at HSBC.
Continued strength in the oil price, which reached multidecade highs above $71 a barrel (later in New York
above $72), and fresh records in the price of some metals also buoyed the U.K. market. Around a fourth of
the market's capitalization is in mining and oil companies. Miner BHP Billiton, for instance, rose 0.8% to GBP
11.90 ($21.18), while oil major BP rose 1% to 704 pence.
Another factor in the FTSE's rise has been the increase of merger-and-acquisition activity in the U.K., where
around $98 billion has been spent buying up British companies so far this year, up 40% from this point last
year, according to data provider Dealogic.
"M&A activity has been about U.K. companies' being taken over, so you get the premium companies to pay to
bid for others coming through," Mr. Parkes said. "And the deals are cash deals, meaning that money is going
back into investors' pockets and into the market again."

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some strategists fear forecasts for European per-share earnings growth of around 10% for 2006 might prove
too optimistic. Some, though, believe U.K. stocks just no longer look so cheap.
---
Other Market Activity
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.92 points, or 0.76%, to 256.13. Excluding the U.S., the
index rose 2.51 points, or 1.14%, to 222.47.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei's rally was led by technology, financial and consumer-sector stocks. Computer maker
Fujitsu rose 1.25% after Tuesday's announcement it expects its 2005 operating profit to rise 13%. Sony rose
2.1% on reports the consumer-electronics company plans to invest more in thin-screen liquid-crystal display
television sets through its joint venture with Samsung Electronics.
In SYDNEY, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 rose 35.30 points, or 0.7%, to a record 5283.90. Zinc and lead
producer Zinifex rose 11%, and copper, zinc and gold miner Oxiana rose 7.7%.
In PARIS, the CAC-40 rose 62.17 points, or 1.2%, to 5158.14, reflecting general euphoria on other European
exchanges. Lafarge led gainers, up 3.8% amid generally firm construction stocks. LVMH rose 3% before
first-quarter sales figures. EADS and Thales were the only two fallers in the index.

Betting on Bernanke
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Betting on Bernanke
948 words
19 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It takes a difference of opinion to make a market, as the last two days have been proving on inflation. On
Monday, the inflation hawks had their day, as markets sold off while gold jumped by $15 an ounce and oil
pushed past $70 a barrel. Then yesterday the doves were in control, as moderate March inflation numbers
renewed hopes that the Federal Reserve is near the end of its rate-raising cycle. Stocks soared.
We'd like to believe the doves are right, but then they've also been telling us the Fed was close to the end a
year ago. These economists -- on Wall Street and in the Fed itself -- have consistently underestimated both
the strength of the post-2003 economy and the rise in gold and other commodity prices. Thus their "target"
for the peak fed funds rate (now at 4.75%) has climbed from 4% to 4.5%, then to 5% and now some are
saying 5.5% or higher.
As recently as last year, the doves were also saying there was no inflation because long-term interest rates
were staying remarkably low. Some of us thought this was better explained by global capital flows and by the
carry trade -- borrowing short more cheaply to buy at the long end -- and therefore wouldn't last. And, sure
enough, long rates have marched up in the last three months, with the 10-year Treasury note rising 70 basis
points since mid-January. This rate rise has been global, by the way, which suggests that investors
everywhere are catching up to the fact that central banks have been flooding the world with liquidity.
Meanwhile, gold has been hitting new highs, including a 25-year record yesterday to about $620 an ounce.
Commodities across the board have been on a two-year price run not seen since the late 1970s. Some of our
supply-side friends explain all this as the result of new demand from China, or for Indian jewelry in the case of
gold, or some similar supply-demand shift. And no doubt some of that is true.
But in our experience, no similar surge in global commodity prices has ever occurred without an
accompanying rise in general inflation. If this time turns out to be the exception because global competition
is making price increases more difficult, no one will be happier than us. On the other hand, prices in services
and other non-tradable goods have been rising. And on the periphery of the dollar zone, notably in China, the
economy and prices are both roaring ahead.
For a while, it looked as if the Fed finally understood that it has stayed too accommodative for too long. Gold
and commodity prices both stabilized, and the Fed maintained its upward march on rates without signaling an
end. But in the last few days assorted Fed Governors have begun to publicly assert that it may soon pause in
raising rates. Janet Yellen was the latest to do so in a speech yesterday, following Donald Kohn and Susan
Bies last week.
We don't think they're doing new Fed chief Ben Bernanke any favors. Any new chairman has to establish his
credibility as someone who'll defend the value of the currency. And the toughest test is to raise rates when
everyone is enjoying the economic boom that they never want to end.
There is always a danger that the Fed will overshoot by tightening too far, as in retrospect it clearly did in
2000. The just-released transcripts of the Fed's meetings for 2000 show Fed economist Michael Prell
discussing with the Fed Governors in May that year that the fed funds rate might have to rise to 8% before
the Fed was finished. Instead it stopped at 6.5% (at that same May meeting) and within a few months the
Fed was cutting rates again as the economy headed toward recession. The difference between 2000 and
today is that price signals then were clearly contained, with gold and commodity prices actually having
declined for an extended period.
That episode shows that the Fed staff tends to look through the rearview mirror by targeting such Keynesian
indicators as the "output gap" -- the difference between the economy's current and maximum capacity -- or
the jobless rate. Since monetary policy operates on a lag, this means the Fed tends to err at the top and
bottom of its interest-rate cycles. In 2000, it tightened too much for too long, and in 2004 and 2005 it stayed
too easy for too long.

--- Page 27 ---
Page 27 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
All of this matters because, like Alan Greenspan before him, Mr. Bernanke seems to be running monetary
policy out of his hip pocket. No one knows what price measures, or money-supply rules, he actually uses. He
has said he favors "inflation-targeting," which means keeping inflation within a range of 0% to 2%. But the
consumer-price and producer-price indices that these targets are based on are also backward looking. This,
too, may explain the market's wide swings of inflation sentiment. Everyone's making his own bet on Mr.
Bernanke.
Our own surmise is that Mr. Bernanke may be muttering to himself that Mr. Greenspan skipped town in the
nick of time. The former Fed Chairman left him a roaring economy but also rising press pressures that few
have wanted to admit were there. Now Mr. Bernanke is the guy who has to quell inflationary expectations
without messing up the good times. We sure hope Mr. Bernanke didn't take the job so he could give away the
hard-earned anti-inflation gains of the last 25 years.

Business and Finance
What's News
Business and Finance
472 words
19 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J

Hydrogenics, Quantum Advance; AirTran, Frontier Airlines Slide
Small-Stock Focus
Hydrogenics, Quantum Advance; AirTran, Frontier Airlines Slide
By Anjali Cordeiro
449 words
18 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Small stocks ended mixed as a spike in oil prices fed a rally in the alternative-energy sector while weighing
heavily on airline companies.
Small alternative-energy companies, which offer technology in areas such as hydrogen and solar power, rose
after crude futures crossed $70 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Hydrogenics gained 25
cents, or 7.2%, to $3.73; Quantum Fuel Systems Technologies Worldwide added 13 cents, or 3.5%, to 3.87;
and
Evergreen Solar rose 36 cents, or 2.6%, to 14.40.
Crude's ascent dragged down the airline sector, the day's worst performer. AirTran Holdings fell 1, or 6.1%, to
15.40 on the New York Stock Exchange, and Frontier Airlines Holdings declined 36 cents, or 5.1%, to 6.73.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index lost 1.64 to 749.47. The S&P SmallCap 600 Index rose 0.20 to 387.76.
"The fact that oil rose above $70 a barrel and is accompanied by a sharp rise in the price of gold is gripping
the market with inflation fears," said Peter Cardillo, chief market analyst at S.W. Bach, "Unfortunately, good
earnings aren't having any real positive effect."
Precious-metal stocks climbed as gold futures rallied in the face of a weaker dollar and persisting worries
about Iran's nuclear program. Apollo Gold added 4 cents, or 5.2%, to 81 cents, and Canyon Resources
climbed 2 cents, or 2.4%, to 85 cents, both on the American Stock Exchange.
Wheeling-Pittsburgh jumped 2.32, or 12%, to 21.28 after reports in the Wheeling News-Register and
Pittsburgh Post-Gazette that the Wheeling, W.Va., steelmaker is considering two buyout offers. A spokesman
declined to comment on whether the company was in discussions about a transaction.
TiVo gained 60 cents, or 7.5%, to 8.65. The Alviso, Calif., digital video-recording company won an important
patent case against EchoStar Communications. Large cap EchoStar rose 7 cents to 30.04.
Crown Media Holdings fell 1.02, or 18%, to 4.78. The operator of the Hallmark Channel dropped plans to sell
itself.
Newpark Resources (NYSE) plunged 1.28, or 17%, to 6.14. The Metairie, La., oilfield-services company
disclosed an internal investigation into possible irregularities related to the processing of invoices by one of its
subsidiaries. Newpark's financial chief was put on administrative leave.
Bookham tumbled 2.36, or 27%, to 6.38. The optical component maker said third-quarter financial results
were hurt by unexpected costs related to factors including a change in product mix and lower semiconductor
production volumes.

Health-Care Gold Mines: Middlemen Strike It Rich --- Rewarding Career: As Patients, Doctors Feel Pinch, Insurer's CEO Makes a Billion...
Health-Care Gold Mines: Middlemen Strike It Rich --- Rewarding Career: As Patients, Doctors Feel
Pinch, Insurer's CEO Makes a Billion --- UnitedHealth Directors Strive To Please `Brilliant' Chief; New
Questions on Options --- Selling Trout for 40 Cents a Pound
By George Anders
2,715 words
18 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[First in a Series]
MINNETONKA, Minn. -- When William McGuire switched careers in 1986, he was so restless that a pay cut
of more than 30% didn't faze him. Health maintenance organizations were booming, and Dr. McGuire wanted
to help run one. So he jettisoned a six-figure income as a pulmonologist in favor of an HMO management job
that paid about $70,000 a year.
Savvy move. Today, the 58-year-old Dr. McGuire is chief executive officer of UnitedHealth Group Inc., one of
the nation's largest health-care companies. He draws $8 million a year in salary plus bonus, enjoying perks
such as personal use of the company jet. He also has amassed one of the largest stock-options fortunes of
all time.
Unrealized gains on Dr. McGuire's options totaled $1.6 billion, according to UnitedHealth's proxy statement
released this month. Even celebrated CEOs such as General Electric Co.'s Jack Welch or International
Business Machines Corp.'s Louis Gerstner never were granted so much during their time at the top.
Dr. McGuire's story shows how an elite group of companies is getting rich from the nation's fraying
health-care system. Many of them aren't discovering drugs or treating patients. They're middlemen who
process the paperwork, fill the pill bottles and otherwise connect the pieces of a $2 trillion industry.
The middlemen credit themselves with keeping the health system humming and restraining costs. They're
bringing in robust profits -- and their executives are among the country's most richly paid -- as doctors,
patients, hospitals and even drug makers are feeling a financial squeeze. Some 46 million Americans lack
health insurance.
UnitedHealth's main business is offering health plans to employers and Medicare beneficiaries. Bigger
employers usually pay employees' medical bills out of their own coffers and hire UnitedHealth to administer
the health benefit. Smaller employers pay an annual insurance premium to UnitedHealth in exchange for
having the insurer take on the risk of covering employees' health care.
The "risk" business has been a particular gold mine for UnitedHealth and its rivals in recent years. As
health-care inflation eased, insurers still raised premiums at double-digit rates. UnitedHealth's stock price
tripled between January 2003 and January 2006, helped by acquisitions, although it has fallen back
somewhat since the beginning of this year. UnitedHealth's net income in 2005 totaled $3.3 billion, nearly four
times the figure in 2001.
UnitedHealth directors in the late 1990s allowed Dr. McGuire the rare freedom to time his stock-option grants.
In several cases the grants carried dates when the company's share price was particularly low, allowing him
to profit when it recovered. The company's options-granting practices were among several scrutinized in a
page-one article in The Wall Street Journal last month and are being examined by the Securities and
Exchange Commission.
The Journal's analysis of 12 options grants to Dr. McGuire from 1994 to mid-2002 found that if the options
had been randomly dated, the odds of their occurring at such propitious times were about 1 in 200 million. It
raised the possibility that the options grants were backdated. Backdating an options grant isn't necessarily
illegal, but civil or criminal actions could be brought if disclosure of the practice were inadequate, securities
lawyers say. A UnitedHealth spokesman said the grants were appropriate, but the company's board is
reviewing options-granting procedures.
The arrival of the $1 billion CEO would be a head-turner in any industry. But it's especially controversial in
health care, where "people tend to view each dollar of executive pay as money that isn't spent on them," says

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Jonathan Weiner, a health-policy expert at Johns Hopkins University. Dr. McGuire and his supporters say the
U.S. would be in even worse shape if it weren't for insurers such as UnitedHealth weeding out unnecessary
treatments, bargaining with doctors and encouraging patients to seek out the highest-quality care.
Ever since missing a stock-market windfall in the late 1980s, Dr. McGuire has pursued stock-options wealth
tirelessly, as an iron-willed leader surrounded by an admiring board. He declined to discuss his pay, but
current and former directors talked at length about their desire to do whatever is necessary to keep Dr.
McGuire happy.
"We're so lucky to have Bill," says Mary Mundinger, a UnitedHealth director who sits on the company's
compensation committee. "He's brilliant." She says his income gives him extra credibility in health-policy
debates because it shows his success. "He needs to be compensated appropriately so that his business
model has believability in the market," says Ms. Mundinger, who is dean of the nursing school at Columbia
University.
Robert Ryan, another UnitedHealth director, notes that the company's stock-market capitalization has
climbed 112-fold since Dr. McGuire took over in 1991. "A lot of the board's job here is to keep him motivated,"
says Mr. Ryan, a retired chief financial officer of Medtronic Inc.
Bill McGuire was born in Troy, N.Y., but moved to Texas as a boy when his father got a job as an
oil-company engineer. He grew up in League City, Texas, a blue-collar town near Galveston. Friends
remember him as shy, pleasant and dead-certain of his expertise in areas ranging from sports to math.
He grew to be 6-foot-6, and as a high-school senior he was the starting center on a basketball team that
nearly won the state championship. "Bill did all my scouting reports for me," recalls Bill Krueger, who was in
his first year as coach. "I'd never played our opponents before. Bill had. He remembered all the other players'
strengths and weaknesses."
After college, Bill McGuire attended medical school in Galveston, Texas, at the urging of family doctor Ned
Dudney. Varsity athletes who go into medicine often end up as surgeons. But Dr. Dudney pegged his young
friend as more of an intellectual problem-solver and pointed him toward internal medicine. "Bill liked the
challenge of diagnosing the rare, complicated disease," Dr. Dudney recalls.
At medical school, Dr. McGuire became famous for his side income as a commercial fisherman, selling
speckled trout to Galveston's best restaurants at 40 cents a pound. The night before one exam, he slipped
away to the Gulf of Mexico and spent hours catching fish.
"It flabbergasted us," recalls classmate Robert Hendler. "The rest of us were struggling to learn the material.
Bill had it down cold. He could go fishing and still get one of the best scores on the test."
In the early 1980s, Dr. McGuire settled into private practice as a lung-disease specialist in Colorado Springs,
Colo. Much of his job involved hospital care of the desperately sick at all hours of the day and night. In one
case, Dr. McGuire -- who wasn't certified as a cardiac surgeon -- reopened the chest incision of a critically ill
patient in the emergency room and massaged his heart. That audacious step kept the patient alive until a
surgeon arrived.
Looking for a new challenge, Dr. McGuire joined Peak Health Plan Inc. in 1984 as its assistant medical
director. He helped found a smaller health plan, CostGuard Inc., in which he took a 10% ownership stake.
"Bill gave us a lot of credibility," recalls Stephen Hyde, Peak's CEO at the time. "I was trying to expand our
provider network, and I couldn't get doctors to come to our recruiting meetings. Bill could."
Within two years, both Peak and CostGuard were sold to UnitedHealth, a larger Minnesota company that had
been founded in 1974, for a total of about $95 million. Dr. McGuire was irked that the company he had
recently joined was suddenly sold, associates say. He earned about $1 million on his CostGuard stake but he
didn't own any Peak stock, so he missed out on bigger windfalls that Peak's three founders collected. "Bill
didn't let me forget about it for years," Mr. Hyde says.
The managed-care industry in the late 1980s was experiencing booming but chaotic growth. Big corporations,
instead of simply paying whatever medical bills their employees submitted, tried steering them into more
restrictive HMOs to hold down runaway costs. Members streamed in so fast that many health plans' computer
systems buckled.
Dr. McGuire moved to UnitedHealth's headquarters in Minnesota, where he quickly became the company's
No. 2 executive. In 1988 and 1989, he performed the business world's version of emergency surgery on a
company that was stretched too thin. After he helped sell or close a half-dozen regional plans that weren't
working out, UnitedHealth became a smaller but more profitable company.

--- Page 33 ---
Page 33 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"When we saw Bill's talent and potential, we started providing him with some big stock options to give him
some incentives," recalls Robert Ditmore, a former UnitedHealth director in the late 1980s. By the end of
1990, Dr. McGuire had options on 229,277 shares, or 0.7% of the company.
This was the beginning of a period when U.S. corporations began making options a big part of pay packages,
seeking to align the interests of executives and shareholders. Traditionally, hired CEOs enjoyed big salaries
but their puny stockholdings left them no hope of approaching the epic wealth of the Rockefellers or DuPonts.
But as stock options took off, highflying bosses such as Dr. McGuire propelled themselves into the top ranks
of their companies' shareholder registries. As stock prices rose, these bosses became tycoons, too.
In the early 1990s, the HMO boom continued and Dr. McGuire found more ways for his company to pull
ahead of the pack. He spent heavily on information technology, ensuring that UnitedHealth could handle its
fast-growing membership even as rivals got snarled in data blowups. And he showed a knack for acquisitions,
picking up smaller plans that fit well into UnitedHealth's networks.
The stock jumped 10-fold during Dr. McGuire's first five years at the company. Directors purred. "We knew
we had a really strong leader in Bill," recalls Walter Mondale, the former presidential candidate, who was a
member of the UnitedHealth board's compensation committee in the early 1990s. "We wanted to keep him
and help him with incentives."
During his first seven years as CEO, Dr. McGuire's base salary more than tripled, to $1.3 million in 1998. His
bonus jumped nearly as briskly for a few years. Then in 1997 and 1998, Dr. McGuire told directors that he
would forgo his bonus. The company's stock price and earnings growth had stalled amid a backlash against
restrictive health plans.
His sacrifice impressed directors. As the expiration of his contract approached in mid-1999, the directors and
the CEO met repeatedly to talk about a new contract.
Dr. McGuire at the end of 1998 had unrealized gains of $22 million on his existing options. That might have
seemed like a huge sum a decade earlier. But Leonard Abramson, the founder of U.S. Healthcare Inc., netted
about $900 million when he sold his company to Aetna Inc. in 1996. And dot-com mania was at its peak.
CEOs of health-care start-ups such as WebMD Inc. held 5% or bigger stakes in their companies, which
looked like passports to great wealth.
In contract negotiations, Dr. McGuire pushed for more options, and directors agreed. When his contract was
renewed, effective Oct. 13, 1999, he got options equivalent to 2% of UnitedHealth's shares outstanding. That
was the biggest slice Dr. McGuire had ever received. "Clearly we were aware of people getting huge gains on
Internet-stock situations. That was perhaps a factor in our mind," says director William Spears.
The dot-coms' onslaught proved laughably brief, but UnitedHealth kept rising. As the industry consolidated --
spurred by several deals Dr. McGuire engineered -- health-plan operators found it easier to raise prices.
Employers rarely complained, or if they did they directed their anger at the health-care system generally.
The better UnitedHealth fared, the more valuable Dr. McGuire's options became. Since 2000, he has cashed
out $488 million of options, yet the value of his remaining options keeps rising. The 1999 grant has proved
about seven times as valuable as the company projected when it was issued.
In Minnesota, such riches have infuriated some people. Joel Albers, a Minneapolis pharmacist, regularly
impersonates Dr. McGuire at state fairs, donning a tuxedo, holding up an enlarged picture of Dr. McGuire on
a stick and handing out leaflets denouncing corporate greed. Most people chuckle and walk on. But Mr.
Albers says he has a serious point. He has been urging Dr. McGuire to spend his options proceeds on
providing free health coverage for Minnesota's 77,000 uninsured children.
UnitedHealth executive John Penshorn says outsiders' efforts to tell Dr. McGuire how to spend his money are
"very parochial" because "the issues are broader than just Minnesota." Dr. McGuire has been speaking out
about national health system reform and UnitedHealth has opened advanced clinics that serve some of the
nation's poorest neighborhoods. Dr. McGuire also has set up a family foundation that gives away millions on
behalf of education, science and the arts.
Some of Dr. McGuire's most eye-catching gifts are unrelated to health care. In January, he announced he
was giving $10 million to help thousands of disadvantaged Minnesota children attend college. Dr. McGuire
also has given nearly $10 million to the University of Florida for a biodiversity center that includes one of the
world's largest butterfly research institutes. An avid lepidopterist himself, Dr. McGuire discovered the brown
Texas butterfly Euphyes mcguirei.
As long as UnitedHealth stock keeps climbing, big shareholders say they aren't likely to badger Dr. McGuire
for a pay cut. "It's hard to say what someone like that is worth," says Tom Marsico, head of Marsico Capital in

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Denver, which owns about 5% of UnitedHealth's shares. "But compared with hedge-fund managers or
athletes, he's probably doing more to improve the world."
Even so, UnitedHealth directors huddled several times last year to discuss whether they have showered Dr.
McGuire with too many options. His 1999 employment contract obligated the company to award him further
options on 2.6 million shares each year, adjusted for splits.
"The number [of options] became larger than we were comfortable with," says Mr. Spears, a member of the
UnitedHealth compensation committee. Directors in August 2005 eliminated the options guarantee. Dr.
McGuire received options last year on 1.7 million shares, down 35% from the previous year.
But several directors say they have no desire to peel away some of the CEO's longtime perks, such as a
$139,000 travel allowance and $69,100 of financial planning last year -- even though Dr. McGuire is long past
the point of needing help with everyday living costs.
"If we did reduce these things, Bill would take it as a signal that directors weren't enthusiastic about his
leadership," says Mr. Spears. "That would be a distraction, at the very least. Bill takes these things as a
benchmark of how directors feel about him."
During their August 2005 overhaul of Dr. McGuire's contract, directors did eliminate an unusual provision that
let Dr. McGuire choose when his options would be awarded. As this newspaper reported on March 18, option
grants to Dr. McGuire in 1997, 1999 and 2000 carried dates on which UnitedHealth's stock hit its low for the
year. Mr. Spears said in March that he wasn't aware of anything inappropriate about the options grants.
In an SEC filing April 7, UnitedHealth said a committee of independent directors will work with outside lawyers
to review the company's "current and historic stock option grant procedures." The company said the board
review was Dr. McGuire's idea.

Stocks Slide as Oil Closes Above $70; Gold Tops $600 on Concern Over Iran
Monday's Markets
Stocks Slide as Oil Closes Above $70; Gold Tops $600 on Concern Over Iran
By Dave Kansas
629 words
18 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS SLID as oil prices shot above $70 a barrel amid concerns that prices might push higher still,
sapping global economic expansion.
Gold futures also closed above $600 an ounce for the first time, for a current-month contract, since January
1981.
Investors have largely shrugged off high oil prices during the past several months, recently pushing major
market measures to multiyear highs. But with concerns about Iran's nuclear program mounting and demand
from places such as China and India increasing, the persistent upward creep in oil is eroding confidence
among investors.
The specter of another big move in oil -- crude futures have already risen 15% this year -- dominated the dour
mood among stock traders. Though still up 3.3% for the year, the Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 63.87
points yesterday, or 0.57%, to 11073.78, now just one big down day away from the psychologically important
11000 level, which it has held above since March 9.
Other major market measures also fell, with the Nasdaq Composite losing 14.95 to 2311.16 and the Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index shedding 3.79 to 1285.33. The Nasdaq is up 4.8% for the year to date, and the S&P
is up 3%.
Oil for May delivery rose $1.08 a barrel to $70.40 on the New York Mercantile Exchange, the highest closing
price for oil since the contract was begun in 1983. On an inflation-adjusted basis, oil remains below its
record of $97.21, in current dollars, set in 1980.
Along with oil prices, gasoline prices are on the rise just ahead of the summer driving season, posing another
threat to economic growth, and, by extension, to stocks.
Gold prices also romped to another 25-year high. While gold tends to head higher on concerns about
inflation or a weak dollar, some traders indicated the risk that the Iran situation might move from simmer to
boil has caused some safety seekers to clutch gold more tightly. Gold for April delivery leapt $18.90, or
3.2%, to $615.40 an ounce.
It was gold futures' biggest gain in percentage terms since the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks. Gold is up
19% for the year to date.
In the bond market, Treasury prices rose after weak news from the housing market. The National Association
of Home Builders index for sales of new, single-family homes came in lower than Wall Street expectations
and indicated that builders are essentially split evenly in terms of "good" sales expectations and "bad" sales
expectations.
With energy prices dominating the news, investors mostly overlooked a string of solid earnings reports.
Citigroup reported strong results, and its shares edged higher. Several big companies report earnings this
week, including Pfizer, International Business Machines, McDonald's and J.P. Morgan Chase.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. Major European markets remained closed for Easter. The Dow
Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose 1.82 points to 218.66
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks fell. Total volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 1.77 billion shares. Some
1,830 stocks fell, and 1,470 advanced.

Bond prices rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 10/32, or $3.125 for each $1,000 invested, dropping the
yield to 5.014%. The 30-year bond rose 15/32, its yield down to 5.085%.
The dollar slumped. Late in New York, the currency traded at 117.83 yen, down from 118.67 yen in Friday's
limited trading, while the euro rose against the greenback to $1.2256 from $1.2111.

Dialing for a Higher Stock Price --- Brazilian Telecom Shifts to a Single, More Popular Class of Stock
World Stock Markets
Dialing for a Higher Stock Price --- Brazilian Telecom Shifts to a Single, More Popular Class of Stock
By Geraldo Samor
1,161 words
18 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Rio de Janeiro -- SEEKING TO BOOST its stock price through better corporate governance, Brazil's biggest
telecommunications company, Tele Norte Leste Participacoes SA, known as Telemar, announced a plan to
eliminate a two-class shareholder structure that concentrates power in the hands of a small group of
investors.
The move follows similar reorganizations by aircraft maker Empresa Brasileira de Aeronautica SA, or
Embraer, and poultry maker Perdigao SA. The two companies recently abolished different classes of stock to
be able to trade on Brazil's Novo Mercado, a separate listing within the Sao Paulo Stock Exchange that
requires a higher level of corporate governance. Research shows that companies in the Novo Mercado trade
at multiples as much as 30% higher than companies that aren't listed there.
Brazil's biggest family-owned businesses often have two classes of stock: nonvoting shares, usually more
easily traded and held by minority investors, and voting shares, which are rarely traded and are often
concentrated in the hands of a family or a small group of investors. Corporate-reform advocates in Brazil
argue that empowering minority shareholders will make companies more open and accountable. (Some U.S.
companies have similar structures, including Dow Jones &Co., the publisher of The Wall Street Journal.)
The two-tier corporate system came into vogue in Brazil in the late 1960s, when the country's military rulers
pushed through a law allowing companies to have as much as two-thirds of their capital in nonvoting shares.
That allowed Brazil's wealthy families to tap the markets for capital without losing control of their companies.
Because of that law, families can control their business so long as they own half of the voting shares -- which
works out to just 17% of total capital. That structure reduces the power of minority shareholders and can lead
to abuse by controlling shareholders.
Telemar is controlled by the Andrade Gutierrez SA construction conglomerate; La Fonte, a diversified family
conglomerate, and the private-equity firm GP Investimentos. The reorganization provides the controlling
shareholders a way to sell their interests. Analysts previously had expected them to sell the company to a
foreign buyer such as Portugal Telecom or Mexican business tycoon Carlos Slim.
Under the plan announced yesterday, shareholders of Telemar -- whose American depositary shares are
among Latin America's most-traded on the New York Stock Exchange -- and its main operating company, will
swap their shares for stock in Telemar's parent company, Telemar Participacoes SA, which will then be listed
in Brazil and on the Big Board. As part of the plan, Telemar's controlling shareholders also will sell some of
their stock to the public. Telemar's American depositary shares rose 5.3% on the news.
Fund managers said investors will watch carefully the terms of the stock swaps in the deal because Telemar
has a history of conflict with minority shareholders. In 2001, the company acquired certain assets from IG, a
big Internet portal based in Sao Paulo, over the protest of minority shareholders. Some IG shareholders were
also Telemar shareholders. That same year, Telemar took a 17.3% stake in Pegasus Telecom SA, a telecom
firm, for some $47 million at today's exchange rate. At the time, Andrade Gutierrez owned 100% of Pegasus
voting stock. Telemar has always maintained those deals were appropriate.
After the restructuring is completed, Telemar will change its name to Oi Participacoes SA, a name borrowed
from the company's fast-growing wireless operation. Oi, in Portuguese, means "hi."
As part of the plan, Telemar also announced a series of provisions to enhance its governance. The company
said its board will be composed of at least 11 members, the majority of whom will be independent directors.
Currently, the controlling shareholders appoint the majority of the directors. Under the new arrangement,
shareholders will be able to vote their shares up to a maximum of 10% of the company's capital. The
company will also require that anyone acquiring more than 20% of the company make an offer to all other
shareholders.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Telemar was spun off from Brazil's state-owned telephone company Telebras and privatized in 1998. Since
then, the company has invested 25 billion reals ($12 billion at today's exchange rate), but its core telephone
business has suffered amid competition from new technologies, and its wireless business is up against rivals
such as Telecom Italia SpA and Spain's Telefonica.
Telemar has 15 million phone lines in service, making it Brazil's biggest telecommunications company. After
four years of operation, Oi, its wireless arm, has 11 million clients. Rothschild and UBS AG are advising the
company.
---
Yesterday's Market Activity
Asian markets traded mixed yesterday, while European markets were closed for the Easter holiday.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies retreated as oil and gold prices rose, sparking concerns that
brewing inflation could mean higher long-term bond yields.
Taiwan, Shanghai, Indian and Singapore markets advanced.
In Seoul, shares fell moderately after climbing to a record high late last week. Markets in Hong Kong,

Gold Leaps Past $600 on Worry About Iran, Weakness of Dollar
Commodities Report
Gold Leaps Past $600 on Worry About Iran, Weakness of Dollar
By Allen Sykora
449 words
18 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures pushed to their strongest level in more than a quarter of a century as geopolitical tensions over
Iran's nuclear program and a tumble in the U.S. dollar fueled speculative buying, traders and analysts said.
Nearby April-delivery gold settled up $18.90 to $615.40 yesterday and hit a high of $616.50 an ounce on the
Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. Most-active June gold settled up $18.70 to $618.80
an ounce after peaking at $619.30.
Comex gold hasn't been at such lofty levels since December 1980.
"There are increased geopolitical tensions, mainly from Iran," said Peter Grandich, publisher of the Grandich
Letter. "Also, the topping of the U.S. dollar is now evident."
As gold was closing, the euro had risen to $1.2256 from $1.2111 late Friday.
Buying of metals in general continues, Mr. Grandich added, referring to recent gains in copper and silver as a
"near parabolic rise."
George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures, cited higher energy prices, as crude
oil climbed back above $70 a barrel. There was a "tremendous" amount of short covering, which is buying by
gold traders to offset positions in which they had previously sold, said Mr. Gero. "New funds were coming in,"
he said. "And there were the momentum players who always buy new highs in heavy volume."
The record high London gold fix, in the spot market, was $850 in January 1980. A spot continuation chart
shows Comex gold got up to $875 back then.
Messrs. Grandich and Gero cautioned that gold is reaching such lofty levels that the market could be
vulnerable to a pullback.
"When everything looks so bullish, an unexpected sale by a central bank or an unexpected political event
could have a lot of the weak holders in the market wanting to exit," said Mr. Gero.
Mr. Grandich said he eventually looks for more gains, but feels the market could be "overextended" in the
short term.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Front-month futures breached the $70 mark, powered by continued concerns over Iran's nuclear
program and China's economic growth. The May contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange settled
$1.08 higher at $70.40 a barrel, a settlement high.
SUGAR: Prices on the New York Board of Trade rose partly because of the influence of higher outside
markets such as crude oil. Trading of the May-July contract spread was also a factor. Nybot May world sugar
futures settled up 0.39 cent at 16.88 cents a pound.
