Small Stocks Lose Some Strength, But Hayes Lemmerz and Buca Rise
By Anjali Cordeiro
474 words
8 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Small-capitalization stocks, which had been soaring, fell Friday after a report showing strong job creation
pushed up bond yields and reignited worries about higher interest rates.
Although small caps had a lackluster week, they continue to outpace the broad market by a wide margin so
far this year. The small-cap group rallied right through the first quarter. Both the small-cap Russell 2000 Index
and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index are up about 12% for the year to date.
Hayes Lemmerz International jumped 34 cents, or 14%, to $2.80. The Northville, Mich., provider of
automotive parts said it is streamlining its organizational structure and reducing some wage and benefit costs
in an attempt to improve profitability.
Buca surged 85 cents, or 18%, to 5.58. The Minneapolis operator of Italian restaurants reported a 5.6%
increase in first-quarter comparable sales at its Buca di Beppo restaurants.
SafeNet slid 5.01, or 19%, to 20.96. The Baltimore security-software company lowered its forecast for the first
quarter, citing higher expenses related to Sarbanes Oxley and a terminated acquisition.
Overall, small caps did worse than the broad market in the past week. The Russell 2000 Index fell 10.10
points, or 1.32%, to 756.13. For the week, the index lost 9.01, or 1.2%. The Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600
Index fell 4.78, or 1.21%, to 391.01. For the week, the index fell 3.82, or 1%.
"The employment report showed that the economy is strong and that employment is strong," said Edgar
Peters, chief investment strategist at PanAgora Asset Management. "The Federal Reserve is concerned
about wage inflation, and they are likely to keep raising interest rates 'til they see some slowing." These
concerns pushed bond yields higher Friday and eventually weighed on stocks, too, Mr. Peters said.
Precious-metals and mining-sector shares fell as gold futures retreated from recent highs because of profit
taking. Canyon Resources (AMEX) slumped four cents, or 4.4%, to 87 cents, and Eldorado Gold (AMEX) lost
15 cents, or 3%, to 4.89.
Real-estate investment trusts pulled back in the face of higher bond yields. REITs are large dividend payers
and their shares lose some of their appeal when interest rates are higher. Potlatch shed 1.40, or 3.4%, to
39.70; Equity LifeStyle Properties slipped 1.05, or 2.1%, to 48, and Equity Inns fell 34 cents, or 2.2%, to
15.12, all three on the NYSE.
OraLabs Holding nearly tripled, rising 3.76 to 5.75 after the Parker, Colo., maker of oral-care products said it
had agreed to a stock-exchange agreement with Partner Success Holdings. An OraLabs representative
couldn't be reached.

Gold Prices Retreat From $600 Level
Commodities Report
Gold Prices Retreat From $600 Level
By Allen Sykora
495 words
8 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures prices fell from near the $600-an-ounce level as investors sought to take profits, with further
pressure from weakness in crude-oil prices and the euro, analysts said.
Benchmark April-delivery gold dropped $6.80, or 1.1%, to $588.40 an ounce from Thursday's 25-year high,
and the most-active June contract dropped $7 to $592.70, Friday on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. That contract had peaked at $603.10 during overnight screen trade. Silver was more
resilient, with the May contract edging up 2.5 cents to $12.07.
"We played with that $600 level. I think the market's inability to get back above it early [Friday] -- with the
weaker energy and stronger dollar -- all contributed to a little profit-taking," said Jim Quinn, commodity floor
analyst with A.G. Edwards.
Also Friday, the euro fell 1% to $1.2102 from $1.2232 Thursday and Nymex May crude-oil prices weakened.
Several analysts said that it wasn't surprising to see gold pull back from the $600 area, because it was an
important psychological level. They added that gold ran into some technical chart-based resistance there.
"We've rung the bell at $600 and now we're perhaps ready to consolidate before we consider higher price
levels," said Tim Evans, an analyst with IFR Markets.
Gold has been trending higher for a few years, amid geopolitical concerns, higher crude-oil prices, worries
about inflation, increased investment demand prompted by still-young gold exchange-traded funds, as well
as general investment interest in commodities as an alternative to stocks and low cash-deposit rates.
John Person, president of National Futures Advisory Service, said a number of analysts had put long-term
gold targets around $600, thus it was a logical place for many traders to book profits.
"The market should probably pause," he said. "We don't want to see prices accelerate too far, too fast and go
into hyperdrive. If a bull market is going to stay intact, a strong steady pace of price change should take
place.
"If prices accelerate too far over $600, people will feel the market is a little overburdened from the long side."
In other commodity markets:
CORN: Prices rose at the Chicago Board of Trade to the highest level since August as the market continues
to find speculative-buying interest on ideas that less acreage will be devoted to corn this year despite
climbing ethanol demand. May corn gained 1.5 cents to $2.43 a bushel.
CRUDE OIL: After failing to breach $68 a barrel Thursday, futures prices recoiled on the prospect that the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries won't trim output this year. Also, Nigeria said its derailed oil
flows may resume in a month. The May contract settled 55 cents lower at $67.39 a barrel on the New York
Mercantile Exchange.

For Mutual Funds, Commodities Boom Proves Surprisingly Hard to Exploit
Fund Track
For Mutual Funds, Commodities Boom Proves Surprisingly Hard to Exploit
By Tom Lauricella and Diya Gullapalli
1,101 words
7 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
COMMODITIES ARE HOT, with gold closing in on $600 an ounce. But some mutual funds that invest in
commodities are being stopped cold.
These funds don't invest directly in commodities -- silver, crude oil, pork bellies -- but instead enter into
complicated financial agreements designed to track their prices. Investors have flocked to funds like these
over the past year or so amid a global boom in commodities. Now, unexpected problems with the funds' tricky
underlying investments -- generically known as derivatives -- are causing headaches for fund companies as
well as their investors.
A week ago, OppenheimerFunds announced it was shutting off all new investments in its $1.8 billion Real
Asset Fund due to problems arranging the investments it is designed to make. This comes on the heels of a
December Internal Revenue Service ruling that forced the $12.3 billion Pimco CommodityRealReturn
Strategy to overhaul its portfolio, and invest in the same types of securities causing issues at Oppenheimer.
Other funds face the same issues.
Investing in commodities has been a challenge for small investors. It is impractical to own direct interests in
them -- few people want, say, bars of silver or bushels of wheat. And futures, which are basically financial
contracts that track the prices of commodities, can be risky and complicated to manage.
While the mutual-fund format addresses these issues, the current problems mean investors might want to
"think twice" before putting money into these mutual funds, says Morningstar Inc. analyst Karen Dolan, at
least until the current crop of issues is cleared up.
For investors, the appeal of commodities is that they rarely move in lock step with stocks or bonds. As a
result, when added to a portfolio in small doses, they can smooth out a portfolio's ups and downs. In addition,
they can provide protection against inflation, which is often accompanied by a rise in commodity prices.
Over the past year or so, investor attention has been focused on a handful of commodities markets where
prices have skyrocketed. Some basic-materials prices, such as on copper, have surged thanks in part to big
demand from China. Other key factors: problems with global oil supplies (Hurricane Katrina caused a big
disruption to the Gulf of Mexico oil business), as well as a rush by investors to snap up gold, which is a
popular investment at times when inflation may be on the rise. Yesterday, for instance, Comex gold closed
at a 25-year high, finishing at $595.20 a troy ounce, up 1.2%.
While mutual funds are an effective way for individuals to own stocks or bonds, tax rules governing funds
makes it tricky to design a fund that can easily invest in commodities. Regulations require funds to put most
of their money into investments that generate what the IRS defines as securities-related income. That
prohibits funds from owning, say, a herd of cattle and certain types of futures. Mutual funds can own futures
in small doses, but a fund entirely dedicated to them would cross the line.
(There are also commodity "pools" that, on the surface, look like mutual funds and invest directly in
commodities and futures. But they have different tax treatment and legal structures, and generally aren't
marketed to small investors.)
In 1997 Oppenheimer Real Asset Fund opened with what at the time was a unique strategy for a mutual fund:
It would negotiate derivative transactions with financial institutions such as brokerage houses and insurance
companies that, in essence, converted the fund's money into securities that tracked the ups and downs of
commodities markets.
Over the long term, the strategy has served investors well. The Oppenheimer fund has gained an average of
20% a year over the past three years and 13% a year over the past five years, far outpacing the benchmark
for many stock funds, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index.

--- Page 89 ---
Page 89 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As interest in commodities exploded, several fund companies, the biggest of which is the Pimco fund, created
new offerings but used a different strategy.
Then last December the IRS ruled that the strategy employed by Pimco, as well as smaller funds run by
Rydex Investments of Rockville, Md., and Potomac Funds, based in New York, violated the tax rules
governing mutual funds. The IRS ruled that the investments used by these funds didn't meet its definition of
securities. The funds have until the end of June to switch to appropriate investments, in particular the kinds
employed by the Oppenheimer portfolio.
John Brynjolfsson, portfolio manager of the Pimco fund, says he doesn't expect any hiccups in the transition.
OppenheimerFunds officials thought they had dodged a bullet because the IRS ruling didn't affect their
strategy. But at about the same time Oppenheimer was blindsided by another issue: A number of the
companies that it had dealt with cut back on the business. This happened as the fund was growing fast --
going from $1.1 billion in assets at the end of 2005 to $1.8 billion today.
"We simply cannot line up a sufficient quantity," says Tom Keffer, director of product management.
Oppenheimer officials say they expect the situation to be temporary, but will likely take months to resolve.
They say the problems aren't related to the IRS ruling or other funds turning to such investments.
Still, the Oppenheimer situation is creating some nervousness. Jim King, director of portfolio management at
Rydex, which offers the $45 million Rydex Commodities Fund, hopes that additional demand for the
investments he needs will bring more issuers, but adds, there is still "a little bit of trepidation right now."
New York-based Potomac Funds, which has one commodity fund in operation and another on the way, says
it doesn't expect to have a problem making the transition. "We've had a variety of big brokers pitch us," says
Dan O'Neill, president of Potomac Funds. "My understanding is it's very doable."
Fidelity Investments' Fidelity Strategic Real Return Fund made its debut in September and puts a quarter of
its $609 million into investments linked to the Dow Jones-AIG Commodities Index and related investments. A
Fidelity spokesman declined to comment on whether the fund has had any issues obtaining the notes.
Another type of investment, exchange-traded funds (which trade on an exchange like stocks), don't appear to
run into these sorts of issues. Because of their structure, they can invest heavily in futures contracts and, in
some cases, invest directly in commodities such as precious metals.

Page 90 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Golden Run: Rally Near $600 Reflects Tensions --- Geopolitical Concerns, As Well as Speculation, Aid Biggest Climb Since '01
Commodities Report
The Golden Run: Rally Near $600 Reflects Tensions --- Geopolitical Concerns, As Well as Speculation,
Aid Biggest Climb Since '01
By Allen Sykora
642 words
7 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
COMEX, a unit of the New York Mercantile Exchange, began trading gold futures on Dec. 31, 1974, when a
ban on private ownership and trading of gold bullion was lifted. An April 7 Commodities column incorrectly
said the Comex wasn't yet trading gold futures on Jan. 21, 1980, when gold hit a record high of $850 a troy
ounce.
(WSJ April 20, 2006)
(END)
It was the latest milestone in a long-running rally for gold.
Some gold-futures contracts topped $600 a troy ounce at one point yesterday for the first time in 25 years,
helped by a weaker dollar and rising energy prices.
Though prices settled below $600, the yellow metal is in its biggest rally since the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist
attacks. At that time, gold had been out of favor on Wall Street for about 20 years, but it quickly reclaimed its
traditional role as an investor haven during times of political and economic uncertainty.
Buyers have continued to snap up gold ever since, encouraged by U.S. involvement in two wars, heavy
government deficits, several catastrophic natural disasters and tension with Iran over its nuclear aspirations.
Yesterday, the most-active June gold futures gained $7.20, or 1.2%, to $599.70 a troy ounce on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. The contract peaked at $601.90 in overnight screen trading.
The benchmark contract, nearby April futures, settled up $7.30 at $595.20, peaking at $595.60.
Analysts said buying interest from speculators also was a factor in trade.
"It was continued fund buying," said Dave Meger, analyst with Alaron Trading. "It was higher in line with the
energy complex."
Nymex-traded May crude oil rose to its strongest level since early February.
Early-day strength in the euro contributed to gold's strength as the yellow metal traditionally benefits from a
weaker dollar, based on the belief that investors will use gold as an alternative, widely accepted store of
value when they lose confidence in paper money. The European currency peaked at $1.2330 before later
backing down.
Mr. Meger said speculators appeared willing to keep buying gold even at these high levels.
During the session, traders and analysts cited several other factors helping gold for some time now. These
include geopolitical concerns in the Middle East, fears of inflation, popularity of gold exchange-traded funds
that are backed by metal put into storage and investors looking for alternatives to equities.
Gold is still off its all-time high of $850, which is a spot cash price, set on Jan. 21, 1980, before the Comex
futures market existed for the metal. Analysts said adjusted for inflation, gold would need to reach roughly
$2,200 a troy ounce to match it.
Several analysts said they look for still more gains for gold over the longer haul, although some cautioned the
market could be due for a pullback and consolidation in the short term.

--- Page 91 ---
Page 91 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management, said it is hard to forecast a top since so much
of the gains have been due to fund flows into gold and other commodities, more so than fundamentals.
"These prices cannot be justified in any way," he said. "But will they go higher? The answer is probably yes,
simply because money is flowing into commodities."
Silver also rallied, with most-active May rising 34 cents a troy ounce to $12.045. April rose 34.2 cents to
$12.007.

A Century Later, Chinatown Revisits History of Quake --- Facing San Francisco Ouster, Survivors Stood Ground; Mr. Lee's Bayonet Wound
A Century Later, Chinatown Revisits History of Quake --- Facing San Francisco Ouster, Survivors
Stood Ground; Mr. Lee's Bayonet Wound
By Rebecca Smith
1,219 words
7 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SAN FRANCISCO -- Bruce Quan Jr. didn't know much about his great-grandfather before he noticed
intriguing references connecting him to the great earthquake and fire that destroyed this city on April 18,
1906.
When Mr. Quan, a 59-year-old law professor, asked his great-aunt for more information, she retrieved two
boxes that had been sitting in her attic since the 1940s. As he rummaged through the papers and
photographs dating from the early 20th century, Mr. Quan recalls thinking: "Wow. This is it."
As the centennial of the earthquake approaches, Mr. Quan and others in San Francisco's Chinese-American
community have embarked on a journey of rediscovery, mining family histories and learning some surprising
things along the way.
From the bits and pieces of personal history Mr. Quan recovered, he learned that his great-grandfather, Lew
Hing, was one of a group of Chinese merchants who stood their ground against civic leaders who wanted to
move Chinatown, and instead demanded to rebuild it where it had always been.
The disaster was a "defining moment" for Chinese America, says Philip P. Choy, an architect and
preservationist born in Chinatown in 1926. Chinatown's merchants confronted anti-Chinese elements and
insisted on their right to self-determination.
Rose Pak, a Chinatown community leader and consultant to the city's Chinese Chamber of Commerce, says
she has been pushing Chinese-American families to compile their histories to build a record of the disaster
from Chinatown's perspective. Ms. Pak, 57, says she warned museums and city officials preparing to mark
the 100th anniversary not to forget the suffering and mistreatment of Chinatown's residents.
Some descendants of those who lived through the disaster are now talking for the first time about what their
ancestors went though. Connie Young Yu, 64, a historian who has written extensively about the
Chinese-American experience, is one.
She did not fully appreciate until this year, she says, that her grandfather, merchant Lee Yoke Suey, "risked
his life" after the disaster to recover proof of citizenship. The centennial, she says, "gives us a reason to find
this hidden history."
In the boxes recovered from the attic, Mr. Quan found personal letters, photographs, business papers, letters
of introduction, promissory notes, even a pair of tiny shoes that belonged to his great-grandmother, who had
bound feet. The material led him to dig into historical accounts of early Chinatown and of the disaster.
Eventually, he pieced together one small slice of the story of how the great earthquake upended, then
brought together, the city's Chinese community.
Mr. Lew immigrated from Southern China in 1869, at age 12, and went to work for an older brother. By 1906,
he owned Pacific Coast Canning Co., one of the largest canneries in the West, which employed 1,000
seasonal workers in Oakland. Later investments included a bank, steamship line and hotel.
The 1906 earthquake hit before dawn, toppling buildings, buckling streets and rupturing water lines. Fire
broke out immediately. As it closed in on Mr. Lew's large house in Chinatown, he fled with his wife to his
Oakland cannery, Mr. Quan learned. When the fire was extinguished three days later, his house was
destroyed, more than half the city was a smoldering ruin, 200,000 people were homeless and thousands
more were dead or missing. Even today, it ranks as the worst urban fire in U.S. history.
For many whites, the Chinatown of old had a lurid reputation for gambling, opium smoking, "idol worship,"
and prostitution. A bubonic plague scare in 1900 had stirred fear that Chinatown would become a breeding
ground for contagious disease, as well. Its 20,000 residents had deflected previous efforts to push them off

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the valuable strip of land they occupied between the mansion district atop Nob Hill and the commercial district
at its base, according to historical accounts.
At first, it looked as though the earthquake would be the event that finally wiped Chinatown off the map. At his
Oakland cannery, Mr. Lew heard distressing reports of Chinese refugees being rounded up by soldiers and
herded from one holding area to another, Mr. Quan discovered.
Mr. Lew got the word out "that anyone who could get to Oakland on the ferry would be taken in" at his
cannery, says Mr. Quan. Many of the 4,000 refugees who came to Oakland wound up there, where Mr. Lew
fed and clothed some of them for months, he says.
Ms. Yu, the historian, who lives outside San Francisco in Los Altos Hills, had balked for years about sharing
the story of what happened to her grandfather, Mr. Lee, after the earthquake. She says it was too humiliating.
As the fire approached, her grandfather bundled his family off to safety, then returned alone to his family's
Chinatown store to retrieve important papers, including his proof of citizenship, according to family accounts.
A soldier rushed into the store, and mistaking him for a looter, bayoneted him in the side, he later told family
members. His injuries weren't serious, partly because he was wearing a Chinese-style quilted jacket, and he
escaped with his documents, which Ms. Yu now has.
Ms. Yu says she decided to share his story for the first time this month during a panel discussion about the
earthquake at the Chinese Historical Society of America in Chinatown. She was motivated, she says, by
descriptions she'd read about black residents of New Orleans being mistaken for looters after the hurricane.
Five days after the earthquake, San Francisco's white leaders formed a committee to relocate Chinatown to
an isolated area of slaughterhouses and mud flats on the city's southern fringe, which they planned to call
"Oriental City." Some Chinatown businessmen thought it pointless to resist. But Mr. Quan's great-grandfather,
Mr. Lew, and others considered it essential to rebuild their old neighborhood.
When they met with city officials, Chinatown's merchants gambled. If denied the opportunity to rebuild, they
said, they would move, en masse, to Seattle or Los Angeles "or some other community that wanted them and
would treat them better," according to Sue Lee, executive director of the Chinese Historical Society.
City leaders did not want to lose valuable trade controlled by Chinatown merchants. Chinese workers swiftly
began rebuilding, and the relocation effort collapsed, according to San Francisco historian Gladys Hansen.
Chinatown had been filled with brick buildings dating from the Gold Rush. In their place, merchants and
community associations erected new "Oriental"-style buildings, many with multi-tier pagoda-type roofs, fancy
tile and iron work and colorful paint. The style was not authentically Chinese -- the fancy roofs in Chinatown
were put on commercial buildings, not Buddhist shrines -- but they appealed to tourists. They also marked the
neighborhood as Chinese, which Mr. Choy, the architect, characterizes as a "tactical response" to the "threat
of removal and annihilation."
Mr. Lee reopened the family store in 1907 in a building made of clinker, or salvaged bricks fused together by
the fire. Mr. Quan's great-grandfather, Mr. Lew, erected a building with Chinese details that stands today in
the heart of Chinatown.

Japan Catches the IPO Fever --- Livedoor Is Just a Memory as Overall Market Continues Ascent
World Stock Markets
Japan Catches the IPO Fever --- Livedoor Is Just a Memory as Overall Market Continues Ascent
By Yuka Hayashi
1,222 words
7 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tokyo -- JAPANESE COMPANIES are going public in droves, unfazed by troubles at former highflier
Livedoor Co., an Internet start-up felled by investigations and accusations of accounting fraud. But analysts
caution investors to choose carefully: The frenzy to list doesn't mean all new shares are good buys.
In the year's first four months, 64 companies are expected to have initial public offerings, compared with 52 in
the same period of 2005, according to TokyoIPO.com, a research company. If the momentum continues,
Japan's record for the number of companies listed in a year -- 203, set in 2001 -- could be broken.
The IPO fever rages as the overall stock market continues to rally. Yesterday the Topix index of all Tokyo
Stock Exchange first-section issues finished at a 14-year high; meanwhile, the Nikkei Stock Average of 225
major companies rose 245.35 points, or 1.4%, to 17489.33, its highest close in nearly six years.
Japan's zest for new issues has affected everyone from start-up companies to regional stock exchanges to
small brokerage firms. And while big companies such as oil refiner Idemitsu Kosan Co. and Aozora Bank Ltd.
may go public this year, there is an increasing number of smaller companies having IPOs -- and more cases
in which the share price shoots up when trading begins, but then plummets. Not all IPOs this year will be
good investments, particularly for investors who want to hold the shares beyond the first couple of days of
trading.
"The market has become polarized," says Hisashi Matsumoto, an IPO analyst at Mitsubishi UFJ Securities,
the brokerage unit of Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group. "There is a huge divergence between the shares that
go up sharply and those that struggle."
The IPO boom comes as Japan's economic expansion of the past few years is raising the country's market
indexes to five-year highs.
The Livedoor scandal, which caused losses for hundreds of thousands of individual investors, hasn't dented
interest in listings of what are considered emerging companies. Shares of Livedoor are expected to be
delisted next week. As the company's woes became known early this year, stocks of emerging companies in
general fell sharply. But recently, many have recovered, at least partially.
The index for the Mothers section for start-ups at the Tokyo Stock Exchange, where Livedoor was listed and
many IPOs take place, fell to 1488 in late February, from 2799 in mid-January. But the index has bounced
back since then, closing at 1936 yesterday.
The Livedoor episode has encouraged investors to look more carefully at start-up companies, before buying
in.
Most Japanese IPOs involve two markets -- the Jasdaq Securities Exchange and the TSE's Mothers. But an
increasing number come from the newly created start-up sections at regional exchanges, such as the Nagoya
Stock Exchange's Centrex, the Osaka Securities Exchange's Hercules and Sapporo Stock Exchange's
Ambitious.
In December the Ambitious market raised some eyebrows by approving a listing by Tokyo online-game
company Gamepot Inc., a subsidiary of Aeria Inc. -- which had its own IPO a year earlier on Hercules. Critics
say the IPO by Gamepot was effectively listing the same company twice, as Aeria and its founders own more
than 80% of Gamepot, which accounts for a majority of Aeria's revenue. In Japan a company is asked to
prove that a subsidiary is separate enough from a parent to merit listing on its own.
"We recognize that the listing of Gamepot may have been a bit too early, but we live in a fast-moving industry
where if you hesitate for a moment, you are 100 steps behind your rivals," says Kimiyuki Suda, an Aeria
director. Mr. Suda adds that Aeria's growth plans would make Gamepot a smaller part of its total revenue.

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Shares of Gamepot have been fast-moving. Offered in December's IPO at 100,000 yen ($852), they
skyrocketed to close at 740,000 yen, but began dropping the next day. Yesterday, they were at 428,000 yen.
Hub Co., an operator of "British-style" pubs that sold shares at 140,000 yen each, saw them jump to 700,000
yen on the first day of trading Monday.
By contrast, the shares of three companies in more traditional industries -- Azuma Shipping Co., Yamashita
Medical Instruments Co. and Shonai Bank Ltd. -- were flat in initial trading after their IPOs.
---
Yesterday's Market Activity
Asian markets ended higher, led by gains in Hong Kong and Japan. Australia, New Zealand and Indonesia hit
record highs. European stock markets edged higher. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.62 of
a point, or 0.24%, to 254.74. Excluding the U.S., the index rose 1.31 point, or 0.60%, to 220.70.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index soared 311.04 points, or 1.93%, to 16411.13, its highest level in 5 1/2
years. The China Enterprises Index of mainland shares listed in Hong Kong, rose 1.60% to an 8 1/2-year
high, lifted by strong foreign buying in anticipation that China will continue to let its currency, the yuan,
appreciate against the dollar ahead of a state visit to the U.S. this month. China shares listed in Hong Kong
offer one of few ways foreign investors can benefit from the yuan's rise. Hong Kong Exchanges &Clearing,
parent of the local stock exchange, jumped as much as 11.7%.
In TOKYO, as the Nikkei and Topix rallied, shares of giant retailer Aeon jumped on strong earnings. News
that Toshiba is teaming with U.S. firm SanDisk to build a factory to produce NAND-type flash memory chips
fueled gains in shares of Tokyo Electron and Advantest.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 increased 1.60 points, or 0.03%, to 6045.70, as the central bank kept interest
rates on hold. Miners Kazakhmys and Xstrata advanced with gold futures at 25-year highs.
In FRANKFURT, the DAX 30 index gained 2.19 points, or 0.04%, to 6031.39. Tech stocks, including software
maker SAP, led advancers. A weaker euro failed to lift auto stocks; DaimlerChrysler and Volkswagen were
lower.
In SEOUL, South Korean shares rose for the 11th straight session, their longest winning streak in nearly
seven years, as foreign investors bought heavyweight semiconductor stocks. The Korea Composite Stock
Price Index rose 8.23 points, or 0.6%, to 1397.00.

Gold Tests $600, Economic Zest Pushes Up Rates
Thursday's Markets
Gold Tests $600, Economic Zest Pushes Up Rates
By E.S. Browning
453 words
7 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SURGING OIL PRICES and Treasury-bond yields caught up with the stock market, sending stocks lower
amid concerns about higher costs for businesses and consumers alike.
Other commodities including gold and copper also pushed upward, with gold futures hitting a 25-year high.
The contract for April delivery rose $7.30 a troy ounce to $595.20. Amid broad expectations that gold is going
higher, contracts for later delivery touched $600.
The yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note rose to 4.897% as prices fell, the highest yield since 2002.
Stock-index declines were modest reflecting continuing optimism about the economic strength that is pushing
yields and commodity prices higher. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 23.05 points, or 0.21%, to
11216.50, up 4.7% this year. It is still within 100 points of the five-year high hit earlier this year, and within
122 points of a six-year high.
"People are scared that overheated growth will force more Federal Reserve interest-rate increases," said
James Paulsen, chief investment strategist at Wells Capital Management.
Stocks that benefit from a strong economy, including technology stocks, continued to rise. The tech-heavy
Nasdaq Composite Index edged up 0.06%, or 1.42 points, to 2361.17, up 7.1% for 2006.
Crude-oil futures rose 87 cents to $67.94 a barrel, nearing the 23-year high of $69.81 hit Aug. 30.
There were some signs that growth could be moderating, but investors looked past them. Chain stores
announced weak March sales.
Investors are nervous about today's Labor Department report on March hiring and wages. Some investors
fear a strong report could push the Fed to raise interest rates, to cool the economy. That nervousness
helped push bond yields higher.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.19%, or 2.52 points, to 1309.04, still up 4.9% so far this year.
Merck's defeat late Wednesday in a liability suit involving its painkiller Vioxx helped to pull down other drug
stocks.
Global stocks hit another record, as measured by the Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
which rose 0.60%, or 1.31 points, to 220.70.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed; bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 13/32, or $4.06 for each $1,000 invested.
The 30-year bond was down one point, pushing the yield up to yield 4.966%.
The dollar strengthened. The currency traded at 117.63 yen, up from 117.38, while the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.2232 from $1.2292.

11239.55. It is ahead 4.9% this year and within 100 points of a six-year high.

rise in times of investor uncertainty, rose $1.90 to $587.90 an ounce.
A stream of Fed officials have been suggesting that the Fed soon could end its interest-rate increases, which
began in June 2004. Higher rates weigh on stocks by raising costs for companies and consumers alike. Late
Tuesday, Thomas Hoenig, president of the Kansas City Federal Reserve Bank, said the Fed is "very close to
where we need to be" on rates.
Fed officials "are saying all the right things right now," said stock trader Larry Peruzzi at Boston Company
Asset Management. "People have definitely forgotten about the oil price, which is kind of baffling."
A report showed the U.S. service economy, representing more than half of economic activity, expanded
strongly in March, fueling hopes for corporate profits. Treasury-bond yields fell slightly, although they
remained at heights rarely seen since 2004.

Intel chips, run Microsoft's Windows XP operating system. The Nasdaq Composite Index, full of tech stocks,
hit another five-year high, rising 0.61%, or 14.39 points, to 2359.75, up 7% for 2006.

shares; 2,028 stocks rose and 1,293 fell.

The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 117.38 yen, down from 117.44, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2292 from $1.2258.

Stocks Advance Amid Optimism On Rates, Profits --- Transportation Average Hits High; Nasdaq,
Industrials Also Climb As Investors Tame Inflation Fears
By E.S. Browning
518 words
5 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OPTIMISM ABOUT coming earnings reports and soothing words from Federal Reserve officials combined to
send the Nasdaq Composite Index to a five-year high.
Other indexes also advanced, with the 20-stock Dow Jones Transportation Average rising 1.29% to a record
4681.81, as investors welcomed the new quarter by channeling money into stocks.
Stock investors overcame the inflation fears that preoccupied them the day before, but bond investors
continued to worry that the Fed will keep nudging interest rates higher to keep the economy from
overheating. Higher rates would make existing bonds less attractive, so bond prices edged down. That
pushed the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up to 4.876%, the highest since June 2002.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 58.91 points, or 0.53%, to 11203.85, up 4.5% this year. It is 134
points from a six-year high and within sight of its January 2000 closing record, 11722.98.
The Nasdaq, whose many tech stocks would see stronger sales in a robust economy, turned in its highest
finish since before the 2001 terrorist attacks, up 0.37%, or 8.62 points, to 2345.36. It is up 6.4% in 2006 but
far from its closing record of 5048.62, in March 2000.
Few major companies have warned of first-quarter profit disappointments, noted Tim Smalls, head of U.S.
stock trading at brokerage firm Execution LLC in Greenwich, Conn. Amid hopes for low inflation and strong
profit gains, "you saw money being put to work on the second day of the new quarter," he said. Merck
forecast better-than-expected quarterly profits.
Investors were reassured after Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond President Jeffrey Lacker said he isn't
concerned about inflation. Dallas Fed President Richard Fisher said he doesn't see signs of recession,
either. Their comments stimulated hope that, though the Fed may raise its target interest rate at its next
meeting, May 10, it may stop soon after.
Investors were pleased to see oil futures fell 51 cents, though they remained high, at $66.23 a barrel. Gold,
typically a refuge in times of worry, and on a tear recently, pulled back $3.40 to $586.00 an ounce.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.63%, or 8.12 points, to 1305.93, up 4.6% this year -- just short
of a five-year high but far from its March 2000 record. Overseas stocks hit a record, measured by the Dow
Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, which rose 0.55%, or 1.19 points, to 218.64.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose; bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 63 cents per $1,000 invested. The
30-year bond was down 9/32 to yield 4.917%.
The dollar weakened, to 117.44 yen, down from 117.72. The euro rose to $1.2258 from $1.2142.

Common Sense: Time May Be Ripe For Profit-Taking, Numbers Suggest
Personal Finance
Common Sense: Time May Be Ripe For Profit-Taking, Numbers Suggest
By James B. Stewart SmartMoney
764 words
5 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS THE MARKET INDEXES keep flirting with records and the bull market rolls on, it's time to consider taking
some profits.
Let's take stock for a moment: The Federal Reserve has raised interest rates and recently signaled that
more increases may be in the offing. Long-term rates have finally crept up a bit, mortgage rates have risen
and housing prices seem to be cooling. Oil prices have stayed high. Iraq continues to be plagued by strife,
and Iran appears to be developing nuclear weapons. This is bullish?
That the market has nonetheless advanced toward a six-year high is exactly why market timing can be so
perilous. The Common Sense system relies strictly on numerical criteria measuring where the market has
been, not where it's going. When the Nasdaq has risen 25% from an interim low, as it will have when it
reaches 2365, I take some profits regardless of where I expect it to go next. If the market goes down, I'm a
buyer; if it rallies another 25%, I sell more.
Numbers aside, no two rallies are quite alike. This has been a relatively unassuming, even plodding one, with
few headline-grabbing advances. Volatility remains low. It has taken almost a year to reach these levels.
There seems to be little of the euphoria you might expect when the market hits a new milestone. That actually
bodes well for the rest of the year.
To see just what stocks and sectors have soared during the current rally, I used a SmartMoney.com screen to
identify the top-performing 300 stocks over the past 52 weeks, which pretty much coincides with this leg of
the bull market, which began last April 19.
This list ranged from GeoGlobal Resources, an oil-and-gas drilling company that has rights to drill in three
tracts off the coast of India, to Glamis Gold, a gold-mining concern.
It's not so surprising to find oil-and-gas or natural-resources companies on the list, given the long run-up in oil
and commodity prices. But none of the major oil producers made the cut. The largest-capitalization company
on the list was Luxembourg-based oil-field-services company Tenaris, which is hardly a household name in
the U.S. Basic-materials companies accounted for 42, or just 14%, of the list.
There were plenty of technology companies on the list -- almost twice as many as basic-materials companies.
Quite a few of the top stocks were once-highflying victims of the technology bust that many investors had
given up for dead, and many were in telecommunications or related fields: Redback Networks; Broadwing;
Ciena; Akamai Technologies; JDS Uniphase; Level 3 Communications; Nvidia; Corning; Red Hat; Broadcom;
Tellabs; and, of course, Google.
In a year when large pharmaceutical concerns continued to struggle, the health-care sector was surprisingly
well represented, with 69 companies on the list. Probably the best-known are Celgene and Elan. The others
are mostly smaller biotech concerns, some with few or even no products.
It's difficult to say how the list compares with previous rallies, but it is striking the degree to which it is
dominated by small and midcap stocks. Quite a few seem highly speculative. All the more reason, if you own
any of these stocks, to target them for reduction or elimination when the time comes to take profits.
It's probably hazardous to generalize, but the list demonstrates that the stock market's overall rise has been
influenced heavily by small and midcap stocks; the persistence of high oil and commodities prices; a revival
of telecommunications and technology spending; and continuing medical discoveries. Will these trends
continue? A correction in energy and commodities prices as well as in small-cap stocks seems long overdue.
Meanwhile, I feel fortunate to own four companies on the list: Ciena, Tellabs, Corning and Google. I've
already sold calls on Google. I'll be ready to move on the other three should the rally continue. And investors

--- Page 108 ---
Page 108 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
would do well to examine their own portfolios to see which stocks have performed best since April 2005. You
may be surprised and better prepared when the time comes to sell.
---

personal investing strategy. Unlike Dow Jones reporters, he may have positions in the stocks he writes about.

ESS Technology, Transmeta, FEI Climb Along With Broad Market

only moderate gains.

The day began with optimism about economic growth, and industrial stocks that would benefit from a strong
economy led the gains. News of the Alcatel-Lucent Technologies merger boosted stocks abroad, which
spilled into the early U.S. market. As often happens on a quarter's first day, big U.S. financial institutions had
new money to put to work. Investors also were pleased to see a report showing March manufacturing strong,
but not too strong -- suggesting the economy wasn't overheating and the Federal Reserve might not raise
interest rates much more.
But then investors began to focus on inflation. The manufacturing report showed that prices paid by
manufacturers were higher than expected. February construction spending was stronger than expected,
further fueling inflation fears. Bond yields were rising, suggesting bond investors were still concerned about
inflation. Oil prices were up.

over higher interest rates took over," said Jay Suskind, director of trading at brokerage firm Ryan Beck in
Florham Park, N.J.

Stock Market Quarterly Review: Commodities Prices Go Their Separate Ways --- As Broad Indexes Languish, Natural Gas Plunges, Sugar Surges...
Stock Market Quarterly Review: Commodities Prices Go Their Separate Ways --- As Broad Indexes
Languish, Natural Gas Plunges, Sugar Surges and Gold Is...Gold
By Peter A. McKay
1,108 words
3 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TRADERS SOMETIMES SAY there is no such thing as a commodities market, just disconnected markets,
plural, with each fluctuating for its own reasons.
That idea has been a tough sell in the past few years, as prices for everything from crude oil to frozen orange
juice zoomed ahead. As this year's first quarter ended, that idea again was gaining credence on Wall Street.
Broad-based commodity indexes were languishing, while some individual commodities made significant
swings up or down -- a trend that could continue well into 2006.
Natural-gas prices plunged almost 36%, or $4.02, to $7.21 a million British thermal units in the latest quarter
on the New York Mercantile Exchange because of a relatively mild winter in the Northeast and rising
inventories. Sugar prices surged 22% to 17.9 cents a pound at the New York Board of Trade, as Brazil and
other countries have increasingly used sugar cane to produce fuel. Crude-oil prices rose nearly 9.2% to
$66.63 a barrel at Nymex, boosted by worries about terrorism and global politics, especially tension over
Iran's nuclear program.
Meanwhile, the Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities was off 3.5%, or 5.96, to 165.19. The
Reuters/Jefferies CRB Index, which puts more emphasis on crude oil, eked out an increase of 0.4%, or 1.35
points, to 333.18.
Such broad commodity measures have flattened in part because one of two big factors behind the past few
years' eye-popping increases has become less important. China's economy still seems strong, which drives
demand for a variety of raw materials to churn out finished goods. The U.S. dollar has stabilized since last
year, buoyed by the Federal Reserve's series of interest-rate increases, which effectively takes greenbacks
out of circulation and increases their value.
Commodities are traded globally in dollar terms. When the dollar is weak, it takes more of them to buy the
same amount of a commodity, hence prices rise. When the dollar strengthens, it takes fewer dollars to buy
the same amount of commodities, pushing prices down.
The dollar's recent stability has left most individual commodities free to fluctuate based on straightforward
supply-and-demand concerns. Many analysts expect that to continue at least until sometime in the second
half of this year, when the U.S. central bank is expected to be done with its rate increases, which could lead
to a new round of dollar weakness and broader strength in commodities.
"Over the long haul, there's definitely such a thing as movement of commodities together, especially when
they're affected by the dollar, or inflation, or both," said Richard W. Asplund, chief economist for the
Commodity Research Bureau. "For now, though, we're not seeing any of that, and there's no consistent
pattern among the index components."
Investment demand has fueled the bull market for commodities, as individuals sought an alternative to the
lackluster stock returns of the past two years. That could change, especially if the first-quarter advances in
stocks continue through the year. In a report summing up the first quarter, Brian Singer, chief investment
officer in the U.S. for the UBS Global Asset Management arm of Swiss bank UBS AG, cautions against using
commodities as an inflation hedge, or to diversify. "The emperor has no clothes," he wrote, noting that there
is no guarantee that commodities will outperform other investments.
If such glum advice from Wall Street professionals becomes more commonplace in the second quarter, that
would help keep commodity prices in check. In particular, hedge funds and other investors have flocked to
broad-based commodity plays in recent years, using natural resources purely as a financial bet rather than
because of a physical need for such items.

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There are, however, a relatively small number of Wall Street traders and firms willing to make big bets on
individual commodities and they are hardly in a downbeat mood these days.
George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures, a New York commodity brokerage
house, said precious metals prices have soared, and could continue to do so in the coming months, because
of a strengthening relationship with crude oil.
He said gold tends to benefit from the same geopolitical crises that have driven up the price of crude oil.
Gold typically is considered an investor haven during times of uncertainty because it can be used as an
alternative, widely accepted store of value when people lose confidence in "paper assets" such as currencies
and stocks. Oil prices, meanwhile, rise in response to any hint that supply might be disrupted.
"By the end of any given week, if energy is up, gold is up," Mr. Gero said. "They've pretty much kept pace
like that since November."
In the first quarter, gold-futures prices rose nearly 13%%, or $64.70, to $581.80 a troy ounce, near a 25-year
high on Nymex's Comex division.
Silver contracts fared even better, up nearly 31%, or $2.70, to $11.52 a troy ounce, near a 22-year high.
Analysts say silver's rally was partly a move in sympathy with gold. Late in the quarter silver also benefited
from the expected launch of an exchange-traded fund linked to the metal, which would require buying to
cover the shares sold to investors.
Agriculture markets have been mixed. In particular, live-cattle prices have been weak because of ample
supplies and selling by speculative funds. The price of futures contracts plunged nearly 20%, or 18.1 cents, to
74.35 cents a pound on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange in the first quarter.
To a certain extent, livestock traders' woes spilled over to the grain markets, since such crops are used as
feed for animals. Futures on wheat and corn rose, but soybean contracts fell 5.1%, or 31 cents, to $5.07 a
bushel at the Chicago Board of Trade.
Looking ahead, Ethan Harris, chief U.S. economist at New York investment bank Lehman Brothers Holdings
Inc., said he expects little movement in broad commodity indexes for at least the next few months. He said
that should at least keep at bay the inflation fears that a continued rise in commodity prices could bring.
"Flat is good," Mr. Harris said. "Even though prices at are quite high levels, it will be a bit of a relief to a global
economy not to deal with continued rises."

Page 115 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Rallies in Silver, Gold May Be Tied To Debt Concerns
Commodities Report
Rallies in Silver, Gold May Be Tied To Debt Concerns
By Spencer Jakab
722 words
3 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Judging by the big moves in global financial and commodity markets last week, it would be easy to conclude
that a serious fear of inflation had taken hold.
Gold -- the classic hedge against inflation -- hit a 25-year high, while silver surged to a 22-year peak. Then
there was the selloff in Treasurys, with the 10-year yield hitting 4.88%, its highest point since before the
Federal Reserve started raising interest rates in June 2004. Copper and zinc hit all-time highs, crude-oil
futures topped $67 a barrel, up over 10% year-to-date, and the dollar fell against many key counterparts.
"The bets out there in the marketplace are interconnected," said Bart Melek, senior economist at BMO Nesbitt
Burns in Toronto.
Investors took profits in many of these markets by Friday, but the confluence of moves -- and the
long-running rally in such key commodities as gold, silver and oil -- has begged the question of whether
there's a growth or inflation common denominator.
At face value, renewed inflation fears appear to be the most logical explanation. During inflationary periods,
hard assets are considered a repository of value while investors demand higher yields in long-term bonds.
But some economists see a major shift in global fund flows as the real culprit and say that there is more to
come.
"You have these people who think commodity booms are inflationary, [but] I'm not so sure," said Anais Faraj,
global strategist at investment bank Nomura in London. "Consumer-price inflation is quite low, wage
pressures are quite low."
Instead, he said, the dislocations in these asset classes may be linked to a fear that the tide of cheap
financing provided by Asian savers in recent years to net borrowers such as the U.S. may be slowing or even
reversing, posing a risk to assets in large debtor nations and meaning potentially more gains for hard assets
such as certain commodities.
"What's happening in Japan is that, for the first time in eight years, monetary policy is about to get tighter,"
he said. "They've really been injecting a tremendous amount of money into the world financial system...and
that money's coming home now."
The U.S., with its massive current-account deficit, is now soaking up two-thirds of global savings in order to
finance its consumption. Much of this "wall of money," as some traders describe it -- $3 billion each business
day -- is parked in Treasurys and other debt securities. Mr. Melek suggested that the flight to gold and other
safe-haven investments might be a response to fears that the dollar could come under pressure from
reduced foreign willingness to plug the gap.
"Perhaps it could be difficult to fund this puppy, and when people are buying gold, that's what they're hedging
against," he said.
There are other reasons to believe that the status quo of brisk capital flows into the U.S. may be at risk. The
recent blocking of a deal by a Dubai-based company to operate ports in the U.S. may have sparked fears of a
less-friendly environment for foreign direct investment in general and a possible alienation of Middle
Easterners plowing petrodollars back to their source.
Mr. Melek noted that hedge funds are talking about increasing their direct exposure to hard assets. This
would have a disproportionate effect on commodity prices due to the relative sizes of the markets. U.S. stock
and bond markets are worth trillions of dollars while physical commodities are valued in the hundreds of
billions. He predicts that gold will hit $700 an ounce in the next 18 months. On Friday the price of gold was
$581.80 an ounce.

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The orders of magnitude are vastly different," he said. "Even if you have a small move from other asset
classes, it can have a material impact."
Bill Powers, a hedge-fund manager at Powers Asset Management in Chicago, is bullish on energy-related
equities. "Clearly, oil and gold, similar to the 1970s, do well together," he said. "If the dollar were to weaken
considerably, it would help both of them."
Gains could come disproportionately in areas like agricultural commodities that have benefitted less from
speculative inflows.

Dow Drops 65 Points as Rates Rise, But Nasdaq Hits a New 5-Year High
By E.S. Browning

The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS RESUMED their declines, as surging bond yields pushed interest-rate worries back to the front of
investors' minds.
With the yield of the 10-year Treasury note moving up to 4.857%, its highest level since 2004, and almost the
highest since 2002, investors worried again that rising market interest rates could weigh on consumer
spending and corporate profits. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 65 points, or 0.58%, to 11150.70, still
up 4% this year.
But the Nasdaq Composite Index bucked the trend and edged to a new five-year high, as some technology
stocks rose on hopes that a strong economy will spark purchases of tech gear.
Most stocks were pulled lower, and bond yields were pushed higher, by revised fourth-quarter economic data
that showed that inflation was stronger than thought. The inflation numbers reinforced fears that the Federal
Reserve will continue raising target short-term interest rates longer than investors had been hoping.
Higher rates are bad for stocks, because they raise borrowing costs for consumers and companies. They
make bonds and money-market accounts more-attractive alternatives. Bond prices fall, and yields rise,
because new bonds, with their higher rates, become more attractive than older ones.
"There was just too much for the market to digest" in the day's news, said Todd Leone, head of listed trading
at brokerage firm Cowen &Co. On top of rising bond yields, oil prices rose 70 cents to $67.15, a fresh
two-month high, on continuing supply worries. Gold futures jumped $13.40 to $586.70, a 25-year high, as
the strong economy stimulated a broad range of commodity prices.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.20%, or 2.64 points, to 1300.25, up 4.2% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.75%, or 3.72 points, to 216.32.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.28 billion
shares, as 1,426 stocks rose and 1,874 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 12/32, or $3.75 for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year
bond was down 27/32 to yield 4.895%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 117.34 yen, down from 117.83, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2155 from $1.2024.

Silver Seduces the Crowd, Again --- Anticipation of Launch of ETF Pushes Price to 22-Year Highs, But Is the Metal Near Its Peak?
Commodities Report
Silver Seduces the Crowd, Again --- Anticipation of Launch of ETF Pushes Price to 22-Year Highs, But
Is the Metal Near Its Peak?
By Ann Davis
1,165 words
31 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SILVER IS GETTING superprecious. Now the question is whether small investors could be rushing in just as
the market could turn down.
After hitting a number of 22-year highs in March, the metal this week breached the psychological barrier of
$11 an ounce, hitting prices not seen since September 1983. Yesterday, silver futures settled at $11.618, up
54.50 cents, on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Lately, it isn't a supply crunch or jewelry-demand increase that is making silver dear. Rather, big investors
have been buying in anticipation of the launch of a silver exchange-traded fund from Barclays PLC's Barclays
Global Investors unit.
To back its proposed silver-tracking ETF, which will trade on the American Stock Exchange, Barclays is
planning to buy huge amounts of silver bullion. The anticipation of such a big buyer in the relatively small
silver market, and predictions that investors will pour into the silver ETF as a way to play the commodities
boom, have led to a roughly 28% jump in the price of silver since the Securities and Exchange Commission
sought public comments on the proposed fund Jan. 23.
The rocket ride accelerated last week after the SEC approved a rule change to allow the AmEx to list the
iShares Silver Trust. The only step left is for the SEC to formally green-light the shares, which could come
any day.
Barclays declined to comment, citing a quiet period for the ETF.
There are other reasons silver has been climbing, and its run-up started late last summer, tracking increases
in other precious and industrial metals. Inflation fears have helped push gold to a 25-year high, with Comex
gold futures settling at $586.70 an ounce, up $13.40, or 2.3%, yesterday. Copper and other industrial metals
are also trading at historically high levels.
Prices are on a tear because of the demand for basic materials in Asian economies; the lag in new
exploration when commodities prices were languishing a few years ago; and the lack of new, easy-to-tap
deposits. Even so, analysts are talking of a commodities supercycle, with even bigger price increases ahead,
sweeping silver up along with the rest.
As a precious metal with industrial uses, silver bridges the gap between gold and industrial commodities. It is
increasingly popular as a "safe" investment, and hoarding of silver by Middle Eastern and other investors is
on the rise, metal watchers say. Silver is used in photography, a declining market as digital photography
overtakes film, but it is also used in electrical applications, batteries and a growing number of antibacterial
applications.
The new silver ETF -- like other ETFs, similar to a mutual fund, but traded on an exchange -- gives investors
a mechanism to participate in the silver market. And analysts expect pension funds and other big traders
could use it, not just small investors. There aren't many publicly traded mining companies that focus purely on
silver, in part because it is a byproduct of copper and other metals mining. And getting exposure to silver via
coins and bullion is cumbersome, while speculating in commodities futures is considered risky.
If the advent of gold ETFs during the past few years is any predictor, it could create more upward pressure
on silver, pushing up the price of everything from silverware to jewelry to industrial parts. "Think of it as a new
use for silver that's just been invented. That use happens to be . . . investment demand," says silver
forecaster Jeffrey Christian of CPM Group, a precious-metals research and consulting company in New York.
For the little guy, the trick is timing. Once the ETF hits the market, will there still be upside?

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"There is some truth that when you start to get new launches for small investors to take advantage of, it's a
potential sign of a top in the market," says Michael Cuggino, president and portfolio manager of the
Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds, which invest in metals as part of a diversified strategy. But he adds that
an investor looking for diversification "should have some gold and silver in their portfolio," and commodities
are going through a "multiyear, supply-demand mismatch."
Silver's recent rise means that it takes fewer ounces of the white metal to buy an ounce of gold. This
gold-to-silver ratio is now around 50 to 1, lower than it has been in a couple of years. Some traders believe
silver's recent rise means it could break away from a pattern of simply tracking gold and scale new heights.
Leonard Kaplan, president of commodities brokerage firm Prospector Asset Management in Evanston, Ill.,
says both metals are overpriced and, eventually, "it's going to end really badly."
Other skeptics say the effect of the ETF is baked into the price. In Barclays's prospectus for its iShares Silver
Trust, in which it outlined risk factors, it warned that enthusiastic receipt of the shares could temporarily push
up the price of silver "that will subside after the initial rush comes to an end." Indeed, in Barclays's weekly
commodities overview report published March 27, its analysts forecast that silver will trade at $8.60 in the
fourth quarter of 2006. Deutsche Bank is forecasting a 2007 price for silver of $10.31.
Investors bullish on silver say Wall Street tends to be a conservative forecaster of commodities prices,
because brokerage firms tend to focus more on the stock market. Rising commodities prices can damp
enthusiasm for equities.
Richard W. Radez, a portfolio manager who manages investments in the mining sector for David A. Noyes
&Co. in Chicago, predicts much higher prices, saying, "There's very little pockets of silver left for the world to
have." He add: "You've got 9,000 hedge funds . . . looking for someplace to put their funds. There's nothing
going on in the system right now that's not bullish for the commodities sector."
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE: Futures on the Nymex topped $67 a barrel for the first time since early February, as gasoline rallied
again and tensions over Iran's nuclear program intensified. Iran said it planned to launch a week of military
exercises, according to the top naval commander. The announcement came after the U.N. Security Council
voted unanimously to demand that Iran suspend nuclear enrichment. The May crude contract jumped 70
cents, or 1.1%, to settle at $67.15 a barrel.
COFFEE: Prices on the New York Board of Trade rose more than five cents a pound amid gains in other
commodity markets and tight supplies of beans from top-producer Brazil. Some traders took profits late in the
session, though, which tempered the gains. The May contract closed up 4.25 cents at $1.0860 a pound.

Dow Industrials Rally 61 Points On Rate Rethink
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Rally 61 Points On Rate Rethink
By E.S. Browning
508 words
30 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS RETHOUGHT some of their interest-rate worries, and stocks recouped a big part of Tuesday's
losses.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rebounded 61.16 points, or 0.55%, to 11215.70, up 4.6% since the year
began. On Tuesday, the blue chips had fallen 95.57 points after Federal Reserve officials ended their policy
meeting without hinting at any plans to stop raising interest rates.
If the Dow holds on to the lion's share of the quarter's gains today and tomorrow, it will turn in its strongest
first-quarter advance since 1999 -- and its first gain of any kind for the year's opening quarter since 2002.
Investors also plowed into commodities, pushing gold futures to a 25-year high, up $6.40 at $573.00 an
ounce.
Yesterday's advance was led by technology and industrial stocks that would benefit from a strong economy.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by technology stocks, finished at a five-year high of 2337.78, up
1.5%, or 33.32 points. It more than recovered Tuesday's 11.12-point decline and is up 6% for the quarter.
Some analysts found the day's strength surprising, since it came as crude-oil futures pushed up 38 cents to
$66.45, their highest finish in almost two months. Bond prices continued to fall, pushing the yield of the
10-year Treasury note up to 4.808%, its highest level since June 2004. The 10-year yield is a benchmark for
some other market interest rates, including certain mortgage rates. A higher yield augurs poorly for
consumer spending.
The stock strength reflected hope that the economy will remain robust despite rising interest rates. Analysts
project double-digit profit gains for companies in the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index during every quarter
this year, and some high-profile companies have offered optimistic projections of first-quarter performance.
But the market strength was somewhat artificial: Some money managers whose monthly accounts aren't
supposed to show uninvested cash were making end-of-month purchases.
"A fair amount of it was money getting put to work at the end of the first quarter," said Todd Clark, director of
stock trading at San Francisco money-management and brokerage firm Nollenberger Capital Partners.
People took advantage of Tuesday's pullback to grab some recent big gainers before the month ended, he
said.
The S&P 500 rose 0.8%, or 9.66 points, to 1302.89, up 4.4% for 2006. The Russell 2000 small-stock index
rose 1.7% to a record, 764.17, up 13.5% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose and bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 5/32, or $1.56 for each $1,000 invested. The
30-year bond was down 20/32 to yield 4.840%.
The dollar weakened, to 117.83 yen, down from 117.98; the euro rose to $1.2024 from $1.2009.

The session was subdued as investors awaited today's interest-rate decision from the Federal Reserve
Board, though some strategists said there was more behind the languid tone.
"I looked at today as kind of a nonevent," said David Klaskin, chief investment officer at Oak Ridge
Investments. "The Fed meeting was part of it but I really think there is more overall lethargy by investors.
There's a tug-of-war over earnings being potentially weak while the stock market is still trading at a
reasonable valuation."
Aluminum producer Alcoa was the highest percentage gainer on the DJIA, rising 50 cents, or 1.7%, to
$30.33. Citigroup substantially lifted its commodity-price forecasts for copper and nickel and modestly
increased them for aluminum and gold. Other beneficiaries of the call were Freeport-McMoRan Copper
&Gold, gaining 2.50, or 4.7%, to 56.19; and copper producer Phelps Dodge, advancing 1.47, or 2%, to 76.61.
GM shares rose 28 cents, or 1.2%, to 22.93. The auto maker was said to be benefitting from progress in
labor-contract negotiations at its biggest supplier, Delphi, that could avert a strike.

underperform, saying that by year end the company's manufacturing muscle will drive a competitive
advantage over rival Advanced Micro Devices. Advanced Micro Devices dropped 80 cents, or 2.2%, to 35.33.

lowered its rating for both orthopedic-products makers' stocks to neutral from overweight, citing a decelerating
growth environment with an absence of near-term catalysts.

sessions. The buying started Thursday, when Bear Stearns upgraded the telecommunications-service
provider's stock, citing an improved operating environment and the benefit of industry consolidation over the
next several years.

long-awaited investor presentation to discuss plans for extricating the advertising giant from the morass it has
been stuck in for years. Persistent accounting problems and a steady stream of client losses at certain units
have contributed to hundreds of millions of dollars in losses in recent years.
Time Warner Telecom (Nasdaq) gained 1.22, or 7.8%, to 16.87. Morgan Stanley upgraded shares to
overweight from equal weight, saying after years of fundamental and financial challenges, the outlook for the
company has improved dramatically. The rise followed a 7% advance Friday, when the telecom company
priced a secondary public offering.

fiscal second-quarter earnings rose 7% despite a milder flu season that hampered sales.
Circuit City Stores rose 39 cents, or 1.6%, to 24.64. Lehman Brothers upgraded shares of the electronics
retailer to equal weight from underweight, citing strong industry trends and "vastly improved" fundamentals.

Monday's Markets
Blue Chips Pull Back in Cautious Trading
By E.S. Browning
481 words
21 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FOLLOWING ITS BIGGEST weekly gain in two months, the Dow Jones Industrial Average edged backward,
as investors awaited an evening speech on interest rates by the Federal Reserve's new chairman, Ben
Bernanke.
As analysts speculated about whether Mr. Bernanke would use his speech to the Economic Club of New York
to hint at when rate increases might end, the Dow Jones Industrial Average inched down 5.12 points, or
0.05%, to 11274.53, little changed from Friday's 4 1/2-year high. It is up 5.2% this year. Trading volume was
below average.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index also pulled back from last week's 4 1/2-year highs, though the Nasdaq
Composite Index managed a small gain.
"People were just kind of waiting to hear" Mr. Bernanke's interest-rate comments, said Mike McDonald, a
trader at Boston-based brokerage firm Canaccord Adams. An end to rate hikes soon would help stocks by
holding down costs for businesses and consumers.
The index of leading indicators declined in February, the Conference Board announced, suggesting that
economic growth may be slowing. Slower growth makes it easier for the Fed to stop raising rates.
Treasury-bond prices rose slightly, pushing yields lower, on hopes for some kind of signal from Mr. Bernanke.
Investors also were expecting that today's report on February wholesale prices would show a decline, to go
with the very small rise in February consumer prices announced last week. Tame inflation reduces fears of
an overheating economy, which makes it easier for the Fed to stop raising rates.
Oracle announced strong quarterly profits, but, in a sign of investors' rising expectations, it wasn't enough to
keep the stock from falling 3.1% in after-hours trading.
The Nasdaq composite rose 0.33%, or 7.63 points, to 2314.11, up 4.9% this year. The S&P 500 fell 0.17%, or
2.17 points, to 1305.08, up 4.6% this year.
Crude-oil futures sagged $2.35 to $60.42 a barrel on signs that supplies remain strong. Gold, however,
continued its rebound, up $1.10 to $555.20 a troy ounce.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.39%, or 0.84 point, to another record, 214.85.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed; bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield down to 4.658%. The 30-year bond was up 10/32 to yield 4.699%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 116.21 yen, up from 115.82, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2170 from $1.2195.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Chinese Investment
In Fixed Assets
Continues to Surge
BEIJING -- China's urban fixed-asset investment in January and February rose 26.6% from the same period a
year earlier to 529.4 billion yuan ($65.9 billion), the National Bureau of Statistics said.
Beijing's national fixed-asset investment growth target this year is 18%, after a 25.7% rise last year. Urban
fixed-asset investment makes up a large portion of national fixed-asset investment.
Some economists say Beijing will find it tough to ensure economic growth that is driven more by consumption
and less by investment. "This is not going to help rebalance growth," said HSBC economist Qu Hongbin in
Hong Kong.
Fixed-asset investment growth remains around 26%, while retail-sales growth continues to hover around
12%. Central-bank money-supply data issued Wednesday showed total loans up 13.4% at the end of
February, compared with the same period a year earlier. Curbing the oversupply of credit has been one of
Beijing's main tools to try to bring growth to more sustainable levels.
-- J.R. Wu and Terence Poon
BRIEFLY:
European Union governments agreed to let the European Commission impose duties on some Chinese- and
Vietnamese-made shoes it says are selling at artificially low prices. Plans call for duties to start next month
and top out at nearly 20% by fall. Some sports shoes and children's shoes are exempted.
Security Officers Die
In Gold-Mine Protest
In Eastern Indonesia
JAYAPURA, Indonesia -- Four security officers were bludgeoned to death by protesters demanding the
closure of a U.S.-owned gold mine in eastern Indonesia, officials said. Dozens were wounded in the clashes,
some seriously.
It was the most violent in a series of demonstrations against Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold Inc.'s mine in
Papua province, 2,300 miles east of Jakarta and home to a decades-long separatist rebellion.
Hundreds of protesters blockaded the road outside a university in the provincial capital yesterday, demanding
that the government close the giant gold mine, located about 300 miles southwest of Jayapura. Protesters
claimed the mine had brought little benefit to the local community.
Riot police tried to break up the rally with tear gas and baton charges, but the protesters, hurling rocks,
refused to move. Police Lt. Col. Paulus Waterpauw said some of the demonstrators bludgeoned three
policemen and a soldier to death with rocks and knives. Hospitals said at least 19 police and eight protesters
were injured. Some had gunshot wounds, doctors said; security forces denied using live ammunition.
Freeport says it pays millions of dollars in taxes each year and funds scores of community projects close to
the mine. Indonesian President Susilo Bambang Yudhoyono said that demands to close the mine were

Stocks Approach a 6-Year High --- Dow Continues Its Strong Run As Fears of Rate Increases Ease; Treasury Bonds Surge Higher
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Approach a 6-Year High --- Dow Continues Its Strong Run As Fears of Rate Increases Ease;
Treasury Bonds Surge Higher
By E.S. Browning
916 words
17 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MAJOR STOCK INDEXES rose to 4 1/2-year highs for a third consecutive day, as benign inflation data
reinforced hopes for a "Goldilocks" economy with steady growth, tame prices and moderate interest rates.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, up 133 points in the previous two days, tacked on another 43.47 points,
or 0.39%, to finish at 11253.24, up 5% this year. The blue chips now are at their highest level since May
2001, less than 85 points from a six-year high and 470 points from the record 11722.98 hit in January 2000.
Treasury-bond prices surged higher, celebrating the government's positive report on February
consumer-price inflation, and the higher prices pushed bond yields down sharply. The yield of the bellwether
10-year Treasury note fell to 4.642% from 4.733%, the largest decline since June 2005.
Not all the day's news was positive, however, as computer-chip stocks fell heavily, pulling the technology
sector and the Nasdaq Composite Index down. Some investors were worried that the recent stock gains have
gone too far, too fast, and they have been selling some stocks that benefit most from economic growth, or
that seem at risk of a pullback. The Dow Jones Transportation Average, which had been at a record, also
declined.
But most stocks rose, powered by news that the consumer-price index gained just 0.1% in February, and that
"core" prices, excluding volatile food and energy costs, also were up only 0.1%. It was the latest sign that the
economy isn't heating up despite high oil prices, which reduces pressure on the Federal Reserve to raise
short-term interest rates.
"We had a very good piece of news that was unexpected," said Alfred Kugel, chief investment strategist at
Chicago investment manager Atlantic Trust/Stein Roe. Investors had feared that the Fed might continue
raising rates for some months to come, but in recent days, "all of a sudden, the consensus has shifted," Mr.
Kugel said, with more investors betting that the Fed will be done raising rates after one or two more
increases.
The prospect that inflation could remain under control also removes the upward pressure on longer-term
rates, such as bond yields, which affect other market rates including some mortgage rates. Lower interest
rates reduce costs for consumers and businesses alike.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.18%, or 2.31 points, to 1305.33, up 4.6% for 2006. It, too,
is at its highest since May 2001, but is well short of its record 1527.46, hit in March 2000.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index rose to yet another record, up 0.11% at 743.79.
The Labor Department reported new unemployment claims were higher than expected last week, which was
bad news for workers but which further eased worries that the economy could be overheating.
Stocks also got a boost from news that a third Wall Street investment bank, Bear Stearns, posted record
quarterly profits. Goldman Sachs and Lehman Brothers released record earnings earlier in the week.
Complicating stock performance, the end of this week marks a trading situation known as "quadruple
witching" -- which is when contracts expire in four stock-related markets: options on stocks and stock indexes,
as well as futures on both. As traders buy and sell stocks to balance exposure to options and futures, trading
volume can rise and stocks -- especially Nasdaq stocks -- can become volatile. Yesterday, the Nasdaq
composite fell 0.53%, or 12.28 points, to 2299.56, still up 4.3% this year. It is less than half way back to its
March 2000 high of 5048.62, however.
