Page 142 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The markets were OK overall, but there were a lot of undercurrents that suggested somewhat stormier
weather below the surface," said Michael Panzner, head of sales trading at Rabo Securities USA. "People
are getting out of some of the things that had been strong performers," he said, including steel stocks,
biotechnology stocks and one-time technology leaders such as Apple Computer.
Also mitigating the optimism was the sight of oil futures rising $1.41 to $63.58 a barrel. Gold, which tends to

Good Corporate, Economic Reports Boost the Dow Industrials Past 11200
Wednesday's Markets
Good Corporate, Economic Reports Boost the Dow Industrials Past 11200
By E.S. Browning
555 words
16 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER HITTING 4 1/2-year highs on Tuesday, stock indexes extended the gains as economic and corporate
reports pointed to continuing growth with only moderate inflation.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average broke 11200 for the first time since May 2001, rising 58.43 points, or
0.52%, to 11209.77. It is up 4.6% this year -- but 513.21 points below its January 2000 record.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index confounded doubters and surpassed 1300, also for the first time since
May 2001. It was up 0.43%, or 5.54 points, to 1303.02 -- ahead 4.4% for 2006.
Most of the gains came after the Federal Reserve released its "beige book" report indicating steady economic
growth with little consumer inflation. Stocks benefited from Lehman Brothers' record quarterly profits, which
came a day after a similar earnings report from Goldman Sachs, and from boosted profit forecasts by Union
Pacific, DuPont and Illinois Tool Works.
Investors also were pleased to see crude-oil futures fall 93 cents to $62.17, as the government's weekly
report indicated stronger-than-expected supplies.
But there still were signs of caution. Bond yields, which had plunged on Tuesday, turned higher again, with
the yield of the bellwether 10-year Treasury note recouping almost half of its previous-day's decline. The
10-year yield affects other market rates, including some mortgage rates, and higher yields raise costs for
business and consumers.
Investors continued to debate how many more times the Fed will raise short-term interest rates as it tries to
prevent inflation. Before the beige book hit, some investors were shocked to see another Fed report, on
manufacturing in the New York region, come in much stronger than expected, with employment up sharply.
That fueled wage-inflation fears, but the beige book helped suppress them.
"Sustained growth, manageable inflation and bond yields under 5%. You can't get a much better scenario
than that," said Tim Smalls, head of U.S. stock trading at Greenwich, Conn., brokerage firm Execution.
Gold continued its recent rebound, rising $1.40 to $553.10 a troy ounce.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.69%, or 15.94 points, to 2311.84. It is up 4.8% and about 20 points
short of its own five-year high.
The Dow Jones Transportation Average rose 2.16% to a record high of 4591.76. The Russell 2000
small-stock index also hit a record, up 0.93% at 742.94.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.43%, or 0.90 point, to 212.08, another record.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.63 billion shares traded, 2,158 stocks rose, 1,156 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.733%. The 30-year bond was down 23/32 to yield 4.756%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 117.26 yen, down from 117.54, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2073 from $1.2015.

Dow Hits New 4 1/2-Year High as Yields Drop

Dow Hits New 4 1/2-Year High as Yields Drop
By E.S. Browning
688 words
15 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TREASURY-BOND YIELDS ended their relentless rise and pulled back sharply, helping boost the Dow Jones
Industrial Average to a new 4 1/2-year high.
Fears that financial stocks would suffer from higher interest rates were allayed by strong quarterly profits
from Wall Street firm Goldman Sachs Group, and stocks in that sector helped lead the rally. Higher rates
increase banks and other financial companies' cost of obtaining money.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 75.32 points, or 0.68%, to 11151.34, its highest finish since June
2001. The blue-chip index is up 4% so far this year.
The broader Standard &Poor's 500-stock index also finished at its highest level in 4 1/2 years, up 1.04%, or
13.35 points, at 1297.48. It recorded its second-largest percentage gain of 2006 and is up 3.9% this year.
Overseas stocks also hit a record high, in dollar terms.
Rising bond prices pushed down the yield of the 10-year Treasury note to 4.700% from 4.773%, the sharpest
one-day decline in three months. The 10-year yield still is above the range in which it traded for the previous
18 months, however.
The bond-yield pullback was partly a natural correction after three weeks of gains. But stocks and bonds also
were reacting to news that retail sales fell a bit last month, creating hopes that economic growth will cool
enough that the Federal Reserve will stop raising interest rates before long.
"We are back to believing that the Fed is closer to finishing the rate-raising program," said Andy Brooks, head
of stock trading at Baltimore money-management group T. Rowe Price. Lower rates hold down costs for
businesses and consumers, and help stocks by making bonds a less-attractive alternative.
Investors looked past a $1.33 rise in oil futures to $63.10 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, a
two-day increase of 5.2%, the biggest such percentage gain since August. Gold jumped $5.60 to $551.70 a
troy ounce.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 1.27%, or 28.87 points, to 2295.90, up 4.1% this year but short of its
recent highs.
Stocks that benefit from low interest rates and continued economic growth, such as technology stocks,
industrial stocks and financial stocks, helped lead the day's rally. That reflected hopes for what investors call
a Goldilocks economy, with moderate growth and neither recession nor serious inflation.
Since summer, investor expectations for the economy and interest rates have yo-yoed between that kind of
hope and deep fear. In recent days, investors have wrung their hands over signs that central banks are going
to raise interest rates in Europe, Japan and the U.S. at once. That would mark the end of easy-money
government policies that have funneled cash into financial markets and helped spur growth for years.
Those fears had been pushing bond yields higher, raising the risk that cash would become increasingly
expensive and profits would suffer. The sight of yields suddenly diving yesterday allayed some of the
interest-rate concerns. Many investors mow will want to see whether the decline in yields was a one-day
wonder or something more lasting.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.84%, or 1.75 points, to 211.18, a record close for that index.
In major U.S. market action:

861 fell.

bond was up 27/32, pushing the yield down to 4.710%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 117.54 yen, down from 118.86, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2015 from $1.1959.

WSJ(3/15) Once Feared, Credit Bureaus Cozy Up To Consumers
WSJ(3/15) Once Feared, Credit Bureaus Cozy Up To Consumers
1,436 words
15 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
(From THE WALL STREET JOURNAL)
By Christopher Conkey
FOR MANY YEARS, consumers viewed the nation's three big credit bureaus as shadowy and vaguely
threatening, about as friendly as the IRS or a debt-collection agent. The credit bureaus didn't much care; they
saw consumers as raw material -- fodder for credit reports and marketing lists that they'd sell to banks,
insurers and mortgage lenders.
Now, that relationship is getting a major makeover. The three firms -- whom the government fined a total of
$2.5 million for failing to answer consumers' phone calls in 2000 -- are going out of their way to interact with
consumers they once shunned, pitching them an ever expanding array of credit-related products. The
about-face gives Equifax Inc., Experian and TransUnion LLC a fast-growing revenue stream to supplement
their traditional focus on business clients, and offers consumers new tools -- for a price -- to help them
demystify the credit system, fight fraud and sometimes save money.
For house hunters, TransUnion's $9.95 KnowYourLoanRate simulator not only gives consumers a credit
report and score, it also analyzes their debt-to-income ratio and breaks down various loan terms. For used
car buyers, Experian's $59.99 AutoCheck with TitleGuard offers 60 days of history checks on cars and title
insurance of as much as $50,000. For those concerned about fraud, Equifax's Credit Watch Gold with 3-in-1
Monitoring, priced at $129.95 per year, provides unlimited access to each of their three major credit reports
and sends email alerts whenever changes occur, such as unpaid balances or the appearance of suspicious
new accounts.
"The consumer reporting agencies used to view [consumer interaction] as kind of an expense and onerous
and it wasn't anything they were too interested in," said John Danaher, the president of TrueCredit,
TransUnion's consumer division. "We want that experience to be a positive one. This is a business now."
Yesterday, the three bureaus unveiled a common approach to credit scores, called VantageScore that, they
said, will make life simpler for consumers -- for a fee. (See related article, Page D4.) Until now, each of the
three firms used a different formula to devise a credit score.
The bureaus hope customers will turn to these services when they are securing big loans or buying
expensive goods on credit. But fears of identity theft are also creating business. All three companies say their
most expensive credit-monitoring services, which use email alerts to help detect fraud, are among their best
sellers. "Don't Let Identity Thieves Ruin Your Credit," reads the pitch for Equifax's Credit Watch Gold service
on its Web site. In the recent movie "Firewall," the filmmakers had Harrison Ford's character discover he's
been targeted by an identity thief by monitoring his Equifax credit report.
Atlanta-based Equifax says annual revenues for its "personal solutions" division, which sells consumers credit
scores (used by lenders to assess risk and set interest rates), reports and monitoring services, jumped by
more than 60% to $115 million over the last two years, outpacing the 19% rise in its $800-million-a-year
division that sells credit information and risk-assessment tools to businesses.
Experian, a Costa Mesa, Calif.-based unit of British retail giant GUS PLC., hardly had a consumer division
four years ago; today, its Experian Interactive accounts for nearly 32% of U.S. sales. Closely held
TransUnion, based in Chicago, won't disclose sales figures but says its direct-to-consumer business has
three million customers.
The rush to tap the consumer market is driven by technology, regulation and competition, and is altering the
historically strained relationship between consumers and the credit bureaus. Since their founding in the late
1800s as a way for merchants to keep tabs on delinquent customers, credit-reporting agencies have profited
by quietly gathering personal information about consumers and their financial accounts, analyzing the
information and then selling it to businesses.

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In an economy increasingly based on credit, those tasks are vital. But credit reports can be flawed, and
consumers have little or no control over the system. For years, many consumers would discover the
existence of credit bureaus only when they were turned down for a loan. The bureaus were content to deal
exclusively with business clients, and direct contact with consumers generally took the form of angry phone
calls or letters of dispute.
The first hint of change came in the mid 1980s when TRW Inc., the predecessor to Experian, started
marketing credit reports directly to consumers. Complaints about inaccuracies, however, brought scrutiny,
and by 1991 the company was pressured by state and federal regulators into giving consumers one free
report per year. Several states adopted similar mandates. After a string of public-relations headaches, TRW
eventually sold its consumer business.
By the late 1990s, however, Web use was spreading and start-up sites like ConsumerInfo.com and
TrueCredit.com began selling the bureau's reports to consumers. By 2000, all three credit bureaus had
followed and were making reports available online, although the business had yet to take off. "We knew
enough to know that this thing was headed somewhere," said Don Robert, CEO of Experian since 2002. "We
really didn't know how big it would ultimately be."
In 2002, Experian upped the stakes by purchasing Consumerinfo.com. TransUnion acquired TrueCredit the
same year. In 2003, Congress mandated that consumers be given one free credit report from each of the
three major bureaus once a year. While the industry feared that the law would undercut its new consumer
efforts, it seems instead to have steered new customers to them. With Experian's new products and
marketing, the company's annual consumer credit sales surged to more than $300 million last year from $60
million in 2001, says Mr. Robert.
Learning to embrace the customer hasn't always been easy. Last year, for example, Experian was fined
$950,000 by the Federal Trade Commission after the company was accused of tricking consumers trying to
get a free credit report into signing up for a $79.95 credit-monitoring service. Many consumer advocates say
consumers often end up buying products from the credit bureaus that they don't need, particularly in light of
the recent law granting free reports each year. The San Diego-based Identity Theft Resource Center, for
example, cautions on its Web site: "For all practical purposes you can create your own monitoring service
using free credit reports . . . In other words, order one bureau this month, a different bureau's report in 4
months, and the third one four months later."
There can be benefits to everyday monitoring, though. Jennifer Jones, a 33-year old marketing executive in
Atlanta, has been victimized by identity thieves who have repeatedly written fraudulent checks and opened
bogus accounts in her name across the country. She regularly checks her credit reports for signs of fraud
through Equifax's Score Watch service, which costs $79.95 a year. "Just last week, I got an email from Score
Watch saying there was a change," Ms. Jones said. "It listed a check paid for some utility bill in Boston."
Craig Hudson, a 33-year-old computer consultant in Chicago, uses a TransUnion credit-monitoring service in
a different way. By learning factors that raise and lower scores, Mr. Hudson has slowly been able to track and
improve his scores, which have gone from an average of 540 a year ago to 620 today. He's hoping to get
them closer to 700 so he can secure better rates on a mortgage later this year.
"We don't learn anything in school about managing credit," Mr. Hudson said. "You don't realize how important
it is until you try and do something. . . It's like I'm micromanaging my credit now."
Executives at the major credit bureaus increasingly are marketing their consumer products around major "life
events" like buying a car or house or taking out a student loan. The idea is to convince consumers that by
better managing their credit reports and scores, they can save money on all types of loans and credit lines.
Experian recently bought the comparison-shopper site PriceGrabber.com, and already owns the
loan-comparison site LowerMyBills.com and others designed to help consumers choose between everything
from school programs to furniture. The acquisitions give the company the ability to cross-market credit
products to consumers considering major purchases. "Whenever they have something big going on," Mr.
Robert said, "it involves money."
Document J000000020060315e23f00001

--- Page 154 ---
Page 154 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

Industry Focus

Welcome Back, Japan
Welcome Back, Japan
By David Malpass
1,259 words
14 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Japan's central bank has set the stage for rate hikes later this year by announcing that it could allow the
excess reserves it has been pushing into the banking system to expire. The ever-lurking bears on the global
outlook have taken this as an anti-growth tightening of world liquidity, adding the "end of the yen carry trade"
to their multiyear list of crash forecasts. But as Japan begins lifting its interest rates above 0%, think growth,
not tightness. It's good news, signaling emergence from a painful, 15-year malaise. As the world's
second-largest economy, a growing, rate-hiking Japan is also good news for the global expansion.
Rate hikes from low levels in the U.S. and soon Japan are welcome proof of economic recovery. As borrowed
money becomes more useful, it's natural for rates to rise. Part of the power in the U.S. growth engine comes
from factors Japan can't match -- the U.S. culture of small-business innovation, a growing and flexible labor
force, a more market-based economy, the thrust and timing of the 2003 cut in the tax rates on capital and
labor. But an important cause of the post-2001 U.S. economic success is monetary -- the low interest rates
in 2002-2004, the related decline in the value of the dollar from its deflation-inducing 2001 strength, and then
enough rate hikes in 2005 to hold down inflation expectations. Japan has started down this same monetary
path, and rather than slowing growth, Japan's interest rate increases should act as an accelerant to its
economy, bank lending and corporate profits.
How can higher rates be stimulative? When actual rates aren't rising as fast as the neutral -- or market
clearing -- rate. As Japan grows, demand for loans and the usefulness of money are likely to rise faster than
interest rates. Already, interest rates are below the inflation rate. The yen's recent weakness is one
market-based indicator of this easing. The huge stock of dormant yen currency -- the equivalent of $6,550 per
capita (versus $2,325 in the U.S.) -- will mobilize, becoming more productive, less idle, as rates rise and cash
hoarding declines. As in the U.S., the velocity or turnover rate of Japan's broader money supply should also
begin to increase, fueling growth even as the central bank restrains or even shrinks the quantity of money.
When Japan's interest rates finally rise, perhaps in the third quarter, some very low-yielding investments
may be priced out of the market. But new investments with higher returns are quickly becoming viable as
Japan's growth prospects improve. And with deflation concerns fading, Japan's banks and savers are more
likely to add to (rather than subtract from) the already-vast pool of ready-to-lend global liquidity.
With over $5 trillion of GDP expected in 2006, Japan's comeback is good news for the rest of the world.
Perhaps anticipating the central bank's "all clear" signal, Japan grew at a 5.4% real rate in the fourth quarter
of 2005, bringing full-year growth to 2.7%. Some of the biggest beneficiaries will be its productive neighbors
around the Pacific Rim -- Korea, Taiwan, Canada and the U.S. -- who will feel Japan's factories and service
providers shift their focus toward meeting internal demand rather than theirs and China's. Welcome back,
Japan. You were missed.
Still, Japan faces many challenges. Its baby boom was sharper and earlier than America's, putting greater
strain on its pensions. Meanwhile, Japan's population and labor force are shrinking, making gains in output
and tax receipts increasingly hard to achieve. And Japan's Finance Ministry is planning a tax increase, joining
slow-growing Germany in thinking it can tax itself into fiscal balance.
One of the biggest challenges is the yen itself. While the U.S. runs a current account deficit and capital
account surplus, Japan is in the reverse position. Rather than investing at home, it pours money abroad in
search of faster growth, safe yields and expanding labor forces. It's healthy enough for Japan's aging
population to trade laptops for Treasurys. However, if the yen weakens in the face of low interest rates, the
pace of the outflow may increase. The government is committed to using at least part of its $850 billion in
international reserves to buy some of the excess yen in the event of an abrupt decline. But in a world of
volatile currencies, momentum often wins out. This makes confidence in the central bank as important for
Japan's low-interest-rate recovery as it was in the U.S.
Several factors complicate Japan's rate-hike decisions. The central bank's independence from political
pressure is -- to put it nicely -- less developed than in the U.S., while its politicians are just as enamored of

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
low interest rates. Further, the Ministry of Finance has several reasons for preferring low interest rates and
usually wins arguments with the less politically connected Bank of Japan. MoF can finance Japan's enormous
national debt more cheaply if rates are low. And yen weakness increases the yen value of MoF's international
reserves, reducing Japan's net debt. Sides have already been drawn, with MoF pointing out that low rates
would complement its plan for tax hikes and reminding that it blames the BoJ's 2000 short-lived rate hike,
rather than excessive strength in the value of the yen, for that part of Japan's malaise.
Beyond the positive global growth implications, a more normal Japanese monetary policy heralds the final
step in the world's 35-year buffeting from inflation and deflation. With the departure from the gold standard
in 1971 and the subsequent declines in the value of money around the world, the U.S., Japan, Germany and
others suffered elevated inflation, interest rates and unemployment. To combat this, central banks worked
hard in the 1980s to get ahead of the curve, applying high real interest rates and sounder money to achieve
ever lower inflation. By the late 1980s, however, Japan had gone too far. The strong and still-strengthening
yen had attracted huge capital inflows into Japan, but it undermined the capital structure, which was not
prepared for the yen to strengthen to the point that prices fell. After the 1990 peak, Japan's GDP growth
stalled, with only $4.2 trillion of output in 2003, the same as in 1993 (while U.S. GDP increased from $6.6
trillion to $11 trillion).
Japan's sudden deflationary economic decline in the 1990s contributed to a global growth imbalance.
Investment growth outside the U.S. slowed to the point that, by 1999, nearly half the world's new investment
was in the U.S. This made the U.S. feel good, but it wasn't healthy or sustainable. Similarly, consumption
growth outside the U.S. slowed to a crawl as Japan went into hibernation.
It will take time, but a global balance seems well on its way to being restored. With more and more of the
world's consumption, investment, profits and stock market gains coming from abroad, the U.S. is no longer
the world's sole growth engine. World markets have already applauded the end of the inflation-deflation
shock, but may continue to be pleasantly surprised by the growth, jobs and profit that flow from it.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief economist at Bear Stearns.

Rising Yields And Oil Hold Stocks in Check
Monday's Markets
Rising Yields And Oil Hold Stocks in Check
By E.S. Browning
534 words
14 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OIL PRICES were up and bond yields were up, which pushed investor confidence down and left stocks going
nowhere to start the week.
With the Dow Jones Industrial Average and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index both nearing the 4 1/2-year
highs hit earlier this year, investors turned cautious. The Dow industrials fell 0.32 point, virtually unchanged,
to finish at 11076.02. That is up 3.3% for the year, about 61 points short of the 4 1/2-year high of 11137.17 hit
last month.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.20%, or 2.55 points, to 1284.13. The index has risen 2.9%
in 2006, and is nearing its own recent high of 1294.18.
Early in the day, stocks got a boost from news that North Fork Bancorp. and newspaper chain Knight Ridder
were being acquired, and that a bidding war might break out for the London Stock Exchange. Investors like
takeovers, because they suggest that industry insiders find stocks attractive to buy.
But as the session wore on, most of those gains evaporated in the face of rising bond yields and oil prices.
Higher bond yields push up other market interest rates, including some mortgage rates, and higher oil prices
serve as a tax on consumers and businesses. Both are bad news for profits at most companies.
"There seem to be more negatives around than positives. Interest rates could be going higher. Oil was up,"
said Bob Basel, senior trader at Citigroup's New York brokerage arm.
Some investors worry that the S&P 500 will have trouble breaking through 1300. And the end-of-quarter
preannouncement season is about to heat up, during which companies begin to warn of any bad news that
will be included in their quarterly reports. Given all of that, Mr. Basel said, it was encouraging to see stocks
hold up as well as they did.
Bond prices fell, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up to 4.733%, topping Friday's 21-month high.
Crude oil rose $1.81 to $61.77 a barrel on continuing worries about supplies. Gold, which tends to rise in
times of uncertainty, jumped $6.20 to $546.10 a troy ounce.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.22%, or 4.99 points, to 2267.03, up 2.8% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose
1.22%, or 2.53 points, to 209.43.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.51 billion shares traded, 1,887 stocks rose
and 1,403 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 63 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.773%. The 30-year bond was down 6/32 to yield 4.764%.
The dollar weakened. The currency traded at 118.86 yen, down from 118.98, while the euro rose against the
dollar to $1.1959 from $1.1915.

10 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal

Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE BANK OF JAPAN voted to adopt a reference rate for inflation of between zero and 2%, a move that
could open an era of steady growth for the world's No. 2 economy. The promise of greater transparency
pushed Tokyo stocks up 2.6%, even as the central bank signaled future higher interest rates.

U.S. households' net worth hit a record last quarter as home values rose, but higher interest rates may slow

Gold, Silver Futures Take Tumble --- Jitters About Rate Increases, Weaker Crude-Oil Prices, Trigger Speculative Selling
Commodities Report
Gold, Silver Futures Take Tumble --- Jitters About Rate Increases, Weaker Crude-Oil Prices, Trigger
Speculative Selling
By Allen Sykora
487 words
9 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Weaker oil prices, fears of interest-rate increases and technical factors triggered speculative selling, sending
New York gold and silver futures sharply lower yesterday.
Most-active April gold tumbled $10.20 to $544.30 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. Nearby March lost $10.20 to $542.50.
Most-active May silver futures settled 26.2 cents lower at $9.845 an ounce. Nearby March lost 25.8 cents to
$9.777.
A decline in crude-oil futures on the Nymex pressured metals, analysts said. They also blamed the weakness
on expectations for interest-rate increases in the U.S. and Japan, and recent rate increases by the European
Central Bank.
"Higher rates are not good for the precious metals," said George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital
Markets Global Futures. "It becomes more expensive to hold precious metals, it is a fight against inflation,
and they are an alternative [investment] to precious metals," he said.
Technical chart-based selling occurred as April gold broke through its key 50-day moving average, which
passed around $554.60, A.G. Edwards analyst Dan Vaught said.
Some weakness in silver was blamed on the lack of an announcement from the Securities and Exchange
Commission on a proposed silver exchange-traded fund from Barclays Global Investors. Earlier this month,
traders bid silver higher, betting the ETF was a "done deal," Mr. Vaught said. ETF shares would be similar to
a stock, but would only track movement of the commodity. They would be backed by silver stored on behalf of
the trust, meaning approval should increase investment demand.
"I suspect some folks may be starting to have ideas . . . that the longer the SEC holds off on any decision, it
may be an indication they are going to rule against the ETF," Mr. Vaught said.
Comments on the silver ETF proposal were due Feb. 13, although a decision wasn't expected for at least two
weeks afterward.
Mr. Gero also said that an increase in the minimum margin requirements for Comex silver futures may have
prompted some selling.
In other commodity markets:
COTTON: The China Cotton Association said domestic buyers and U.S. exporters of cotton will soon get to
make deals based on a new trading contract, which was revised to reduce possible disputes arising from
vague contract provisions. China is the world's largest importer of cotton. The Chinese group struck a deal
with two U.S. cotton groups, American Cotton Shippers Association and Amcot.
CRUDE OIL: Futures fell for the third straight session after the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries
left its output ceiling unchanged. Losses were compounded by U.S. data showing a big build in crude
inventories. The April contract settled $1.56 lower at $60.02 a barrel on the Nymex.

Seven Pillars of Folly
Seven Pillars of Folly
By Edward Chancellor
1,419 words
8 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The oil exporters of the Persian Gulf are flush with cash. Some of that money is going towards acquiring
P&O, the British shipping concern, thus sparking off the heated controversy over foreign control of U.S. ports.
This has led people to worry that Arab petrodollars might be scared away from the U.S. In fact, unlike during
the last oil boom of the late 1970s, relatively little of the current Arab oil surplus has been directly invested in
U.S. assets or even deposited in the international banking system. This time much of the oil money has
remained at home where a classic speculative mania is now being played out.
Lawrence of Arabia took the title of his celebrated book from a passage in the Book of Proverbs: "Wisdom
hath builded her house, she hath hewn out her seven pillars." In homage to Lawrence, we identify the seven
pillars of folly upon which the Great Arab Boom has been weakly constructed.
-- The first pillar is liquidity: OPEC members have earned around $1.3 trillion in petrodollars since 1998,
according to the Bank for International Settlements. The extra liquidity injected into the Gulf economies by the
oil price hike since 2002 is estimated at around $300 billion by HSBC. Some of this money has been spent on
building up foreign currency reserves and on the acquisition of foreign companies, such as P&O. Arab
takeovers of European and U.S. firms totaled $30 billion last year. Some money has even been invested in
hedge funds and gold. However, a great deal has stayed in the Gulf region.
This has contributed to an extraordinary explosion of bank credit in Saudi Arabia and its neighbors. Since the
member countries of the Gulf Cooperation Council link their currencies to the U.S. dollar, they have also
enjoyed the Federal Reserve's easy money policy. The Saudi government has recently repaid around $100
billion of outstanding debt, further contributing to domestic liquidity.
The deposit base of Gulf commercial banks has increased by over 60% since 2000, according to a recent
report from Credit Suisse. Bank loans have financed business investment, personal consumption, property
development and stock margin loans, thereby boosting both the economy and asset prices.
-- The second pillar is the new economy: The Gulf economies are growing rapidly, along with corporate
profits. Returns on equity in the region are approaching 20%, calculates Credit Suisse. Saudi Arabia has
recently joined the World Trade Organization. Kuwait is selling off some state-owned businesses. A new era
of permanently high oil prices and perpetual prosperity has been hailed.
The Gulf rulers are seeking to reduce their economies' dependence on oil. This is spurring a massive
investment boom. Dubai is attempting to transform itself into a leading financial center and tourist resort.
Saudi Arabia intends to become a world leader in fertilizer production. A bridge costing $3 billion is proposed
to span the Red Sea. A new economy is coming into being. The current oil boom, unlike former ones, won't
be followed by a bust, say the believers. This time it's different.
-- The third pillar is the stock market: The recent performance of Arab stock markets makes the Nasdaq of the
late 1990s look like a slouch. Since January 2002, the Egyptian, Dubai and Saudi stock markets are up
respectively by over 1,100%, 630% and 600%. Only four years ago, Gulf companies were priced at around
twice book value. Today they trade on an average of 44 times historic earnings and at over eight times book
value. Gulf banks are valued at over nine times book value, according to Credit Suisse.
Sabic, a Saudi conglomerate, is currently ranked among the world's 10 largest companies by market
capitalization. The Saudi stock exchange has a market cap of around $750 billion. That's roughly three times
the country's GDP. By comparison, the U.S. stock market reached a peak of 183% of GDP in March 2000. In
fact, the relative overvaluation of the Saudi stock market is even greater than these figures suggest. Nomura
analyst Tarek Fadlallah points out that as the oil industry remains in state hands, a far smaller fraction of
Saudi economic activity is captured by the stock market than in the U.S.

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- The fourth pillar is an IPO boom: In the late 1980s, the Japanese authorities kindled a speculative mania by
floating telecom giant NTT. In unconscious imitation, the Gulf states have stimulated their mania with
privatizations and IPOs at bargain prices. It is not unknown for stocks to climb 500% on the first day's trading.
Applications for new issues have been oversubscribed by up to 800 times. One IPO in the United Arab
Emirates attracted aggregate subscriptions greater than $100 billion, a larger sum than the UAE's GDP.
-- The fifth pillar is a property boom: Dubai is the fastest-growing city in the world. Hundreds of new buildings
are under construction, including what is planned to be the tallest building ever, the Burj Tower. Cynics point
out that the capping of the world's highest property, from the Empire State Building to the Petronas Towers in
Malaysia, has occasionally in the past coincided with economic crises. Reports suggest that the majority of
new Dubai properties are being acquired for speculative purposes, with only small deposits put down. They
are being flipped in the contemporary Miami manner.
-- The sixth pillar is market inefficiency: Financial information in the Gulf is totally inadequate. The Saudi
megacap conglomerate Sabic attracts no domestic financial analysis, says Nomura's Mr. Fadlallah.
Companies report their results in a rudimentary fashion. It is against the law to sell short overpriced stocks in
the Saudi market. And foreigners' financial sophistication is absent since only Gulf nationals can purchase
Saudi stocks. Instead, speculators operate in an information vacuum in markets reportedly dominated by
insider trading and practiced manipulation.
-- The seventh pillar is the madness of crowds: Newspapers gleefully report stories of police called to protect
banks from overeager IPO subscribers. A Saudi woman is said to have been divorced by her husband for no
reason other than that he'd had lost money in the stock market. Up to two million of the 16 million Saudi
population are said to be playing the market. Interest-free loans are commonly available. Saudi bank foyers
are lined with LCD screens showing stock movements. A local TV station has started to provide stock market
reports. The education minister has warned teachers to stop day-trading at schools. People are quitting their
jobs to trade.
This is a familiar tale of folly, similar in certain aspects to the global technology bubble of the late 1990s. And
like the tech bubble it is set to burst. The current Gulf prosperity is a mirage created by a haze of liquidity. The
Federal Reserve, which inadvertently caused the Arab bubble when it slashed interest rates in 2002, is
currently mopping up that liquidity. The Gulf Arabs are likely to be rudely awoken from their speculative
dreams. In fact, the Arab markets are beginning to crack: Dubai has fallen 40% from its November peak, and
the Saudi market is down by around 12% in the past few days.
There are several implications of the coming Arab crash. Speculative booms lead to capital being
misallocated. Many of today's investments in the Gulf region may appear, in retrospect, as extravagant as
U.S. fiber-optic expenditures in the late 1990s. As for Dubai's desire to become an international financial
center, it is spookily reminiscent of Tokyo's ambition to rival New York and London in the 1980s. Japan's
ambition was shattered by the collapse of its bubble economy.
The political consequences could be more serious. Arab rulers have deliberately encouraged the boom in the
hope that rising asset prices and a strong economy would distract their youthful populations from religious
fundamentalism. This strategy could backfire. History teaches that when speculative bubbles burst and the
public loses large sums, there is normally a political backlash. This was true of the U.S. in the 1930s, and to a
lesser extent in the early 2000s, and of Japan in the 1990s. It's not hard to imagine Islamists capitalizing on a
future bust with denunciations of stock-market gambling. Some of today's young Arab day-traders could well
turn into tomorrow's al Qaeda recruits.
---
Mr. Chancellor is deputy U.S. editor for Breakingviews.com.

Decline in Gold Hurts Great Basin, Eldorado, Apollo
Small-Stock Focus
Decline in Gold Hurts Great Basin, Eldorado, Apollo
By Anjali Cordeiro
614 words
8 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Persisting concerns about higher interest rates acted as a drag on small stocks, which finished lower for the
fourth consecutive session and trailed behind the overall market yesterday.
Selling was broad with 69 of the 100 sectors that Dow Jones tracks ending lower. Commodity stocks and
interest-rate-sensitive sectors were among the worst hit.
Mining stocks stumbled after gold futures fell back as the dollar strengthened in the face of rising Treasury
yields. Eldorado Gold declined five cents, or 1.2%, to $4.30; Apollo Gold slid four cents, or 6.2%, to 61 cents;
and Great Basin Gold lost six cents, or 3.2%, to 1.80, all on the American Stock Exchange.
Real-estate investment trusts also retreated amid interest-rate jitters. REITs tend to pay high dividends and
their stocks look less attractive when interest rates rise. Among small
REITs, Saxon Capital declined 45 cents, or 4.6%, to 9.40 on the NYSE; and Impac Mortgage Holdings lost 12
cents, or 1.5%, to 7.94, also on the NYSE. The pullback in REITs came a day after several members of this
group made robust gains on news that Blackstone Group agreed to buy large cap CarrAmerica Realty.
Overall, the Russell 2000 lost 10.10, or 1.38%, to 721.06. The S&P SmallCap 600 fell 4.61, or 1.23%, to
371.49.
"Productivity data indicated that the economy might be slowing and prices might be going up a little, and that
spooked the market," said David Klaskin, chief investment officer at Oak Ridge Investments. Small caps did
poorly as they tend to have a greater sensitivity to interest-rate fluctuations, because there is some
perception that they could be harder hit by higher rates, he said.
The semiconductor sector was weak after large cap Texas Instruments' first-quarter targets failed to enthuse
investors. Among small stocks, Mindspeed Technologies fell 22 cents, or 6.1%, to 3.38; Semtech declined
1.15, or 5.9%, to 18.37; and LTX lost 40 cents, or 6.8%, to 5.50.
Natural-gas related stocks declined, with the U.S. Energy Information Administration lowering its expectations
for both natural-gas supply and prices for 2006 in its short-term energy outlook. In the small-cap arena, GMX
Resources declined 3.87, or 11%, to 31.71; Carrizo Oil &Gas declined 1.27, or 5.4%, to 22.39; and Gasco
Energy lost 31 cents, or 6.5%, to 4.44 on the Amex.
Krispy Kreme Doughnuts jumped 1.32, or 21%, to 7.71 on the NYSE. The doughnut company named Daryl
Brewster, who was previously president of large cap Kraft's North American snacks and cereals businesses,
as its new chief executive.
MortgageIT Holdings fell 1.34, or 12%, to 9.75 on the NYSE. The New York real- estate investment trust
projected a loss for the first quarter, due to expected losses at its mortgage bank.
Isonics climbed 16 cents, or 10%, to 1.73. The Golden, Colo., provider of products for the homeland-security
and semiconductor markets said it expects revenue growth from its semiconductor segment to exceed 45% in
its fiscal third quarter compared with the second quarter.
Sunrise Senior Living rose 2.74, or 8.3%, to 35.75 on the NYSE. The McLean, Va., provider of assisted-living
services reported fourth-quarter revenue above Wall Street's expectations, aided by acquisitions and
increased resident capacity.
Chipotle Mexican Grill slid 79 cents, or 1.7%, to 45 on the NYSE after several analysts initiated coverage of
the Denver burrito chain with neutral ratings.

Dow Industrials Fall Back Below 11000 As Bond Yields Rise on Inflation Fears
Monday's Markets
Dow Industrials Fall Back Below 11000 As Bond Yields Rise on Inflation Fears
By E.S. Browning
528 words
7 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SURGING BOND YIELDS helped knock the Dow Jones Industrial Average back below 11000.
Amid worries that higher yields will slow the economy and make bonds attractive alternatives to stocks, the
blue-chip average fell 63 points, or 0.57%, to 10958.59, the lowest finish since Feb. 13. The industrial
average is up 2.2% this year.
Treasury-bond prices fell, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up to 4.742%, its highest level since
June 2004.
Yields are rising partly on fears that inflation threats will make the Federal Reserve raise short-term interest
rates. Some bond investors were betting that yields, which have been exceptionally low and have been
wrongly forecast to move higher for years, now finally may do so. Higher interest rates, both in the form of
Fed increases and higher bond yields, raise the cost of borrowing for businesses and consumers. The rates
on some fixed-rate mortgages, for example, are based on Treasury yields.
Higher bond yields also make bonds a more attractive prospect for retirees and others who want investments
to provide regular income. Such people could shift money out of stocks.
"Let's face it, we have bond yields at a level we haven't seen in some time," said Tim Smalls, head of U.S.
stock trading at Greenwich, Conn., brokerage firm Execution LLC. "This was the day where you sit there and
say, `we are crossing over a threshold.' "
The day's actual economic data were conflicting, with January factory orders stronger than expected but the
January index of pending home sales weak.
The stock declines remained moderate, and there was good news in other markets. Oil prices fell $1.26 to
$62.41 a barrel on hopes that the nuclear confrontation with Iran won't affect its oil exports. Members of the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries indicated they won't try to cut production at their next meeting.
Gold, to which investors turn in times of anxiety, slumped $11.10 to $554.90 an ounce, the sharpest decline
in three weeks.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.7%, or 8.97 points, to 1278.26, up 2.4% this year. The Nasdaq
Composite Index declined 0.72%, or 16.57 points, to 2286.03, ahead 3.7% for 2006.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.16%, or 0.34 point, to 209.27.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.65 billion shares traded, 1,025 stocks rose and 2,312 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 14/32, or $4.38 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.742%. The 30-year bond was down 1 2/32 to yield 4.728%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 117.58 yen, up from 116.39, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2020 from $1.2037.

Missed Chance: In Latin America, Commodities Boom Has Unlikely Fallout --- Many Mayors Flub Opportunity To Help the Region's Poor; Populists...
Missed Chance: In Latin America, Commodities Boom Has Unlikely Fallout --- Many Mayors Flub
Opportunity To Help the Region's Poor; Populists See an Opening --- A Bull Ring Rises in Peru
By Matt Moffett
2,077 words
6 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LA ENCANADA, Peru -- When gold prices started rallying briskly a few years ago, residents of this
impoverished Andean mining town hoped for things like improved schools or sewage treatment. That is
because Peru's federal government had begun allocating a big share of natural resource revenue to local
administrations.
But people in La Encanada, where motorists share the streets with sheep herders, didn't count on the
peculiar spending priorities of the town's flamboyant mayor, Fidel Valera Sanchez. He is building a
15,000-seat soccer stadium, revamping a sports arena and preparing the groundwork for a 10-story municipal
hotel -- which would be by far the tallest building in town. A sign in the big municipal bull ring he is
constructing reads "The great men like Fidel Valera are remembered by their works."
A China-fueled commodities boom is pouring money into state coffers across Latin America. With growing
grass-roots demand for some of the wealth, many national governments are sending an unprecedented
portion to local authorities, who presumably know where it is most urgently needed. In many places, it isn't
working the way it was supposed to.
"The gold is being trucked out, but the benefits aren't coming back to the community," says Absalon Salazar
Huayhua, a small farmer and rancher in Encanada.
That is contributing to a surge of support for populist candidates across the region, as local officials fail to
meet the rising expectations of the poor. Mr. Salazar is organizing a voting drive for a fiery populist
presidential candidate, Ollanta Humala, an ex-military officer who has come out of nowhere to grab second
place in polls anticipating next month's election.
This year Peru has earmarked $800 million in taxes paid by mining and energy companies for town halls in
the mountains and jungles where the resources are produced. In a country with a long tradition of strong
central government, local and regional leaders were handed a once-in-a-lifetime opportunity to help the half
of Peru's population that earns less than $2 a day. But many of them are blowing it.
Some of the revenue is being siphoned off through fraud, federal officials say. Despite desperate social
needs, other leaders can't make up their minds what to do with the cash influx. More than 30% of the money
is sitting idle in bank accounts, Peru's economy ministry says. "The money coming in has completely outrun
the capability to spend it," says Pedro Pablo Kuczynski, Peru's prime minister and economic architect.
Still other mayors are throwing away the earnings on white elephants, ranging from heated swimming pools
to ornate public plazas. In the northern oil town of Tumbes, almost half the population lives without sewage
service and one-quarter are illiterate. But the local government recently unveiled a 7-foot-long cement
monument to the iguana, after earlier having built a ball-shaped monument to soccer referees. A town
spokesman says the monuments are good for tourism and that the government is also spending money on
services.
Augusto Baertl, a longtime mining executive and consultant, says Mr. Humala's strength with the poor reflects
"that in a time of abundance, the gains are not reaching the people with the greatest needs." Polls show that
Mr. Humala has pulled within striking distance of conservative front-runner Lourdes Flores. They are vying to
succeed Alejandro Toledo, a centrist whose market-oriented policies have weighed on his popularity. Mr.
Humala has vowed to boot out traditional politicians and revise mining and energy contracts with foreign
companies on terms he says will bring more income to poor Peruvians.
Bolivia also has endured a populist backlash, in part because of the slow flow of benefits from a
natural-resource boom. Leftist Evo Morales was elected president in December pledging to take a tougher
line with the foreign companies that control the country's vast oil and natural-gas reserves. Last year, Bolivia

--- Page 177 ---
Page 177 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
approved a controversial new tax on energy multinational companies that will be used in part to fund local
and regional governments. But, as in Peru, Bolivian local leaders haven't always made good use of the
income. Local administrations hadn't been able to spend about one-third of the oil and gas revenue they had
been receiving even before the new fees, according to Bolivia's economy ministry.
More common throughout the region are local leaders who are spending too freely. The central Brazilian city
of Paulinia is rolling in money from a local oil refinery, and Mayor Edson Moura, a member of a centrist party,
isn't shy about flaunting it. At the entrance to the city, he built a pair of giant arches, in the shape of a
medieval castle gate. In 2004, he built a $40 million municipal building and shopping-center complex, and
spent $200,000 for an inaugural concert by pop singer Ivete Sangalo. (Not a single merchant has rented
space in the shopping center.)
Mr. Moura last year announced plans to turn Paulinia into a film mecca. He then flew himself and a
delegation, at government expense, to France for the Cannes Film Festival. State attorneys have launched
investigations into Mr. Moura's contracting policies and a citizens group is trying to get him removed. A
spokesman says Mr. Moura has done nothing wrong and is dedicated to diversifying Paulinia's economy.
In Colombia, local corruption involving oil revenue has been so flagrant that the central government has been
trying to "recentralize," or assert greater control of oil revenue. Decentralization critics note that Chile, the
strongest economy in Latin America, is also one of the most centralized. Rather than giving local leaders
carte blanche, Chile's federal government has been squirreling away some of the windfall from higher copper
prices into a stabilization fund to be tapped in the future should prices fall.
Peru's economy minister last year presented a bill seeking to create such a rainy-day fund, but regional and
local leaders blocked the proposal in Congress -- a sign of just how strong the local officials have become.
For centuries, Peru had been ruled by strong central leaders, from the Spanish viceroys of the 16th century to
the military strongmen of the 1960s and '70s. The move to earmark greater revenue to grass-roots politicians
was part of the democratic effervescence following the ouster of authoritarian President Alberto Fujimori,
whose decade-long rule ended amid a corruption scandal in 2000.
Throughout Latin America, there has been a steady trend toward decentralization of public budgets, with
state, regional and local governments' share of spending rising to 19.3% in 2004 from 13.1% in 1985,
according to the Inter-American Development Bank. Advocates argue that local control can foster
transparency and efficiency. Local leaders say they deserve the money to defray the social and
environmental costs of natural-resource industries.
Peru has assigned local and regional governments half the income taxes paid by mining companies, a
payment known in Spanish as the canon. Grass-roots governments also get the lion's share of a royalty on
mining-company sales that went into effect last year.
To keep localities from padding payrolls with patronage jobs, the central government initially specified that
canon money could go only into infrastructure projects. Analysts say that fostered an unhealthy emphasis on
brick-and-mortar projects, often of questionable benefit. A couple of years ago, the impoverished town of
Calamarca budgeted 60% of its canon revenue for soccer fields and upgrades to public plazas.
Decentralization also has been plagued by corruption. A couple of years ago in the rough-and-tumble mining
town of Yungay, radio reporter Antonio de la Torre Echeandia denounced spending irregularities in the
government of Mayor Amaro Leon. Yungay's budget had recently been swollen with mining revenue. In
February of 2004, Mr. de la Torre was stabbed to death by assailants, one of whom he identified with his
dying breath as the mayor's driver. Last December, a Superior Court convicted Mr. Leon as the mastermind
of the murder and sentenced him and the two assassins to 17-year prison sentences.
Peru has tried promoting citizen activism by mandating "participatory budgets," in which residents of each
barrio gather and vote on spending priorities. But only 3% of the citizens surveyed by the national university
in the mining center of Cajamarca had ever attended such a budgeting session.
When citizens do participate, they don't always vote the way development economists might prefer. Barrios in
the town of Jangas have repeatedly voted to spend money fixing up churches rather than on basic services or
economic development. After signing off on the first church repair request, town officials have started turning
down others.
Inefficient public spending helps explain the odd paradox between Peru's superb macroeconomic
performance and its ugly public mood. The economy is set to expand more than 6% this year, inflation is
infinitesimal and exports have doubled since 2003. Amid a surge in commodity prices, Peru is sitting in the
catbird seat as the world's second-largest producer of silver and a major source of zinc, tin, copper and gold.

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yet in a region-wide survey of attitudes conducted by Latinobarometro, a Chilean polling company, Peruvians
ranked last among 18 nations in terms of their satisfaction with democracy and the market economy, as well
as their confidence that their taxes were being well spent. Stanford-educated President Toledo, who has
pursued a market-oriented agenda, has an approval rating languishing in the teens. Due to suspicions of
corruption, one Andean mayor was lynched a couple of years ago, and several other local leaders have
narrowly escaped angry mobs. There also have been repeated, disruptive protests against foreign
companies, especially mining firms such as Newmont Mining Corp. and Barrick Gold Corp.
Beatriz Boza, founder of Citizens Up To Date, a watchdog group in Lima, says it will take many years to fortify
Peru's stunted institutions and civic sense. To raise awareness, her group posts municipal budget information
on the Internet and trains local journalists to monitor public spending. The group also gives awards to town
leaders who spend wisely, such as Alfredo Edgar Vera Arana, a medical doctor who is mayor of the isolated
mountain mining town of Independencia.
Mr. Vera used canon money to build a garbage-treatment plant to replace the open dump that fouled local air
and drinking water. The town built the plant itself for $130,000 -- about 20% of what an outside engineering
firm bid. It sorts and processes 25 tons of garbage a day. Proceeds from recycling of inorganic waste like
metal and paper have helped reduce Independencia's garbage-collection fee to half that of neighboring
towns. Skin and stomach ailments related to poor sanitation have plummeted, local medical officials say.
The six tons of compost produced daily in the plant are being used to help farmers switch to export crops
from traditional subsistence crops. The city also has used some canon money to guarantee bank loans for
the farmers. Alejandro Antunes, 65, was recently hoeing a field full of artichokes that will be exported to
Europe at a price five times what his traditional potato crop would fetch. "Even at my age," he says, "I'm
learning something new."
Mr. Vera's steady hand is the exception. "They gave an open checkbook to mayors who never had money to
fix a pothole before," says Alejandro Villegas Rojas, who runs a training and consulting program for public
officials, known as "The School for Mayors." A psychologist by training, Mr. Villegas has been trying to teach
some lessons in budget priorities to free-spending mayors like La Encanada's Mr. Valera, a friend from his
youth.
After some prodding, Mr. Valera has been focusing on more substantive projects, involving roads,
electrification and a new hospital. "We are trying to do important works without losing sight of those that
people enjoy," Mr. Valera says.
The mayor certainly isn't abandoning his flashy style. Ahead of elections, Mr. Valera published a 110-page
color booklet detailing the building boom, including photos of himself visiting the Eiffel Tower and the Vatican
while on an economic-assistance mission in Europe.

Abercrombie, Chico's and Saks Fall --- February Sales Disappoint; Men's Wearhouse, PetSmart Advance in a Down Market
Abreast of the Market
Abercrombie, Chico's and Saks Fall --- February Sales Disappoint; Men's Wearhouse, PetSmart
Advance in a Down Market
By Karen Talley
874 words
3 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell as retailers Abercrombie &Fitch, Chico's FAS and Saks failed to live up to expectations for
February sales.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average dropped 28.02, or 0.25%, to 11025.51. The Nasdaq Composite Index lost
3.53, or 0.15%, to 2311.11. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index shed 2.10, or 0.16%, to 1289.14.
"The issues today were retail sales and the gain we saw in the price of oil," said Joseph Saluzzi, co-head of
equity trading at Themis Trading LLC. "They created nervousness."
A rise in long-term interest rates also weighed on the market.
Abercrombie &Fitch fell $6.20, or 9.2%, to $61.05. Sales missed Wall Street's expectations, with the teen
retailer blaming a shortage of trendy jeans at its mall-based stores.
Chico's FAS fell 6.41, or 13%, to 41.35. Fourth-quarter profit for the women's clothier missed Wall Street's
forecast, and February sales at stores open longer than a year rose 5.7%, short of the average analyst
expectation of an 8.7% gain.
Saks gave up 80 cents, or 4.1%, to 18.80. February same-store sales fell 6.4%; analysts projected a rise of
1.5%.
There were some exceptions, including Men's Wearhouse, which jumped 4.59, or 14%, to 36.80 and ended
up as the Big Board's second-best percentage gainer. The men's clothing retailer posted
better-than-expected fourth-quarter results as gross margins climbed above company estimates.
PetSmart (Nasdaq) rose 2.04, or 7.8%, to 28.25. The pet-supply chain bounced back from the third quarter
with better-than-anticipated fourth-quarter earnings and sales.
General Motors was the biggest percentage decliner in the Dow industrial average for a second consecutive
session, losing 49 cents, or 2.5%, to 19.41. On Wednesday, the No. 1 auto maker said total deliveries
dropped 2.5% in February from a year earlier and, coping with continued market-share loss, that it planned to
cut production in the second quarter.
Google gained 11.65, or 3.2%, to 376.45 on Nasdaq. The Internet search engine's executives hosted a
meeting with analysts and discussed steps they are taking to improve operations.
ImClone Systems (Nasdaq) lost 1.58, or 4%, to 37.42. The Food and Drug Administration approved Erbitux
as a treatment for head and neck cancer. But ImClone faces other issues, analysts said. Oppenheimer cited
concerns about a lofty stock price and the "stagnant penetration" of Erbitux into the colorectal-cancer market.
Nasdaq-traded Ciena rose 29 cents, or 6.7%, to 4.64. The telecommunications-equipment maker narrowed
its first-quarter loss, and said an increase in orders in recent weeks could boost second-quarter sales by 7%
from the prior quarter.
PHH dropped 2.73, or 9.5%, to 26. The mortgage company delayed filing its 10-K form with the Securities
and Exchange Commission, citing accounting adjustments related to its spinoff from Cendant. Cendant shed
14 cents to 16.63.
C.R. Bard gained 4.06, or 6.1%, to 70.06. The medical-technology company agreed to buy closely held
catheter-systems company Venetec International for about $166 million and, as a result, edged up its 2006
revenue projection.

--- Page 181 ---
Page 181 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
CBS lost 59 cents, or 2.4%, to 24.06. Citigroup initiated coverage of the entertainment company's shares with
a sell rating, feeling per-share earnings estimates for 2006 and 2007 are too high.
Fluor dropped 6.01, or 6.8%, to 81.80. The engineering and construction company said fourth-quarter
operating profit fell 10% because of loss provisions on a transportation project and a provision for taxes in
foreign jurisdictions.
McDermott International rose 2.75, or 5.4%, to 53.60. The energy-service company's fourth-quarter earnings
were 48 cents a share, down from a year ago, but well ahead of analysts' expectations for 39 cents a share.
Gold Fields' American depositary receipts fell 1.63, or 7%, to 21.57. Polyus Gold, Russia's biggest gold
miner, moved to divest its stake in the South African gold miner, calling upon Goldman Sachs Group and
Citigroup to place its 98.5 million shares.
Progressive eased 3.30, or 3.1%, to 103.80. Merrill Lynch cut shares of the insurer to sell from neutral, saying
growth "will likely be more of a challenge."
Molson Coors Brewing rose 2.20, or 3.5%, to 64.76. Stifel Nicolaus upgraded the beer maker's stock to buy
from hold and raised its 2006 earnings estimate, citing business trends in Canada and no more losses in
Brazil.
Manulife Financial rose 81 cents, or 1.3%, to 64.80. Bear Stearns raised shares of the financial-services
provider to peer perform from underperform, reflecting, among other things, the investment firm's higher
earnings and return-on-equity estimates.
Cablevision Systems added 20 cents to 26.40. UBS increased shares of the cable and entertainment
company to buy from neutral on "standout results" and strong 2006 guidance.

Stocks and Souks: In Quest to Build A Financial Center, Hurdles for Dubai --- Already a Busy Economic Hub, Ancient Trading Crossroads Aims...
Stocks and Souks: In Quest to Build A Financial Center, Hurdles for Dubai --- Already a Busy
Economic Hub, Ancient Trading Crossroads Aims to Channel Oil Money --- Morgan Stanley to Set Up
Shop
By Bill Spindle and Yasmine El-Rashidi
2,216 words
2 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
KINGDOM HOTEL Investments listed its shares on the Dubai International Financial Exchange on March 1. A
page-one article Thursday incorrectly said the company was expected to list its shares on the exchange in
the future.
(WSJ March 4, 2006)
(END)
DUBAI, United Arab Emirates -- Four years ago, Sheik Mohammed Bin Rashid al-Maktoum, then crown
prince and now emir of Dubai, stood overlooking 110 acres of fallow land and unveiled an astonishing plan:
Turn Dubai into an international financial hub that would rank with Tokyo, London or New York.
How Dubai pursued that goal offers a vivid look at the tiny emirate's outsize ambitions on the world economic
stage -- ambitions that now have created a political furor as Dubai accelerates a push into global port
operation.
It was just months after the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks, and Dubai's financial system was suspected of being a
conduit for some of the funds behind the plot. The United Arab Emirates, of which Dubai is a part, had been
one of only two countries to recognize the Taliban government in Afghanistan that sheltered al Qaeda.
And yet, surrounded by an entourage clad in traditional white gowns known as thobes, Sheik Mohammed
invited the financial industry's elite to set up shop in one of the world's most turbulent corners. It was a region
where business often works by the murky rules of the bazaar, not by open, internationally recognized
standards.
Today, the budding Dubai International Financial Center occupies those same 110 acres, which now are
landscaped and filled with buildings, including a tall office tower. Morgan Stanley is moving much of its
growing Middle East operations to the center. Global banks including HSBC Holdings PLC and Standard
Chartered PLC are expanding regional operations here. Within the center, trading has begun on the Dubai
International Financial Exchange, which has hopes of riding the region's tidal wave of oil money to a position
as a major bourse someday.
It is far from that now. While it is already attracting money from across the Persian Gulf region, and plans to
expand into a broad range of financial trading and products, so far it trades a mere handful of securities.
Moreover, it faces competition from other prospective regional financial centers that also are appealing to
global financial firms with somewhat more-open and Western-friendly regulatory frameworks.
Qatar is wooing financial firms with the lure of the huge financing needed for giant natural-gas projects
planned in that country over the next decade. Bahrain, long an offshore financial hub for neighboring Saudi
Arabia, has taken the lead as a center for Islamic finance. And Saudi Arabia itself has strengthened its
regulatory infrastructure and has slowly begun opening its financial industry to foreign competition.
Increasingly it is the Persian Gulf, rather than the older financial centers such as Lebanon, where significant
financial reform is taking place in the Middle East.
Dubai's new center has made the boldest step toward a more transparent and Westernized way of doing
business. It is already helping to lead a financial renaissance in the region, fed by petrodollars and by the
desire of many Middle Eastern investors to keep their money closer to home since Sept. 11.

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But developing the financial center has been a bumpy learning process for Dubai -- just like its controversial
plan to acquire Britain's Peninsular &Oriental Steam Navigation Co. and operate five U.S. ports. The financial
center, as it bids for a lucrative piece of the global economy, has had to go the extra mile to try to convince
the world that the emirate is serious about playing by international rules and standards.
Whether it succeeds matters beyond the emirate. If Dubai works as a hub where oil wealth can be recycled
into long-term Middle East investments, it could help integrate into the global economy a region that has been
left behind in recent years. Dubai's historic links to places such as Pakistan, Iran, Saudi Arabia, Egypt and
East Africa -- while raising concerns for some about terrorism or money laundering -- could help seed
opportunities in lands soon to be swamped by waves of young people entering moribund job markets.
Without such opportunities, the new generation could be prime recruiting ground for extremists.
For centuries, Dubai has been a trading hub, a crossroads linking South Asia, the Middle East and Africa. Its
gold and diamond souks, houses of barter and informal cash-transfer storefronts have long formed an
opaque business world based on connections and clan allegiances. Black-market operators, arms dealers,
terrorist financiers and money launderers have taken advantage of the freewheeling environment, even if the
vast bulk of business is legitimate. Dubai's legal system in some ways amounts to a set of codes and courts
superimposed on a clan-based power structure. For foreign investors, none of this has engendered much
confidence or even understanding.
Starting about 15 years ago, the emirate began building something few other places in the region have
managed: a vibrant modern economy not dependent solely on oil. With Dubai lacking the large oil and
natural-gas reserves common elsewhere in the Persian Gulf, Sheik Mohammed and his circle of
Western-educated advisers built Dubai's port into one of the world's biggest and turned the emirate into a
major transportation hub.
Then they made the emirate a tourist destination, with a mix of Western glitz, Middle Eastern atmosphere and
duty-free shopping. They hired British, American and other foreign executives to lead these efforts and
provide experience that Dubai lacked.
Dubai also built specialized development zones to attract foreign technology, media and health-care
companies with tax exemptions and other advantages. It did it all with its own brand of over-the-top panache:
The emirate is erecting what it bills as the world's tallest skyscraper and the largest aquarium. It features an
indoor ski slope, despite temperatures that can reach 130 degrees Fahrenheit in the summer, and a property
development built on land shaped into a map of the world.
Sheik Mohammed, a horse-racing enthusiast who is a fixture at tracks in Australia, Kentucky and England,
was the force behind most of this development, even while his older brother served as the emir. When the
brother, Sheik Maktoum Bin Rashid al-Maktoum, died two months ago, Sheik Mohammed became emir.
In the latest phase of its development, Dubai sought to lure global financial firms to its soil. And if they wanted
international legal and regulatory standards, Dubai was determined it would provide them -- at least inside
one section of downtown. After some wrangling with the U.A.E.'s central authorities, Dubai won permission to
exempt its financial center from nearly all of the federation's commercial laws.
The U.A.E.'s central bank, under international pressure to improve its oversight, set some limits. It retained
jurisdiction over investigations of possible terrorism financing and money laundering. But it let Dubai set up an
entirely separate, Western-based commercial system for its financial district that would do business in dollars,
and in English.
This included independent regulators and judges imported from the West. Dubai scored its first coup in 2002
when it lured Ian Hay Davison, a former chief executive of Lloyds of London, and Phillip Thorpe, a former
senior British financial regulator, to set up and oversee the regulatory side of the proposed financial center.
The old Dubai intruded. When some property-development rights in the financial center were allocated to
local businessmen outside the designated auction process, Messrs. Davison and Thorpe cried foul, recall
people familiar with the case. Although there was no suggestion the recipients got preferential terms in the
2004 deal, the Western regulators argued that the perception of back-room dealing could undermine the
center's reputation.
When word of the dispute leaked to the news media, senior financial-center officials sacked the two
regulators, escorting Mr. Thorpe from his office and confiscating his computer in the process. The result was
a spate of bad press and so much anxiety among international financial firms that the whole project nearly
collapsed, say officials who were involved. The two top officials heading the project soon moved to different
jobs.
Officials now call the episode a "cultural misunderstanding." While they say no one behaved inappropriately,
officials concede the episode was a major setback. When Dubai advertised that it wanted to create a financial

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
center built on transparency and global standards for investing, the initial response was, "In the Middle East?
Yeah, right," says Omar Bin Sulaiman, who was brought in by the crown prince to rescue the project. "It just
confirmed what everybody expected."
Habib Al-Mulla, a Western-educated Dubai lawyer who is chairman of the regulatory operations of the
financial center, calls the handling of the dispute "a mistake," adding: "We learned from it."
Furthering the embarrassment, Mr. Thorpe and several members of his team quickly went to work for Qatar,
which also hopes to build a global financial center.
Dubai didn't give up. Mr. Bin Sulaiman, a confident veteran of other Dubai megaprojects, then just 31, raced
back and forth to London and Zurich to talk with senior executives at wavering financial firms, wearing a
business suit on his Western trips and switching to a thobe when he returned. To assure financial firms the
project still was on track, as well as to bolster his staff's morale, he pushed to get the decree officially setting
up the center in time for an annual staff meeting. The decree from the emir arrived just two hours before the
gathering.
Dubai strengthened the regulators' statutory independence, so they reported strictly to their own board and,
through it, to the emir. The center hired a new regulatory head, David Knott, former chief financial regulator of
Australia.
Mr. Al-Mulla had been among the skeptics when Dubai officials first outlined plans for the center not long after
9/11. "I thought, `These guys are crazy,' " he recalls. Today, his office is at the top of a new tower in the
center, where he presides over the financial regulatory apparatus of which Mr. Knott is part.
Morgan Stanley decided late last year that it would concentrate many of its regional operations at the Dubai
center. It says the surge in regional wealth and the Middle East's rapidly developing financial markets
demand a bigger on-the-ground staff rather than one based in London or elsewhere.
The opening of Morgan Stanley's Dubai office, slated for this month, is part of a push by Chief Executive John
Mack (who is of Lebanese descent) to expand the firm's footprint in the Middle East and other emerging
markets. Last month, Morgan Stanley named as an outside director Hutham S. Olayan, head of a U.S. unit of
Olayan Group, a diversified business including energy with operations in the Middle East. In addition to the
Dubai office, Morgan Stanley last month applied for a license for an office in Qatar.
The center in Dubai also is home to many small firms important to the region's economic prospects. Rasmala
Investments, a boutique investment bank, recently moved its holding company to Dubai from Bermuda.
Founder Ali al-Shihabi, a Saudi, says Dubai's economic leadership and international work force enable it to
build quickly "what would take 50 years anyplace else."
The financial center plans to oversee its first major public offering soon. Kingdom Hotel Investments,
controlled by Saudi investor Prince al Waleed bin Talal and other Middle Easterners, is expected to sell
shares in its operations, which include stakes in hotels in 13 countries.
Dubai's center won't readily be able to escape a cultural tug of war with the rest of the region's financial
markets -- including a local stock market across town.
On the one hand, growing global attention to terrorism and money laundering are a selling point for the
international center's more open, tightly regulated financial services. "Secrecy has become a disadvantage"
since Sept. 11, says Mr. Knott, who sees the financial center as an opportunity "to help big regional
companies take those last steps toward global standards of governance and transparency."
On the other hand, it is an open question how many of the people deploying the region's oil riches are the
kind of investors for whom transparency is important. The local market operates under rules that are far more
opaque, and it is booming. "Hot money" in search of quick profits is flooding into this exchange, known as the
Dubai Financial Market, where trading takes place in the local currency, accounts needn't be funded on an
international scale, and standards are much less rigorous.
Essa Abdulfattah Kazim, who heads the local stock market and sits on the board of the international center,
acknowledges the challenge. Because of his positions on both exchanges, he knows the task ahead: "It's
building a whole new culture," he says.
---
Randall Smith in New York contributed to this article.

Dana missed $20.8 million in interest payments on two bonds, heightening concerns about the auto-parts

U.S. personal income and consumer spending climbed in January and manufacturing activity rose last month,
suggesting that first-quarter growth will be robust.
