Chasing Performance: Investors Pour Money Into Hard-Asset Mutual Funds
Getting Going
Chasing Performance: Investors Pour Money Into Hard-Asset Mutual Funds
By Jonathan Clements
898 words
1 March 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MUTUAL-FUND INVESTORS are doing the right thing for the wrong reason -- and maybe at the wrong time.
Proclaiming a desire to diversify, folks have been shoveling money into gold, natural-resources and
real-estate mutual funds. Chicago investment-research firm Morningstar Inc. calculates that these so-called
hard-asset funds now have combined assets of almost $104 billion, up from less than $19 billion five years
ago.
Maybe some of these investors really are looking to lower their portfolio's risk level. But let's be honest: A lot
of what we're seeing is foolish performance chasing. Will it end badly? It always does.
-- Playing hard. Over the past 15 years, mutual-fund investors have grown increasingly sophisticated in their
portfolio building.
They are now paying more attention to a manager's stock-picking style. They're buying everything from
blue-chip to microcap stocks. And they have added a slew of specialized foreign funds, including those
targeting emerging markets and international small-company stocks.
More recently, the big push has been to add alternative investments. Mutual funds that use hedge-fund-type
strategies now boast assets of $41 billion. But the truly startling growth has occurred among hard-asset
funds, which saw assets jump 41% in 2005.
As is often the case, ordinary investors are mimicking institutional investors. A 2005 Russell Investment
Group survey found that North American institutional investors were earmarking 21% of their assets for
alternative investments, with that money divvied up almost equally among real estate, hedge funds and
private equity.
In theory, there's nothing wrong with the move into alternative investments. Hard assets and their ilk can
perform quite differently from conventional stocks and bonds, so adding them to your investment mix should
damp down your portfolio's overall price gyrations.
Moreover, this extra diversification could prove mighty valuable if inflation accelerates. In that scenario,
conventional stocks and bonds will likely get hammered, but hard assets should perform well.
-- Chasing returns. Nonetheless, the hard-asset craze makes me uneasy. By spreading your bets as widely
as possible, you are conceding that you don't know which investments will perform best.
Yet many of today's hard-asset junkies aren't conceding anything. Instead, they're looking to win. They have
seen the fabulous returns of recent years, and they are hoping for more.
According to Morningstar, natural-resources funds have posted average annual gains of 19.1% over the past
five years, real-estate funds have clocked 20.3% a year, and precious-metals funds have soared 35.7% a
year. That's far ahead of the 0.4% annual gain for the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index.
Are today's hard-asset buyers getting in at the top? I have no clue. Once a market trend gets rolling, it can
run for years, leaving naysaying investors eating dust. Still, there are some ominous signs.
Take equity real-estate investment trusts, which make their money by buying offices, shopping centers and
other buildings and then renting them out. As equity REIT prices have soared, yields have shrunk from 9.3%
in December 1999 to 4.1% today, according to the National Association of Real Estate Investment Trusts.
-- Changing behavior. It isn't just performance that will likely disappoint. Hard-asset funds probably won't
continue to be the wonderful diversifiers that they've been in the past half-dozen years.

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Once again, consider real-estate investment trusts. Before the 2000-2002 bear market, REITs weren't
especially popular, and their bear-market credentials weren't exactly stellar. When the S&P 500 slipped 3.2%
in 1990, REITs plunged 15.4%.
Yet, today, REITs are seen as an almost magical investment. Not only have they outperformed the S&P 500
in each of the past six years, but they also posted big gains during the 2000-2002 bear market. Result: REITs
are now widely owned and closely followed, and that means you aren't likely to see a huge divergence
between their performance and that of the broad market in the years ahead.
Remember, when you buy hard-asset funds, you aren't getting raw land and barrels of oil. Instead, you are
typically getting stocks, commodity futures and other securities that offer a play on hard assets.
The good news is, history suggests you will earn higher long-run returns owning these securities rather than
the underlying hard assets. The bad news? You are dealing with securities -- and that means their results are
more likely to be driven by the whims of investors.
"The diversification benefits of all these things have been oversold," says William Bernstein, an investment
adviser in North Bend, Ore. "When you package anything as a readily tradable security, its correlation with
the stock and bond markets is going to increase."
That said, you should have some hard-asset exposure. Consider earmarking maybe 5% of your total portfolio
for REITs and maybe 5% for a commodity or gold-stock fund. You might, however, build up your stake slowly
over the next year or two.
"It might seem like there's never a bad time to diversify," Mr. Bernstein says. "But if you're buying into an
asset class that has recently performed well, you're probably getting in at the wrong time."

Oil Futures Jump After Terror Attack On Saudi Facility
Commodities Report
Oil Futures Jump After Terror Attack On Saudi Facility
By Masood Farivar
500 words
25 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- A terrorist attempt Friday to blow up a key oil-processing facility in Saudi Arabia pushed up oil
futures while raising fresh questions about the kingdom's security.
Crude futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange, reflecting the additional "security premium" in the
contract's price, rallied nearly 4% and climbed past $63 a barrel.
The April crude futures contract was up $2.61 a barrel to hit a high of $63.15 before easing back a bit to settle
at $62.91 a barrel, up $2.37 for the day.
"This shows that there are people in Saudi Arabia willing and able to try to attack oil facilities and get very
close to [the royal family]," said Lowell Feld, an international analyst at the federal Energy Information
Administration in Washington.
The attack may represent an end to a year and half of relative calm and a "trend that saw a decreasing
sophistication in terrorist attacks in the kingdom," according to Rochdi Younsi, an analyst at the New York
based Eurasia Group.
Early reports suggested a pipeline was set on fire but Saudi Oil Minister Ali Naimi denied that the attack had
disrupted operations or oil flows at the facility, which processes about two thirds of the kingdom's oil output of
just less than 10 million barrels a day.
News of the terrorist attack had an outsize impact on oil prices because it came amid heightened worries
about terrorist threats and the vulnerability of global oil supplies. Those worries have been fueled recently by
violence in Nigeria, a spike in sectarian conflict in Iraq, and increasingly bellicose rhetoric from Venezuela.
"Terrorist threats have been and will continue to be a background supporter" for oil prices, said Tom Bentz,
an analyst at BNP Paribas Futures in New York, adding that al-Qaeda leader Osama bin Laden "recently
indicated that there were more attacks in the works."
In addition to threatening fresh attacks in a recently released audiotape, Mr. bin Laden has previously called
for attacks on Saudi oil facilities in a bid to bring down the ruling al-Saud family.
Jamal Qureshi, an analyst with Washington-based consultancy PFC Energy, said the threat of small-scale
attacks should be taken seriously.
"It takes a small band of folks" to carry out an attack, he said. "They're going to keep coming and they're
going to get lucky one of these days."
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex rose sharply, supported by safe-haven buying and
crude-oil futures after the foiled terrorist attack. Spot-February rose $10.40 to $558.80 an ounce. Most-active
April gold gained $10.30 to $561.20.
CORN: Futures at the Chicago Board of Trade rallied to near six-month highs on supportive export demand
and general strength in commodity markets. March corn gained 4.75 cents to $2.2775 a bushel.

Political Gulf: In Ports Furor, a Clash Over Dubai --- Where Bush Sees an Ally, Foes in Congress See Links to Terrorism --- New Wealth From...
Political Gulf: In Ports Furor, a Clash Over Dubai --- Where Bush Sees an Ally, Foes in Congress See
Links to Terrorism --- New Wealth From an Oil Boom
By Bill Spindle, Neil King Jr. and Glenn R. Simpson
2,556 words
23 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Dubai is the chameleon of the Persian Gulf -- which explains why it is suddenly at the heart of an international
controversy over who should manage America's seaports.
When members of Congress realized in recent days that a company from Dubai, which is part of the United
Arab Emirates, was set to assume management of some terminals at five large U.S. ports, they hit the alarm
button. In the UAE, they saw an Arab country that served as a jumping-off point for Sept. 11 hijackers, has
been suspected as a site of terrorism money transfers and has tried to stay friendly with nearby Iran.
When President Bush looks at the UAE, he sees something quite different: a strategically important country
that hosts scores of visits by U.S. Navy ships each year, allows U.S. forces to use a major air base and
passes on significant intelligence tips in the war on terrorism. As a result, Mr. Bush has staunchly defended
the right of the UAE company to buy the terminal manager.
Those contrasting passions explain why there has been such a spectacular political collision over what just a
few weeks ago seemed an innocuous international business deal. That deal -- in which state-owned Dubai
Ports World has agreed to buy Peninsular &Oriental Steam Navigation Co. of London -- now sits at the
intersection of the global economy and the war on terrorism.
And that can be a dangerous spot, as Dubai and the UAE have learned in a hurry.
The outcome of the struggle has big implications, and not just for the management of a handful of American
ports. The flow and investment of petrodollars around the globe could be affected, as could the attitudes in
both America and the Arab world. Many in the U.S. worry that a change of management at such sensitive
facilities would open the door for Middle East terrorists to ship in weapons and explosives.
For their part, many in the Arab world see American opposition as another sign of prejudice against Muslims
and an unwillingness of the U.S. to work even with an Arab government that has tried to remain friendly.
For President Bush, who has chosen to take a stand against most leaders in his own party by defending the
ports deal, the political stakes are high. In an attempt to quell concerns that Middle Eastern port management
would open the door to terrorist activity, his administration yesterday said it had obtained extra security
commitments from Dubai Ports World before clearing the takeover. Administration aides also stress that
security screening at U.S. ports is primarily the responsibility of government law-enforcement agents, not the
commercial firms that manage terminals.
Meanwhile, DP World's chief operating officer, Ted Bikey, told CNN his company already works with U.S.
officials in Dubai to ensure cargo safety, and declared: "We'll do anything possible to make sure this deal
goes through."
Still, congressional efforts to craft legislation blocking the transaction continued. New Jersey Gov. Jon
Corzine, complaining the port plan would expose the country to "the onloading of potential dangerous
material," reiterated his plans to file a federal lawsuit to stop the transaction.
The effort to reach a consensus is made all the more difficult because at the heart of it lies Dubai, a distant
and little-understood land that defies stereotypes.
Dubai is a city-state that is one of seven tribally based Persian Gulf sheikdoms that united in 1971 to form the
United Arab Emirates. The UAE is a rough confederation that runs along the southern rim of the Persian Gulf,
directly across from Iran. Geographically, the UAE is about the size of Maine. It has about four million people
-- though only an estimated 15% to 20% of them are UAE citizens. The rest are a mix of Indians, Pakistanis,
Bangladeshis and other foreigners who have drifted into the UAE over the years, as well as expatriate
Britons, Americans and Australians who have come for work or, increasingly, to retire.

--- Page 200 ---
Page 200 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dubai is the most free-wheeling of the Emirates, with business tentacles that reach far and wide. As recently
as the early 1950s, it was a backwater comprised of coral-hut villages.
While Dubai sits on the edge of large oil and natural-gas reserves, it doesn't have them in the same massive
quantity as does neighboring Emirate Abu Dhabi, nearby Qatar or Saudi Arabia. As a result, Dubai has
aggressively leveraged its role as a crossroads to develop a diversified economy. The Emirate took off in the
1980s and 1990s, by developing a world-class port facility and then transforming into one of the region's
biggest and glitziest tourist destinations. It welcomes more visitors each year than Egypt with its pyramids.
The Dubai government also runs the Middle East's biggest airline, Emirates Air.
By virtue of its position along Persian Gulf shipping lanes, Dubai has long been a financial and trading hub for
goods and money from the oil-rich Gulf states, as well as from India, Pakistan, Central Asia and Russia. Even
today, small junks, dingies and other boats line the waterways crammed with all manner of goods -- from
gold and diamonds to televisions and DVD players -- in the shadows of the modern glass skyscrapers under
construction nearly everywhere.
Dubai is ruled by Sheikh Mohammed bin Rashid al-Maktoum, a fixture on the global thoroughbred
horse-racing circuit. As crown prince before the recent death of his elder brother, Sheikh Mohammed was the
driving force behind the city's hyperactive economic development and its sky's-the-limit approach to major
projects. With a team of close advisers, he oversees a web of interlocking companies controlled by the Dubai
government.
The UAE came under tremendous pressure to improve regulation of its financial system after some of the
money used to finance the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks was found to have moved through an Emirates
bank. Since then, UAE central-bank officials have strengthened antimoney-laundering and terror-financing
laws and have increased oversight of the financial system.
While the U.S. would like to see the UAE, along with other countries in the region, continue to become more
proactive in ferreting out problems, the government has worked with the U.S. and international regulatory
bodies to put a "comprehensive" regulatory framework in place, according to a senior U.S. Treasury official.
Dubai has built itself in the past five years on a rush of funds washing over the region from increased oil
revenue amid high oil prices. After aggressively marketing itself as a new international financial hub, Dubai
has seen its stock and property markets skyrocket. Some of that newfound wealth has then flowed outside
the region. Investment companies controlled by the Dubai government recently purchased the Essex House
Hotel and the Helmsley building in New York City, the Sir Francis Drake Hotel in San Francisco and a $1
billion portfolio of apartments in the U.S. They also have purchased a one-third stake in the London Eye, a
giant Ferris wheel on the banks of the Thames River.
Continuing this economic development has required the UAE to dance delicately through the
sometimes-lethal politics of the Gulf region. Because of its history as a commercial link between the Gulf and
the outside world, it has tried hard to maintain good relations all around.
That has been difficult. In the 1980s, while the Iran-Iraq war raged, it tried to maintain neutrality even as other
Arab states lined up behind their brothers in Iraq. Dubai -- which has a substantial number of Iranians and
others who share Iranians' Shiite faith -- tried to lean more toward Iran's direction than did its fellow emirates.
Once the war was over, the UAE was the Arab country that emerged with the closest commercial ties to Iran.
The UAE also has leaned in America's direction. When Iraq invaded Kuwait in 1990, the UAE rallied to
Kuwait's side, opening up its facilities for the giant American-led military force that routed Saddam Hussein's
troops. That led to military cooperation that continues.
In fact, U.S. ties to the country now run deep across nearly every major U.S. agency, from the Pentagon and
the Treasury Department to the Central Intelligence Agency and the Department of Homeland Security.
"Across the board, the UAE is one of the region's strongest supporters of U.S. policy," said William Rugh,
U.S. ambassador to the UAE in the early 1990s.
For the Pentagon, the UAE offers the region's only deep-water port and the only dry-dock facility large
enough to take in aircraft carriers or nuclear submarines. U.S. officials also pointed out that two of its ports,
including the DP World-administered Jebel Ali Port in Dubai, host more U.S. naval visits than any other port
outside of the U.S. The country also hosts major air bases for U.S. refueling and surveillance flights.
The ties that began building after the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait deepened all the more after the Emirates in
early 2000 agreed to a $6.4 billion purchase of 80 F-16 Desert Falcons, one of the most sophisticated fighter
jets available at the time.

--- Page 201 ---
Page 201 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"That sale was a symbol that the United States trusted the UAE and took the UAE seriously," said Theodore
Kattouf, U.S. ambassador to Abu Dhabi from 1998 to August 2001. "There was a lot that flowed from that,
and it put us in good stead for everything that has come since 9/11."
At the same time, Dubai's history as the most free-wheeling of the Emirates, especially in commerce and
finance, has led to tensions amid the war on terror and the confrontation with Iran in recent years.
For one thing, Dubai is believed to have been one of the most important conduits for Iran's nuclear
technology acquisition program, according to U.S. court cases and interviews with experts in the field. The
Wisconsin Project on Nuclear Arms Control, a nongovernment advocacy group, last year published a list of
38 weapons-related smuggling cases since 1982 in which the goods moved through Dubai and the other
islands that constitute the United Arab Emirates. Most of the illicit goods crossing Dubai go through its ports.
More generally, according to sanctions experts and numerous U.S. court and regulatory cases, Iran uses
Dubai to evade U.S. economic sanctions on Iran and other Middle Eastern countries. The UAE doesn't
recognize those sanctions.
Iranian front companies in Dubai routinely obtain prohibited U.S. goods, federal court records show. In one
undercover investigation by the Immigration and Customs Enforcement agency that resulted in a November
2005 guilty plea in U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia, the representative of an Iranian front
company was caught on tape assuring an undercover agent posing as a businessman not to worry about
sanctions regulations.
"You are going to export to Dubai, which does not have any regulations. It's a free, uh, country for importing,
exporting," said Khalid Mahmood, according to his guilty plea. Asked if the equipment would then be shipped
to Iran, Mr. Mahmood replied, "Once it comes here, we'll ship it anywhere in the world, no problem."
Similarly, in 2003, UAE officials refused a U.S. request to intercept a shipment of nuclear technology bound
for South Africa by a smuggler named Asher Karni, according to University of Georgia sanctions expert Scott
Jones, who works with U.S. agencies on proliferation issues. Mr. Karni was convicted of violating sanctions
against weapons of mass destruction last year in U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia. The UAE
also was believed to be a nexus for Pakistan's nuclear program and hosted at least two front companies that
forwarded material to Islamabad.
William Reinsch, a former top official at the Commerce Department during the Clinton administration, says
that when he was working on exports controls, "Dubai was famous as a port of exit of everything from
refrigerators to computers going across the Gulf to Iran. It was long regarded as a major enforcement point."
Recently, though, the UAE has moved to the forefront of Gulf states in cooperating with U.S. diplomatic
initiatives against terrorism and nuclear proliferation. In little-publicized missions, State and Treasury
Department officials have been shuttling to the Emirates over the past two years to work out cooperation.
Only last week, the State Department's nonproliferation chief, Steven Rademake, was in Abu Dhabi seeking
to coordinate new security initiatives.
Current and former U.S. officials say the UAE has provided significant assistance both in passing along
terrorism tips and in helping apprehend suspects. In the lead up to Sept. 11, UAE officials passed along
information that led to the arrest of several "major terrorism suspects," said Mr. Kattouf, the former
ambassador. "These were not small fries."
U.S. officials say that UAE has continued to pass along significant intelligence, though the country's
constitutional prohibition against extraditions has caused tensions in cases where the U.S. has wanted
particular suspects to be transferred to U.S. custody.
The country was also the first in the region to implement the U.S. cargo-security initiative to prescreen
containers destined for the U.S. Five U.S. Customs officials now work in the Dubai Customs Intelligence Unit.
Now the question is whether a Dubai company would impede cargo security on the American end of
shipments. The terminals at U.S. ports that are at the heart of the controversy don't appear to have been the
goal of Dubai Ports World in acquiring Peninsular &Oriental Steam Navigation.
DP World -- part of the Ports, Customs &Freezone Corp., which is owned by the government of Dubai --
operates 22 terminals in ports world-wide and handles about 13 million 20-foot container units a year, a
spokesperson said.
Just a few years ago, Dubai Ports World was a little-known Middle Eastern company engaged in the then
low-visibility business of operating terminals to load and unload cargo containers. In 2004, it took a giant step
by acquiring the global port facilities of CSX Corp. of Jacksonville, Fla., for $1.15 billion, which didn't include
any U.S. ports.

--- Page 202 ---
Page 202 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The acquisition gave Dubai Ports, which was operating terminals in the Mideast, Romania and India, access
to what it wanted most: a presence in ports in fast-growing mainland China.
The company had much the same goal in mind when it bid aggressively in the takeover battle for Peninsular
&Oriental Steam Navigation. "We want to be in the markets our customers [the container shipping lines] are
telling us they are developing, and that means a focus on India and China in particular, as well as the Middle
East," the company's spokesperson said. "The U.S. is not the focus of the acquisition."
---
Daniel Machalaba contributed to this article.

Stocks Fall as Oil Tops $61 a Barrel, Fed Warns of Higher Interest Rates
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Fall as Oil Tops $61 a Barrel, Fed Warns of Higher Interest Rates
By E.S. Browning
522 words
22 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH OIL PRICES pushing back above $61 a barrel and the Federal Reserve rattling its saber about higher
interest rates, stocks continued the pullback that began Friday.
But the day's decline was minor, leaving investors to debate whether it marked a simple pause in the gains or
the start of something more serious.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 46.26 points, or 0.42%, to 11069.06, still up 3.3% this year. The
blue-chip average remains less than 52 points below Thursday's close of 11120.68, its highest finish since
before the 2001 terrorist attacks.
Crude-oil futures jumped $1.22 to $61.10 a barrel, on supply concerns following hostage-taking and attacks
on oil installations in Nigeria, a major exporter.
"Oil was the lead story," said Warren Epstein, head trader at brokerage firm Fagenson &Co. With stock
indexes up strongly over the previous two weeks, "people used that as an excuse to take some profits."
In addition, the Fed released minutes of its January policy meeting, Alan Greenspan's last as chairman,
which indicated that some Fed officials may be more concerned about inflation than people thought. The
minutes warned that the Fed may need to raise short-term interest rates further to keep the economy from
overheating. Higher interest rates raise costs for companies and individuals alike, which is bad for stocks.
The index of leading indicators, which foreshadows coming economic activity, was stronger than expected for
January, reinforcing concerns that the Fed will continue to feel the need to cool the economy.
Investors got bad news from retailing company Wal-Mart Stores, which posted strong quarterly profit but
forecast disappointing net in the coming year.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.33%, or 4.21 points, to 1283.03, up 2.8% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index, heavy with technology stocks, fell 0.85%, or 19.40 points, to 2262.96, up 2.6% in
2006.
Gold rose for a third consecutive day, up $2.10 to $553.90 an ounce, $18.60 short of the 25-year high hit
earlier this month.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced modestly in dollar terms, though Japanese stocks surged again, by nearly
3%. The Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.04%, or 0.1 point, to 243.35. Excluding U.S. stocks, the index
rose 0.36%, or 0.75 point, to 206.89.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.54 billion shares traded, 1,562 stocks rose and
1,758 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.19 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.567%. The 30-year bond was down 12/32 to yield 4.532%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 118.74 yen, up from 118.25, while the euro
fell against the dollar to $1.1912 from $1.1946.

Life After Greenspan
Life After Greenspan
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,329 words
22 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The real coming-out party for Ben Bernanke was on Capitol Hill, where he went last week to present the
Fed's semiannual report to Congress. Like his predecessor, he remained calm and pleasant under
questioning from legislators who, with only a few exceptions, have little understanding of monetary policy.
Unlike his predecessor, who preferred long and opaque answers, Mr. Bernanke replied in a brief and mostly
clear format. Regardless of the style, though, one reason Fed chairmen remain so genial when up on the Hill
is that these hearings are a release valve. As long as he's answering questions about the trade deficit, budget
deficit, China, tax cuts, education, the minimum wage and assorted other topics, he doesn't have to answer
questions about actual Fed policy.
For the most part, Mr. Bernanke kept his promise not to make specific recommendations about fiscal policy.
But he did have a little something for everyone. He supported free trade, went out of his way to plug school
vouchers, and said that the Bush tax cuts helped the economy recover from recession. He also said that
budget deficits are a "negative for the economy" and that a sustained trade deficit "lowers national wealth and
lowers our ability to consume in the future." One of my favorite exchanges was when Rep. Emanuel Cleaver
fretted that the Chinese might stop buying U.S. debt. The new Fed chair said, "China's not holding our debt
because they want to be nice to us" -- succinct and 100% correct. The Chinese hold our debt because it is
the safest and most liquid asset in the world.
Despite his straightforward approach, Mr. Bernanke did exactly what he needed to do. He did not overreach
at his first public appearance by attempting to argue for inflation targeting (a method of managing monetary
policy that keeps inflation in a low and stable range). He left Capitol Hill in one piece and made certain that
one leg of his four-legged stool was secure. Every Fed chairman sits on the stool, and must constantly work
on his relationships with Congress, the public (including Wall Street), the press and Federal Reserve
colleagues.
While the test with Congress often comes in the form of a verbal duel on live TV, the public test unfolds over
many years. As long as the economy is strong and near price stability, or the Fed can successfully blame
economic troubles on someone or something else, those relationships will be fine. Issues with the press and
internal Fed battles are much more complicated, as well as interrelated. For example, last week's public
attacks on Kevin Warsh -- a key White House staffer and one of President Bush's recent nominees for
Federal Reserve Board governor -- appear to be part of this nexus.
These attacks violated an unwritten rule that former Fed members do not publicly disagree with the Fed or its
chairman. They were orchestrated by a retired Fed governor who claimed that Mr. Warsh was not qualified to
be on the Fed as he was not trained by academics to be an economist. Instead, he is a lawyer with extensive
Wall Street experience and knowledge of the capital markets. Given that Mr. Bernanke worked with him in the
White House, and likely had veto power, we can assume he supported the nominee. So someone else inside
the Fed may have encouraged the attacks in order to send a warning shot about any moves toward inflation
targeting that might be made by the new chairman and his newest colleagues. Like any D.C. institution, the
Fed abhors change and accountability, and inflation targeting would force both.
But all of this should take a back seat to the current stance of monetary policy. On this front, Ben Bernanke
is being called Mr. Status Quo because his testimony sounded no new themes. But it's way too early to draw
any conclusions. The forecasting process that goes into the Fed's semiannual report to Congress starts many
months before the actual event. The board and the 12 regional banks all submit forecasts and thoughts on
policy, which in turn drive the testimony. It should never be taken as a snapshot of what the chairman is
actually thinking.
In fact, the timing of this testimony, so soon after a change of leadership, means that it was probably written
mostly by Fed staff. As a result, it was biased toward the Phillips Curve -- a view that low unemployment
causes inflation. In the end, the models used by the Fed staff are very simple. If there is spare capacity
(labor or capital) in the economy, inflation should remain low. But if labor markets become too tight or

--- Page 208 ---
Page 208 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
capacity utilization rates rise too high, then inflation worries increase. With first quarter real GDP growth
looking above 5%, the unemployment rate at 4.7% (already below the Fed's year-end 2006 forecast), the
"core" inflation rate above 2% (the top end of its comfort zone), and capacity utilization rates at a six-year
high, the warning sirens are sounding. A hawkish tone from the Fed is not surprising.
Unfortunately, this look into the Fed staff models is not very comforting. Economic growth and low
unemployment do not cause inflation, and following models such as these has caused the Fed to make
some huge mistakes. For example, back in June 1999, with the federal funds rate at 4.75%, the economy
was booming and the unemployment rate was 4.3%. The Fed ignored deflationary signals from falling
commodity prices and hiked rates because it feared inflation. By mid-2000, the federal funds rate was 6.5%.
Inflation never appeared, and the Fed's Phillips Curve disciples were blindsided by both deflation and a
recession. Once reality took hold, the Fed cut interest rates a record-breaking 11 times in 2001, and
eventually pushed them to 1% -- the lowest level in 50 years.
The aggressive nature of those rate cuts was a tacit admission that its models made a mistake. These cuts
ended deflation, while tax cuts lifted growth. Just six weeks after 9/11, the economy began a recovery that
continues to date. Now the Fed is worried about inflation again, and has lifted rates at an unprecedented 14
conecutive meetings. One more rate hike and the Fed funds rate will be back at 4.75% -- a perfect round trip
from 1999, as the chart here shows.
The Fed's insistence on using capacity measures and the Phillips Curve causes it to overshoot on both sides
-- tightening too much and then easing excessively. This creates unnecessary volatility in the economy. The
U.S. would have been better served if the Fed had held rates at 4.75% for the past six and a half years. The
big question facing the markets today is whether the Fed will go too far once again. Some think it has already
done so, but with gold above $550 per ounce, commodity prices elevated, nominal growth accelerating, and
inflation at the upper end of the Fed's comfort zone, it seems clear that monetary policy remains
accommodative.
Every now and then, market-based indicators and the Fed's Phillips Curve models align. Today is one of
those times. As a result, Mr. Bernanke's acceptance of the status quo was the appropriate move. The real
test will come when gold and commodity prices start to fall while the economy remains strong. At that time,
the new chairman will make his name. Stopping the tightening before economic damage is done would be a
huge and very beneficial development. But the only way to do that is to move the Fed away from its Phillips
Curve bias and toward a market-based method of inflation targeting. Believe it or not, Ben Bernanke is off to
a good start.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist with First Trust Portfolios L.P., in Lisle, Ill.

Alaska Reaches Gas-Pipeline Deal With Exxon, BP, ConocoPhillips
Leading the News
Alaska Reaches Gas-Pipeline Deal With Exxon, BP, ConocoPhillips
By Russell Gold
631 words
22 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After decades of negotiations, Alaska has reached an agreement with three major energy companies to build
a mammoth new pipeline to transport natural gas from the North Slope to the rest of North America.
The agreement sets in motion plans to undertake one of the largest civil-engineering projects ever -- a
pipeline nearly big enough in diameter for a young person to stand up in. It will stretch from a point several
hundred miles north of the Arctic Circle into Canada, and most of the gas would ultimately end up in Chicago
to be distributed from there. A 2001 estimate pegged the cost at $20 billion, but steel prices have risen
dramatically since then. The project will take well into next decade to complete.
Energy giants BP PLC, Exxon Mobil Corp. and ConocoPhillips have been sending oil from the prolific North
Slope to markets, but reinjecting natural gas -- extracted in the process -- back into the ground for lack of a
pipeline.
Pressure has mounted on the companies to finalize the deal in recent months, especially as gas prices
soared in December. Negotiations got a push from President Bush, who last month pressured Exxon to come
to terms with the state "in order to make sure there's more natural gas available," according to the Associated
Press. A state authority even filed an antitrust suit against BP and Exxon last year, claiming the oil companies
were conspiring to withhold gas from U.S. markets by not building a pipeline.
There are still significant hurdles. The deal has to be approved by the state legislature, where there could be
resistance if legislators believe that Gov. Frank Murkowski is giving away too much.
In addition, the energy companies have only committed to push ahead with a two- to three-year period of
designing the pipeline and getting the actual permits. Construction is still several years away.
There also are technical hurdles, including the fact that preliminary plans call for a 52-inch diameter steel pipe
that no steel mill currently produces.
The governor also proposed retooling the state's taxation of both oil and gas production. Gov. Murkowski is
expected to submit a proposal to the legislature today calling for a 20% tax on net production. If oil prices
remain above $25 a barrel, this will raise the effective tax rate for oil production, says Chuck Logsdon, a
spokesman for Gov. Murkowski.
In a statement, Exxon Mobil confirmed it had reached an agreement on the "major provisions of the gas fiscal
contract." But in language that reinforced the notion that negotiations were complex and would still take time,
Exxon also said the new tax regime would provide fiscal terms necessary to "advance the gas project to the
next phase."
This reticence was shared by William M. Walker, general counsel of the Alaska Gasline Port Authority, which
sued BP and Exxon last year and has been pushing to speed up construction of the pipeline. "I'd like to see
them say it's the beginning of a project, but all they say is it's the beginning of a process," he says.
There is an enormous amount of natural gas in the North Slope, a barren tundra where Alaska meets the
Arctic Ocean. The federal government has estimated there may be 100 trillion cubic feet of gas there, equal
to U.S. demand for five years.
Once the pipeline is finally built -- no earlier than 2012 to 2014 -- as much as 4.5 billion cubic feet of gas per
day could be fed into North American markets. By then, North America is also expected to be importing
significant amounts of natural gas from overseas in a super-cool liquefied form.

Enterprise: Entrepreneur Heads Far South to Launch Firm --- Leather-Goods Maker Finds Savings -- and Obstacles -- In Argentine Start-Up
Enterprise:
Small Business
Enterprise: Entrepreneur Heads Far South to Launch Firm --- Leather-Goods Maker Finds Savings --
and Obstacles -- In Argentine Start-Up
By Tara Siegel Bernard
994 words
21 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FRESH OUT OF college and living in a converted closet in Manhattan, Amanda Knauer knew she wouldn't
land her dream job -- combining fashion design with the adventure of international business -- at her age. So
she decided to fashion something for herself.
Buenos Aires, the so-called Paris of South America, beckoned. Ms. Knauer, now 25 years old, arrived in
2004 with one suitcase, $45,000 in the bank, and a conversational knowledge of Spanish. She explored the
city streets for inspiration, and it didn't take long to find her muse: Argentine leather.
Within months, she launched Qara Argentina, a luxury leather-accessories company that makes hand-crafted
calfskin wallets, messenger bags and other leather trimmings, targeting what she deems an underserved
market: 25- to 40-year-old urban men.
While starting a business on familiar terrain would have been easier, Ms. Knauer knew that with the
devaluation of the Argentine peso in 2002, her budget could be stretched much further there. But she also
knew there would be challenges.
For an entrepreneur establishing a business in a foreign country -- or even setting up an overseas unit -- it's
easy to make a mistake if you assume what would work well at home would work in another country.
"Everything reverts back to the cultural divide," Ms. Knauer says, "which is also what makes it interesting." In
Argentina, "you can't come here as an American and expect to do business as an American," she says. "You
have to observe and immerse yourself and study the Argentine way of doing business and more or less
mimic it."
Thousands of miles from family and friends, Ms. Knauer has had to quickly master an unfamiliar business
tango without a partner, as well as craft an authentic Argentine persona for her company so that she'd receive
the same treatment from suppliers as an indigenous operation would.
She was a quick study. She immediately contacted a lawyer, whom she found through friends back home,
and incorporated in both countries. Her lawyer also helped her find an "on-paper-only" Argentine partner. The
arrangement cost money, but having such a partner made it much easier to incorporate in Argentina.
There were other challenges as well. Ms. Knauer initially used well-known leather manufacturers, but they
had trouble translating her ideas into goods that met her standards. In one instance, she says, "they had run
out of the leather I had asked for so they gave me this messenger bag in metallic gold leather. It didn't occur
to them that I was expecting what I asked for. So I looked at the numbers and realized it made more sense to
open my own production facility."
By August, seven months after Qara's creation, she had rented a 1,200-square-foot space in the heart of
Buenos Aires for $365 a month, bought second-hand machines and found artisans through advertising in the
local paper.
But when she discovered that several leather tanneries she visited were inflating their prices to her, she says,
"I realized . . . what I needed to do was hire more Argentines to go as my cultural brokers. There were
linguistic subtleties that an American would just miss, as well as cultural issues," she explains.
She now has seven employees -- a manager, two assistant designers, two artisans and a part-time leather
cutter -- all of whom are Argentine except a fellow American expatriate who handles public relations. She
plans to hire two more artisans and buy more sewing machines to double weekly production to a total of
about 3,000 bags this year. Although marketing didn't begin in earnest until late last year, Ms. Knauer says
Qara should be profitable by the end of this year, the company's second.

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 213 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But hanging on to her artisans, who were used to working a single task on an assembly line, hasn't been
easy. "You have to retrain them," Ms. Knauer says, "They are used to working for quantity instead of quality
and I am asking for the opposite." She says she pays them more to make them stay. "If they stayed," she
adds, "they would realize what I required of them was more attractive than going back to a factory and
working in an assembly line. I think they take a lot more pride in their work now."
Qara's goods retail anywhere from $60 for a calfskin credit-card holder and $65 for an iBook laptop cover, to
$286 for a leather messenger bag and $650 for a weekend bag, Ms. Knauer says. While Qara's line is
currently available through its Web site, as well as a boutique, Foley + Corinna, in Manhattan, she needs
department-store shelves to bring her brand to the next level. On a recent visit to New York, she met with a
big department store as well as a few major fashion magazines and trade publications. "It's really important to
be on shelves," she says. "People really like touching and smelling leather, which they can't do online."
Qara's items are also available in Buenos Aires hotel boutiques, and she plans to open a store in the city's
fashion district, within the next six months.
While Ms. Knauer has other plans for expansion -- including a women's line -- she's close to exhausting her
initial investment. She's now seeking angel investors, which would allow her to establish a New York office,
as well as hire a sales executive. She'd also use the funds to buy more leather in bulk to lock in costs and
help ward off the effects of Argentine inflation.
